Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Definition, Conditions, and Types of Kafalah (Suretyship)
Opening rulings of Kafalah: definition, evidence, conditions, and classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define kafalah according to the Hanafi school, list its essential conditions, and classify its types.

Core Idea
Kafalah joins the surety's liability to the principal's liability for claim, not for the debt itself.

This lesson introduces the book of Kafalah (Suretyship) from Kanz al-Daqaiq. We will unpack the Hanafi legal definition, the conditions that must be met for a valid suretyship, and the detailed classification of types. Understanding the precise nature of kafalah as 'joining for claim' is crucial to avoid conflating it with a direct transfer of debt.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition
Understand the linguistic meaning (ضم) and the legal definition: joining a liability to a liability for the purpose of claim. Note the key nuance: the debt itself remains on the principal; the surety is only liable for the claim.
The Arabic 'ضم ذمة إلى ذمة' is key.
2
Identify the essential elements
Note the rukn (offer and acceptance), sabab (claim of the right-holder), and dalil (consensus and hadith).
3
List the conditions
The debt must be established and sound, the surety must be capable of donation (ahl al-tabarrayu'), the principal must be known, and a sick surety's kafalah is limited to one-third of his estate.
Ahl al-tabarrayu' means the surety can freely donate, thus minors and the insane are excluded.
4
Classify the types
Follow the hierarchical classification: kafalah bi-nafs and bi-mal; bi-mal subdivides into debts and specific items; specific items further into guaranteed, trust not obligatory to deliver, and trust obligatory to deliver. Understand which are valid and which are not.
Memorize the branches: bi-nafs, bi-mal (a) debts (b) a'yān (i) mardūn, (ii) amāna ghayr wājibat al-taslīm, (iii) amāna wājibat al-taslīm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة أحكام الكفالة هي ضم ذمة إلى ذمة فى المطالبة أي الكفالة شرعاً أي ذمة الأصيل كتاب الكفالة أي في بيان أحكامها كتاب الكفالة: [هي في اللغة الضم، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَكَفَلَهَا زَكَرِيَّا (آل عمران:٣٧) أي ضمها إلى نفسه. (عيني)] المناسبة بين الكتابين: أن البيع يوجب ديناً في الذمة، والكفالة شرعت وثيقة لاستيفاء الدين غالباً، ولها مناسبة خاصة بالصرف؛ لأنه ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في الواجب بالسبب أي العقد، والكفالة ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة، وهي في اللغة الضم. وفي المصباح: كفلت بالمال وبالنفس كفلا من باب قتل وكفولاً، فالاسم الكفالة بالفتح، ونقل سماعاً من العرب من باب تعب وقرب ويتعدى بنفسه وبواسطة الباء وعن يقال: كفلته وكفلت به وعنه. (مسكين، فتح) هي ضم ذمة الذمة وصف شرعي به الأهلية لوجوب ماله وعليه ويقال في ذمتي كذا، أي في ضماني، وهذا تعريف الكفالة في الشرع، وركنها الإيجاب والقبول بالألفاظ الآتية، وسببها: مطالبة من له الحق، ودليلها الإجماع، وسنده: قوله عليه: "الزعيم غارم. ومن محاسنها تفريج كرب الطالب والمطلوب، وشرطها: أن يكون الدين ثابتاً صحيحاً، وأهلها أن يكون الكفيل من أهل التبرع، حتى لا تصح ممن لا يملك التبرع، وكذا لا تصح من المريض إلا من الثلث وأن يكون المكفول عنه معلوماً. وأنواعها في الأصل نوعان: كفالة بالنفس، وكفالة بالمال والكفالة بالمال نوعان: كفالة بالديون، فتجوز مطلقاً إذا كانت صحيحة وكفالة بالأعيان وهي نوعان كفالة بأعيان مضمونة، فتجوز الكفالة بها كالمغصوب وبدل الخلع ونحوهما، وكفالة بأعيان هي أمانة غير واجبة التسليم كالودائع والمضاربات، فلا الكفالة بها أصلاً تصح لا بنفسها ولا بتسليمها، وكفالة بأعيان هي أمانة واجبة التسليم كالعارية والمستأجرة، أو بعين مضمونة بغيره كالمبيع، فإن الكفالة بها لا تصح وبتسليمها تصح. (فتح)
Chapter of Suretyship. Rulings of Suretyship. It is the joining of a liability to a liability for the purpose of claim; that is, suretyship legally, i.e., the liability of the principal. Chapter of Suretyship, i.e., on the exposition of its rulings. Chapter of Suretyship: [Linguistically it is joining, Allah said: 'And Zakariyya sponsored her' (Aal-e-Imran 3:37), meaning he took her under his care. (al-'Ayni)] The connection between the two chapters (sarf and kafalah): Sale creates a debt in the liability, and suretyship is legislated as a security for the collection of a debt mostly, and it has a special connection with sarf because it joins one liability to another in what is obligatory by cause i.e., the contract, while suretyship joins one liability to another in the claim. Linguistically it is joining. In al-Misbah: 'kafala with wealth and with self' as a verb, and the noun is al-kafalah with a fatha. It is transmitted from the Arabs. (Miskin, Fath) It is the joining of liability to liability, a legal description that qualifies a person for the obligation of what is for him and against him. It is said 'in my liability' meaning in my guarantee. This is the definition of suretyship in law. Its pillar is offer and acceptance with the following expressions. Its cause is the claim of the right-holder. Its evidence is consensus, and its authority is his saying (peace be upon him): 'The guarantor is liable.' Among its merits is relieving the distress of the claimant and the one claimed against. Its condition is that the debt be established and sound. Its capacity condition is that the surety be capable of voluntary donation, so it is not valid from one who does not have the capacity to donate, nor from a sick person except from one-third, and the principal must be known. Its types originally are two: suretyship for the person and suretyship for wealth. Suretyship for wealth is of two types: suretyship for debts, which is absolutely permissible if the debt is valid, and suretyship for specific items (ayn), which is of two types: suretyship for guaranteed specific items, which is permissible like for stolen property, substitute for khul', etc.; and suretyship for specific items that are trusts not obligatory to deliver like deposits and mudarabah, then suretyship for them is not valid at all, neither for the item itself nor for its delivery; and suretyship for specific items that are trusts obligatory to deliver like borrowed goods and rented goods, or for a specific item that is guaranteed by something else like the sold item, then suretyship for them is not valid but suretyship for their delivery is valid. (Fath)
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage opens the book of Kafalah (Suretyship) in Kanz al-Daqaiq. It begins by defining kafalah as 'joining a liability to a liability for the purpose of claim' — meaning the surety (kafil) joins his liability to the liability of the principal debtor (asil), but only for the purpose of being able to claim the debt from the surety, not that the debt itself becomes owed by the surety. The linguistic meaning of kafalah is 'joining,' supported by the Quranic verse about Zakariyya sponsoring Maryam. The connection between the previous chapter (sarf) and kafalah is that sale creates a debt, and suretyship is a security for that debt. The text then details the rulings: the pillar (rukn) is offer and acceptance; the cause (sabab) is the claim of the right-holder; the evidence (dalil) is consensus and the hadith 'The guarantor is liable'; conditions include that the debt be established and valid, the surety must be capable of donating (so minors or insane cannot be sureties), and the principal must be known. The classifications: kafalah is of two main types — bi-nafs (for the person, i.e., producing the debtor) and bi-mal (for wealth). Wealth suretyship further divides into suretyship for debts (which is valid for any valid debt) and suretyship for specific items (a'yān). The latter has subcategories: guaranteed items (like stolen property) — suretyship is valid; trusts not obligatory to deliver (like deposits) — suretyship is invalid; trusts obligatory to deliver (like borrowed goods) — suretyship for the item itself is invalid, but suretyship for its delivery is valid. This hierarchy ensures that only liabilities that are eventually due can be guaranteed.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafālah): Linguistically: joining. Legally: the joining of a surety's liability to the principal's liability for the purpose of claiming the debt (not for the debt itself).
الذمة (al-dhimmah): Legal liability; the capacity to owe and be owed. In kafalah, the surety's dhimmah is joined to the principal's dhimmah.
الأصيل (al-aṣīl): The principal debtor; the person on whose behalf the surety is given.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for a valid kafalah?
A) The debt must be established and sound.
B) The surety must be capable of donation.
C) The principal must be known.
D) The suretyship must be registered with a judge.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah in the Hanafi school is a joining of liability for claim, not for the debt, with specific conditions and a detailed classification of valid and invalid types.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
The Legal Nature of Kafalah: Claim vs. Debt
Inter-madhhab dispute on the legal nature of kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the nature of kafalah (joining in claim, not debt) and explain the core logical argument against the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Kafalah joins liability for the claim, not the debt — two debts for one thing is impossible.

This passage presents a classical dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i over the legal nature of suretyship (kafalah). The Hanafis argue that the surety only becomes subject to the creditor's claim, not a debtor himself, while al-Shafi'i holds that the surety becomes a co-debtor. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping subsequent rulings on forgiveness, purchase, and subrogation.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi view: kafalah joins the surety's liability to the principal only for the purpose of claim (mutālabah), not for the debt itself. The debt remains solely on the principal.
The text opens with 'ضم في المطالبة' — joining for claim.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and his proofs
Imam al-Shafi'i argues kafalah joins in the debt. His proofs: forgiveness of the debt to the surety discharges the principal; buying with the debt from the surety is valid; analogy to the usurper and the usurper of the usurper.
These proofs aim to show the surety must be a debtor.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis reply that two debts for one thing is logically impossible. The validity of forgiveness and purchase is explained by practical necessity (to validate a rational person's actions) without creating two debts. Claiming a debt from someone who does not owe is possible, like an agent.
The text states 'يستحيل أن يجب دينان' — impossibility of two debts.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi, if the surety pays, he can reclaim from the principal because the debt was not his; under Shafi'i, payment by the surety is his own debt payment, so he cannot reclaim unless by contract. Also, forgiving the principal also discharges the surety in Shafi'i, but in Hanafi forgiving the principal extinguishes the debt, so the claim also ends.
The difference affects inheritance, discharge, and subrogation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضم في المطالبة أي الكفالة . ذمة الكفيل إلى ذمة الأصيل في المطالبة دون الدين، فيكون الدين باقياً في ذمة الأصيل كما كان، وفي رواية عن أصحابنا أن موجبها وجوب الدين على الكفيل، وهذا غير صحيح؛ لأنه يستحيل أن يجب دينان، ولا يستوفي إلا أحدهما، وقال مالك: يبرأ الأصيل، نقله الشيخ أبو حفص، والمشهور عنه خلاف ذلك، وقال الشافعي: ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في الدين؛ لأن الطالب لو وهب الدين من الكفيل صح، وي به على الأصيل، ويرجع وهبة الدين من غير من عليه الدين لا تصح وكذا لو اشترى الطالب بالدين شيئا من الكفيل صح، والشراء بالدين لا يجوز إلا ممن عليه الدين؛ ولأنه مطالب بالدين والمطالبة به بلا وجوده محال وصيرورة الدين الواحد دينين لا مانع منه شرعاً بعد أن لا يستوفي إلا أحدهما كالغاصب وغاصب الغاصب، ولنا: أنه يستحيل أن يجب دينان، ولا يستوفي إلا أحدهما وأما وجوب المطالبة بدين على غيره فممكن كالوكيل بالشراء يطالب بالدين، وهو على الموكل وصحة الهبة والشراء ويجعل الدين الواحد في حكم دينين؛ لضرورة تصحيح تصرف العاقل، =
Joining for the claim, i.e., suretyship: the liability of the surety is joined to the liability of the principal in the claim, not in the debt, so the debt remains in the liability of the principal as before. And in a narration from our companions that its effect is obligation of the debt upon the surety, and this is incorrect, because it is impossible that two debts should be due while only one can be collected. Malik said: The principal is discharged, reported by Shaykh Abu Hafs, but the well-known from him is otherwise. And al-Shafi'i said: It is joining a liability to a liability in the debt, because if the claimant forgives the debt to the surety, it is valid and it (is valid) against the principal, and forgiving a debt from someone who does not owe it is invalid. Similarly, if the claimant buys something from the surety with the debt, it is valid, but buying with a debt is not permissible except from the one who owes the debt. And because he is claimant of the debt, and claiming it without its existence is impossible. And making one debt into two debts is not legally objectionable as long as only one is collected, like the case of a usurper and the usurper of a usurper. And we argue: It is impossible that two debts should be due while only one is collected. As for the obligation to claim a debt from someone else, it is possible, like an agent for purchase who claims a debt while it is on the principal. And the validity of forgiveness and purchase (in the Shafi'i argument) makes the one debt like two debts out of necessity to validate a rational person's transaction. =
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage delves into the legal nature of kafalah, presenting a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi position, established by Abu Hanifa and supported by his students, is that kafalah only joins the surety's liability to the principal's liability for the purpose of claim (mutālabah), meaning the creditor can demand payment from either the principal or the surety, but the debt itself remains solely on the principal. A minority Hanafi narration that suggests the debt transfers to the surety is rejected because it would create two debts for the same thing, which is logically impossible (only one can be collected). Imam Malik is reported to have said the principal is discharged (so the debt moves entirely to the surety), but the famous view from Malik is otherwise. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that kafalah joins liability in the debt itself, meaning both the principal and surety become equally indebted. His proof: if the creditor forgives the debt to the surety, it is valid and also discharges the principal — but forgiving a debt from someone who doesn't owe it is invalid. Similarly, if the creditor buys something from the surety using the debt as payment, it is valid, but debt-based purchase is only valid from the debtor. Thus, the surety must be a debtor. The Shafi'i also argues that making one debt into two is permissible as long as only one is collected, analogous to a usurper and the usurper of a usurper (both liable but only one pays). The Hanafi response: two debts for one thing is impossible. However, obligation to claim a debt from someone other than the debtor is possible, like an agent who can claim a debt on behalf of the principal. The Shafi'i proofs (forgiveness and purchase) are explained by necessity to validate transactions; they do not prove the surety is a true debtor. The Hanafi view is that the surety is only a guarantor of the claim, not of the debt itself.

Key Terms
المطالبة (al-muṭālabah): The claim or demand for payment; in kafalah, it refers to the creditor's right to demand payment from either the principal or the surety.
الدين (al-dayn): The debt; the financial obligation that the principal owes to the creditor.
الغاصب وغاصب الغاصب (al-ghāṣib wa ghāṣib al-ghāṣib): The usurper and the usurper of the usurper; an analogy in Hanafi law where both are liable for the same item, but only one ultimately pays.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school reject the view that kafalah makes the surety a co-debtor?
A) Because the hadith says the guarantor is not liable
B) Because it is impossible for one debt to be owed by two persons
C) Because the surety cannot be forced to pay
D) Because the debt disappears when the surety becomes liable

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that kafalah joins liability for the claim only, not the debt, based on the logical impossibility of two debts for one thing.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
The Legal Nature of Kafalah: Joining in Demand or in Debt?
Defines kafalah and presents Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of kafalah, distinguish it from the Shafi'i view, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Kafalah joins liability only for demand, never for the debt itself.

Kafalah is a key security contract in Islamic finance, but its precise legal nature is debated between madhhabs. The Hanafi school defines it as joining a guarantor's liability to the principal's liability solely for the purpose of demand, leaving the debt intact on the principal. This lesson unpacks the definition, conditions, and the rigorous argument with the Shafi'i school from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, showing how a single hadith generates two different legal structures.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the precise Hanafi definition: kafalah joins liability only in demand (ضم في المطالبة), not in the debt.
The Arabic phrase is 'ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة'.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Know that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that kafalah joins liability in the debt itself (ضم في الدين), making both parties debtors.
The Shafi'i view is supported by examples of gifting and buying with the debt.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Detail the Shafi'i arguments: valid gift of debt to guarantor, valid purchase from guarantor, and analogy to the usurper's case.
Understand why these are presented as evidence for debt transfer.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
See how the Hanafi school rebuts each point: impossibility of two debts for one claim, analogy to agency, and distinction by necessity.
The core rational argument is rational impossibility.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: under Hanafi, debt remains on principal; under Shafi'i, both are debtors but only one payment collected.
This affects recovery rights and transactions like gift and purchase.

Source Text

(عيني)] المناسبة بين الكتابين: أن البيع يوجب ديناً في الذمة، والكفالة شرعت وثيقة لاستيفاء الدين غالباً، ولها مناسبة خاصة بالصرف؛ لأنه ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في الواجب بالسبب أي العقد، والكفالة ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة، وهي في اللغة الضم. وفي المصباح: كفلت بالمال وبالنفس كفلا من باب قتل وكفولاً، فالاسم الكفالة بالفتح، ونقل سماعاً من العرب من باب تعب وقرب ويتعدى بنفسه وبواسطة الباء وعن يقال: كفلته وكفلت به وعنه. (فتح) ضم في المطالبة أي الكفالة. ذمة الكفيل إلى ذمة الأصيل في المطالبة دون الدين، فيكون الدين باقياً في ذمة الأصيل كما كان، وفي رواية عن أصحابنا أن موجبها وجوب الدين على الكفيل، وهذا غير صحيح؛ لأنه يستحيل أن يجب دينان، ولا يستوفي إلا أحدهما، وقال مالك: يبرأ الأصيل، نقله الشيخ أبو حفص، والمشهور عنه خلاف ذلك، وقال الشافعي: ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في الدين؛ لأن الطالب لو وهب الدين من الكفيل صح، وي به على الأصيل، ويرجع وهبة الدين من غير من عليه الدين لا تصح وكذا لو اشترى الطالب بالدين شيئا من الكفيل صح، والشراء بالدين لا يجوز إلا ممن عليه الدين؛ ولأنه مطالب بالدين والمطالبة به بلا وجوده محال وصيرورة الدين الواحد دينين لا مانع منه شرعاً بعد أن لا يستوفي إلا أحدهما كالغاصب وغاصب الغاصب، ولنا: أنه يستحيل أن يجب دينان، ولا يستوفي إلا أحدهما وأما وجوب المطالبة بدين على غيره فممكن كالوكيل بالشراء يطالب بالدين، وهو على الموكل وصحة الهبة والشراء ويجعل الدين الواحد في حكم دينين؛ لضرورة تصحيح تصرف العاقل، = ولا ضرورة قبل الهبة والشراء، وفي الغاصب وغاصب الغاصب لا يجب إلا دين واحد على أحدهما غير عين، ولهذا إذا اختار أحدهما ليس له أن يطالب آخر. عيني وفتح)
Book of Kafalah: It is joining a liability to a liability with respect to demand – i.e., kafalah legally: joining the liability of the principal. Book of Kafalah: i.e., regarding its rulings. Kafalah linguistically means joining. Allah said: 'And Zakariya took charge of her' (3:37) i.e., he joined her to himself. The connection between the two books: that sale necessitates a debt in the liability, and kafalah was legislated as a security for collecting the debt usually, and it has a special connection with sarf (currency exchange) because it joins liability to liability in the obligation due to the cause (the contract), and kafalah joins liability to liability in demand. In al-Misbah: kafaltu bi al-mal wa bi al-nafs kafalan from the pattern of qatala and kufulan, so the noun is kafalah with fatha, transmitted as heard from the Arabs from the pattern of ta'iba and qaruba, and it is transitive by itself and by means of ba and 'an: one says 'kafaltuhu' and 'kafaltu bihi' and 'kafaltu 'anhu'. (Miskin, Fath) It is joining of liability [i.e.,] a legal description by which occurs capacity for obligation of what is owed and incumbent. It is said 'fi dhimmati kadha' i.e., in my guarantee. This is the definition of kafalah in shari'a. Its pillar (rukn) is offer and acceptance with the following expressions. Its cause (sabab) is the demand of the right-holder. Its evidence (dalil) is ijma', and its support is his ﷺ saying 'The guarantor is liable' (al-za'im gharim). Among its virtues is relieving the distress of both the claimant and the sought [debtor]. Its condition: that the debt be established and valid. Its capacity condition: that the guarantor be from among those who are capable of voluntary donation, so it is not valid from one who cannot donate, and likewise it is not valid from a sick person except from one-third, and that the principal be known. Its types in origin are two: kafalah for the person (nafs) and kafalah for property. Kafalah for property is two types: kafalah for debts – it is valid absolutely if the debt is valid; and kafalah for specific things (a'yan) – two types: kafalah for guaranteed specific things – it is valid, like a usurped item, the consideration of khul', etc.; and kafalah for specific things that are trusts not obligated to be delivered – like deposits and mudaraba – it is not valid at all, neither for the thing itself nor for its delivery; and kafalah for specific things that are trusts obligated to be delivered – like borrowed items and hired items, or a specific thing guaranteed by another, like the sold item – then kafalah for it is not valid, but for its delivery it is valid. (Fath) 'Joining' with respect to demand: i.e., kafalah joins the guarantor's liability to the principal's liability in demand, not in the debt, so the debt remains in the principal's liability as it was. There is a riwaya from our companions that its requirement is the obligation of the debt upon the guarantor, but this is incorrect because it is impossible that two debts should be owed while only one is collected. Malik said the principal is released; transmitted by Shaykh Abu Hafs, but the well-known [position] from him is otherwise. Al-Shafi'i said: joining of liability to liability in the debt, because if the claimant gifts the debt from the guarantor it is valid, and it [the gift] reverts upon the principal, and gifting a debt from someone other than the one on whom it is owed is not valid; likewise, if the claimant buys something from the guarantor with the debt, it is valid, and buying with a debt is only valid from the one on whom it is owed; and because he [the claimant] demands the debt, and demanding it without its existence is impossible, and making one debt into two debts is not prohibited legally as long as only one is collected, like the usurper and the usurper of the usurper. Our position: it is impossible that two debts be owed while only one is collected. As for obligation of demanding a debt from someone else, it is possible, like the agent for purchase who demands the debt while it is upon the principal. And the validity of gift and purchase that make the single debt in the ruling of two debts is due to the necessity of validating the transaction of a sane person. There is no such necessity before the gift and purchase. And in the case of the usurper and the usurper of the usurper, only one debt is owed upon one of them not upon a specific thing, therefore if he chooses one he may not demand the other. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Kafalah. It first states the legal definition: kafalah is the joining of one liability to another liability with respect to the right of demand, not with respect to the debt itself. The author then explains the linguistic meaning and cites the Quranic verse to illustrate. He then lists the rukn (pillar: offer and acceptance), sabab (cause: demand of the creditor), dalil (evidence: ijma' and the Prophet's saying 'al-za'im gharim'), and shart (conditions: the debt must be established and valid, the guarantor must be capable of donation, the principal must be known). The text then categorizes kafalah into two primary types: for the person and for property, with further subdivisions for property. The central legal issue here is the nature of kafalah: does it transfer the debt itself to the guarantor, or merely add a second party for demand while the debt remains on the original debtor? The author reports a weak riwaya from Hanafi sources that the debt becomes obligatory on the guarantor, and dismisses it as incorrect because two debts cannot be owed for the same single claim. He then notes Imam Malik's reported view (though the well-known Maliki position is otherwise) and presents Imam al-Shafi'i's argument that kafalah joins the guarantor's liability to the principal's in the debt itself, supporting it with examples of gift and purchase. The Hanafi response is that the impossibility of two debts on one claim is rationally untenable; rather, kafalah only creates an additional recourse for demand, as seen in agency (wakala) where an agent may demand a debt that remains on the principal. The Shafi'i examples are distinguished by necessity: before any gift or purchase, the debt remains single. The analogy to the usurper and his usurper is likewise rejected because there only one debt is owed, not two with separate objects.

Key Terms
ضم ذمة إلى ذمة (damm dhimmah ilā dhimmah): Joining one legal liability to another; in kafalah, it means adding the guarantor's liability to the principal's for the purpose of demand.
ضم في المطالبة (damm fī al-muṭālabah): Joining in demand; the Hanafi view that kafalah only creates a second party for demand, not a second debtor.
ضم في الدين (damm fī al-dayn): Joining in the debt; the Shafi'i view that the guarantor also becomes a debtor.
الزعيم غارم (al-za‘īm ghārim): The guarantor is liable; a hadith used as evidence for kafalah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of kafalah on the debt?
A) The debt is transferred entirely to the guarantor.
B) The debt remains on the principal, but the guarantor is liable for demand.
C) The debt becomes shared equally between principal and guarantor.
D) The principal is released from the debt.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah under Hanafi law only adds a party for demand; the debt remains on the principal, and this has direct legal consequences for gift, purchase, and recovery.

Terminology of Kafalah: Parties and Guaranteed Item
Technical terms definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four technical terms in Hanafi kafalah and match each term to the correct party.

Core Idea
In kafalah, each party has a distinct technical name: makful lahu, damin, makful bihi, and asil.

This brief passage introduces the standard technical terms used in Hanafi fiqh for the contract of kafalah (suretyship). Understanding these terms is essential for studying rulings related to guarantee, as they precisely identify who is who. We will learn the four terms and their exact definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four roles
Recognize the creditor (right-holder), guarantor, guaranteed item/person, and original debtor (the one sought).
These roles are universal in guarantee contracts.
2
Match roles to Arabic terms
According to the passage: creditor = al-makful lahu, guarantor = al-damin, guaranteed item = al-makful bihi, original debtor = al-asil (also al-makful 'anhu).
Memorize these pairings precisely.
3
Note the synonym for the original debtor
The original debtor is called both asil and makful 'anhu. Both terms are used interchangeably.
The passage explicitly mentions both.
4
Apply to a simple scenario
Imagine A wants to borrow from B, C guarantees A's debt. Identify: B = makful lahu, C = damin, the debt = makful bihi, A = asil.
Practice with different scenarios to reinforce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة ومما ينبغي أن يعلم أن في اصطلاح الفقهاء يسمى صاحب الحق مكفولاً له، والضامن يسمى المضمون يسمى مكفولاً به، والمطلوب يسمى أصيلاً ومكفولاً عنه عنه. (محشي)
Appendix: It should be known that in the terminology of the jurists, the right-holder is called 'mukful lahu', the guarantor is called 'dammin', the thing guaranteed is called 'makful bihi', and the sought person is called 'asil' and 'makful 'anhu'.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This brief note clarifies the technical terms used in kafalah. The creditor (the one to whom the right is owed) is called al-makful lahu. The guarantor (the person who provides the guarantee) is called al-damin. The item or person guaranteed is al-makful bihi. The original debtor, the one on whose behalf the guarantee is made, is called al-asil or al-makful 'anhu. This terminology is standard in Hanafi fiqh and helps avoid confusion when discussing different parties.

Key Terms
مكفول له (makful lahu): The creditor or person in whose favor the kafalah is made.
ضامن (damin): The guarantor who undertakes the kafalah.
مكفول به (makful bihi): The item or person that is guaranteed.
أصيل (asil): The original debtor on whose behalf the kafalah is given.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Makful lahu
B) Damin
C) Asil
D) Makful bihi

Key Takeaway
The four key terms in kafalah are makful lahu (creditor), damin (guarantor), makful bihi (guaranteed item), and asil (original debtor).

Kafalah bi al-Nafs: Validity and the Shafi'i Dispute
Ruling on kafalah bi al-nafs and inter-madhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on kafalah bi al-nafs, explain the Shafi'i objection and the Hanafi response, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Kafalah for the person is valid: the general hadith and practical possibility overcome the objection of lack of authority.

This lesson examines the validity of guaranteeing a person's presence (kafalah bi al-nafs). The Hanafi school affirms its validity, countering the Shafi'i objection that a free person cannot be compelled. The key evidence is the general Prophetic hadith and the practical possibility of delivery through compliance or state aid. We also explore how multiple guarantors or multiple guaranteed persons work.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the core ruling: kafalah bi al-nafs is valid in the Hanafi school. Note the hukm level (mubah) and that it is a permissible contract.
The text begins directly with 'Wa tasihhu bi al-nafs'.
2
Present the Shafi'i objection
Recall Imam al-Shafi'i's argument: no authority over a free person, especially without his command, so delivery is impossible.
The text mentions 'la qud'rata lahu 'ala taslimihi'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi evidence
Cite the hadith 'al-za'im gharim' (the guarantor is liable) as a general proof covering both property and person. Also note the support from Companions and Ahmad ibn Hanbal.
Hadith is narrated by al-Daraqutni; 'ghurm' means harm in general.
4
Explain the possibility of delivery
Delivery is possible in two ways: if the principal ordered the guarantee, he will likely comply; if not, the guarantor can seek help from the ruler's agents.
This refutes the Shafi'i objection of impossibility.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: Hanafi allows kafalah bi al-nafs, Shafi'i (in one view) does not. However, the text notes that al-Shafi'i's well-known position agrees with Hanafi. The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
The text says 'mash'huru madhhabihi ka-madhhabina'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتصح بالنفس وقال الشافعي: لا تجوز الكفالة بالنفس؛ لأنه لا قدرة له على تسليمه؛ إذ لا ولاية له عليه، لاسيما إذا تكفل بغير أمره، ومشهور مذهبه كمذهبنا ولنا قوله عليه: "الزعيم غارم"، رواه الدار قطني، والزعيم: الكفيل، والمراد بالغرم الضرر من غير فصل بالكفالة بالمال والكفالة بالنفس، وبه قال أحمد، وهو قول عمر وعثمان وابن مسعود وابن عمر وضمرة بن عمرو الأسلمي وجرير بن عبد ا وأبي بن كعب وعمران بن الحصين والأشعث بن قيس . [ رمز الحقائق ٩٤/٢] والتسليم ممكن، أما في صورة الأمر؛ فلأن الظاهر أنه ينقاد إليه، وأما في صورة غير الأمر، فلأنه يمكنه إحضاره بالاستعانة بأعوان الحاكم. (فتح) وإن تعددت [ أي الكفالة، بأن أخذ منه كفيلا ثم كفيلا، وكذا يجوز إذا تعدد النفوس المكفول بها أيضاً. (عيني)] يحتمل ثلاثة أوجه: الأول: أن يجعل الضمير للكفالة بأن أخذ منه كفيلا ثم كفيلاً آخر، فهما كفيلان، ويطالب صاحب الحق أيهما شاء، والثاني: أن يكون المراد من تعدد الكفالة ما إذا كان للكفيل كفيل، والثالث: أن الضمير إلى النفس، بأن يكفل واحد نفوساً، كما يجوز الكفالة بالديون الكثيرة. (عيني، فتح) "كفلت بنفسه": [متعلق بقوله : وتصح؛ لأنه حقيقة في معناها (عيني)] شروع في ذكر الألفاظ المستعملة في الكفالة، وهي في ذلك على قسمين قسم يعبر به عن البدن حقيقة كالنفس والجسد والبدن، وقسم: يعبر عنه عرفاً كقوله تكفلت بوجهه أو برأسه؛ فإن كلا منهما مخصوص بعضو خاص، فلا يشمل الكل حقيقة، لكنه يشمله بطريق العرف حتى لو قال كفلت بجسده أو بروحه أو برأسه أو رقبته ونحوهما تصح الكفالة بالنفس، وقوله: " وبجزء شائع" أي تصح الكفالة لو قال: تكفلت بثلثه أو ربعه ونحوهما؛ لأن النفس الواحدة في حق الكفالة لا تتجزأ، فكان ذكر بعضها شائعاً كذكر كلها، ولو أضاف الكفيل الجزء إلى نفسه كـ كفل لك نصفي أو ثلثي، قال في ب فلاناً تصح يعود "السراج": إنه لا يجوز ، لكن لو قيل: إن ذكر بعض ما لا يتجزأ كذكر كله، لم يفترق الحال. (عيني، فتح) وبـ"ضمنته": [ أي وتصح أيضاً بقوله: "ضمنته"، لأنه تصريح بموجب الكفالة (عيني)] أي إذا قال: ضمنتِ الكفالة بالنفس؛ لأن هذا اللفظ تصريح بموجب الكفالة، وقوله: "وب علي" أي تصح الكفالة أيضاً بقوله: "علي"؛ لأنه صيغة التزام، ومن هنا أفتى قاري الهداية بأنه لو قال: التزمت بما علي فلان كان كفالة، وقوله: "وإلي" أي وتصح أيضاً بقوله: "إلي"؛ لأنه بمعنى "علي"، ومنه قوله عليه: "من ترك مالاً فلورثته ومن ترك كلاً أو عيالاً فإلي"، والكل هو اليتيم والعيال من بعوله أي ينفق عليه. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
And [kafalah] is valid for the person. Al-Shafi'i said: Kafalah for the person is not permissible because he has no power to deliver the person, as he has no authority over him, especially when he guarantees without his command. But the well-known [position] of his school is like our school. Our evidence is his ﷺ saying 'al-za'im gharim', reported by al-Daraqutni. Al-za'im is the guarantor, and by 'ghurm' is meant harm without distinction between kafalah for property and kafalah for the person. This was held by Ahmad, and it is the saying of 'Umar, 'Uthman, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn 'Umar, Damra ibn 'Amr al-Aslami, Jarir ibn 'Abdillah, Ubayy ibn Ka'b, 'Imran ibn al-Husayn, and al-Ash'ath ibn Qays. Delivery [of the person] is possible: in the case of a command, because it appears that he will comply; in the case of without command, because he can bring him by seeking help from the ruler's assistants. (Fath) And if [kafalah] is multiple, i.e., taking a guarantor and then another guarantor, and likewise it is valid if the persons guaranteed are multiple. ('Ayni) It admits three interpretations: first, that the pronoun refers to kafalah, i.e., taking a guarantor and then another, so they are two guarantors, and the right-holder may demand from whichever he wishes; second, that what is meant by multiplicity is when there is a guarantor of a guarantor; third, that the pronoun refers to the person, i.e., one person guarantees multiple persons, just as kafalah for multiple debts is valid. ('Ayni, Fath) 'Kafaltu bi-nafsihi' is connected to the saying 'And it is valid' because it is a literal expression for [the person]. ('Ayni) This begins the mention of the expressions used in kafalah. They are of two types: one that expresses the body literally, like nafs, jasad, and badan; and one that expresses it customarily, like saying 'I guarantee his face' or 'his head', for each is specific to a particular limb, so it does not include the whole literally, but includes it by custom, such that if he says 'I guarantee his body' or 'his spirit' or 'his head' or 'his neck' and the like, kafalah for the person is valid. And his saying 'or a common part' means kafalah is valid if he says 'I guarantee a third of him' or 'a quarter' etc., because the single person, with respect to kafalah, is indivisible, so mentioning a common part is like mentioning the whole. If the guarantor adds the part to himself, like 'I guarantee you half of [my] self' or 'a third of [my] self', al-Siraj said it is not valid, but if it is said that mentioning part of what is indivisible is like mentioning the whole, then the case does not differ. ('Ayni, Fath) And by 'damanthu': and it is valid also by his saying 'damanthu', because it is explicit about the requisite of kafalah. ('Ayni) i.e., if he says 'damanthu' for kafalah for the person, because this expression is explicit about the requisite of kafalah. And 'or 'alayya' means kafalah is also valid by his saying 'alayya' because it is a form of commitment, and from this Qari al-Hidaya gave a fatwa that if he says 'I commit myself to what is owed by so-and-so', it is kafalah. And 'or ilayya' means it is also valid by his saying 'ilayya' because it is in the meaning of 'alayya', and from it is his ﷺ saying: 'Whoever leaves wealth, it is for his heirs, and whoever leaves a dependant or family, then to me (ilayya).' Al-kall is the orphan, and al-'iyal are those under his care, i.e., he spends on them. ('Ayni, Fath, 'Inaya)
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage begins by affirming that kafalah for the person (kafalah bi al-nafs) is valid in the Hanafi school. It then records the objection of Imam al-Shafi'i, who argues that a person cannot guarantee the delivery of a free person because he has no authority over him, especially if he guarantees without his permission. However, the text notes that the well-known position of the Shafi'i school actually agrees with the Hanafi school. The Hanafi evidence is the Prophetic hadith 'al-za'im gharim' (the guarantor is liable), which is general and covers both property and person. This is supported by numerous Companions and Ahmad ibn Hanbal. The text then argues that delivery is possible: if the principal authorized the guarantee, he will likely comply; if not, the guarantor can enlist the help of the ruler's agents. The passage then discusses multiplicity of kafalah. If there are multiple guarantors for the same person, the creditor may demand from any of them. Alternatively, multiplicity can mean there is a guarantor for the guarantor, or that one person guarantees multiple persons. The last part details the valid expressions for kafalah. Literal expressions for the body (nafs, jasad, badan) are valid. Customary expressions (face, head, etc.) are also valid because custom includes the whole. A common fractional part (e.g., one-third) is valid because the person is indivisible in kafalah, so mentioning part is like mentioning the whole. There is disagreement when the guarantor adds the part to himself (e.g., 'I guarantee half of myself'): al-Siraj deems it invalid, but the text notes that if indivisibility is the rationale, it should be valid. The word 'damanthu' (I guaranteed him) is valid as it explicitly indicates the obligation. The term ''alayya' (upon me) is a form of commitment, and 'ilayya' (to me) is synonymous with ''alayya', as in the hadith about the Prophet taking responsibility for dependants.

Key Terms
كفالة بالنفس (kafalah bi al-nafs): A guarantee that a specific person will be produced or will appear.
زعيم غارم (za'im gharim): The guarantor is liable; the hadith used as evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Ijma' of the Companions
B) The hadith 'al-za'im gharim'
C) Qiyas on kafalah bi al-mal
D) The practice of the Medinans

Key Takeaway
Kafalah bi al-nafs is valid in the Hanafi school based on the general hadith and the practical possibility of delivery.

Valid Expressions (Sighah) for Kafalah bi al-Nafs
Details of valid expressions for kafalah bi al-nafs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid and invalid expressions for kafalah bi al-nafs, including literal, customary, and fractional terms, and explain why mentioning a common part is sufficient.

Core Idea
Any expression that indicates guarantee of the person—literal, customary, or fractional—is valid because the person is indivisible in kafalah.

After establishing that kafalah bi al-nafs is valid, the text details the correct verbal formulas (sighah). Literal words for the body (nafs, jasad, badan) are valid. Customary expressions (face, head) are valid because custom extends the meaning. Even mentioning a common fractional part (e.g., one-third) is valid because the person is indivisible in the context of kafalah. Other expressions like 'damanthu', ''alayya', and 'ilayya' also form valid contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify literal expressions for the body
Recognize that words like 'nafs', 'jasad', and 'badan' literally mean the whole person and are valid for kafalah.
The text says 'haqiqatan' (literally).
2
Analyze customary expressions
Terms like 'face' or 'head' do not refer to the whole literally but customarily include the entire person. This is accepted because custom ('urf) gives them the meaning of the whole.
Example: 'kafaltu bi-wajhihi' is valid by custom.
3
Understand fractional expressions
Saying 'I guarantee a third of him' is valid because the person is indivisible in the context of kafalah, so mentioning a part is like mentioning the whole. This is the Hanafi qiyas: the whole is in each part.
Exception: if the guarantor adds the part to himself (e.g., 'half of myself'), al-Siraj considers it invalid, but the text implies it should be valid.
4
Recognize other valid formulaic words
Words like 'damanthu', ''alayya', and 'ilayya' are also valid. 'Damanthu' explicitly indicates the obligation. ''Alayya' is a commitment. 'Ilayya' has the same meaning, as seen in the hadith 'man tarka kullan aw 'iyalan fa-ilayya'.
These are explicit (sarih) expressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتصح بالنفس وقال الشافعي: لا تجوز الكفالة بالنفس؛ لأنه لا قدرة له على تسليمه؛ إذ لا ولاية له عليه، لاسيما إذا تكفل بغير أمره، ومشهور مذهبه كمذهبنا ولنا قوله عليه: "الزعيم غارم"، رواه الدار قطني، والزعيم: الكفيل، والمراد بالغرم الضرر من غير فصل بالكفالة بالمال والكفالة بالنفس، وبه قال أحمد، وهو قول عمر وعثمان وابن مسعود وابن عمر وضمرة بن عمرو الأسلمي وجرير بن عبد ا وأبي بن كعب وعمران بن الحصين والأشعث بن قيس . [ رمز الحقائق ٩٤/٢] والتسليم ممكن، أما في صورة الأمر؛ فلأن الظاهر أنه ينقاد إليه، وأما في صورة غير الأمر، فلأنه يمكنه إحضاره بالاستعانة بأعوان الحاكم. (فتح) وإن تعددت [ أي الكفالة، بأن أخذ منه كفيلا ثم كفيلا، وكذا يجوز إذا تعدد النفوس المكفول بها أيضاً. (عيني)] يحتمل ثلاثة أوجه: الأول: أن يجعل الضمير للكفالة بأن أخذ منه كفيلا ثم كفيلاً آخر، فهما كفيلان، ويطالب صاحب الحق أيهما شاء، والثاني: أن يكون المراد من تعدد الكفالة ما إذا كان للكفيل كفيل، والثالث: أن الضمير إلى النفس، بأن يكفل واحد نفوساً، كما يجوز الكفالة بالديون الكثيرة. (عيني، فتح) "كفلت بنفسه": [متعلق بقوله : وتصح؛ لأنه حقيقة في معناها (عيني)] شروع في ذكر الألفاظ المستعملة في الكفالة، وهي في ذلك على قسمين قسم يعبر به عن البدن حقيقة كالنفس والجسد والبدن، وقسم: يعبر عنه عرفاً كقوله تكفلت بوجهه أو برأسه؛ فإن كلا منهما مخصوص بعضو خاص، فلا يشمل الكل حقيقة، لكنه يشمله بطريق العرف حتى لو قال كفلت بجسده أو بروحه أو برأسه أو رقبته ونحوهما تصح الكفالة بالنفس، وقوله: " وبجزء شائع" أي تصح الكفالة لو قال: تكفلت بثلثه أو ربعه ونحوهما؛ لأن النفس الواحدة في حق الكفالة لا تتجزأ، فكان ذكر بعضها شائعاً كذكر كلها، ولو أضاف الكفيل الجزء إلى نفسه كـ كفل لك نصفي أو ثلثي، قال في ب فلاناً تصح يعود "السراج": إنه لا يجوز ، لكن لو قيل: إن ذكر بعض ما لا يتجزأ كذكر كله، لم يفترق الحال. (عيني، فتح) وبـ"ضمنته": [ أي وتصح أيضاً بقوله: "ضمنته"، لأنه تصريح بموجب الكفالة (عيني)] أي إذا قال: ضمنتِ الكفالة بالنفس؛ لأن هذا اللفظ تصريح بموجب الكفالة، وقوله: "وب علي" أي تصح الكفالة أيضاً بقوله: "علي"؛ لأنه صيغة التزام، ومن هنا أفتى قاري الهداية بأنه لو قال: التزمت بما علي فلان كان كفالة، وقوله: "وإلي" أي وتصح أيضاً بقوله: "إلي"؛ لأنه بمعنى "علي"، ومنه قوله عليه: "من ترك مالاً فلورثته ومن ترك كلاً أو عيالاً فإلي"، والكل هو اليتيم والعيال من بعوله أي ينفق عليه. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
And [kafalah] is valid for the person. Al-Shafi'i said: Kafalah for the person is not permissible because he has no power to deliver the person, as he has no authority over him, especially when he guarantees without his command. But the well-known [position] of his school is like our school. Our evidence is his ﷺ saying 'al-za'im gharim', reported by al-Daraqutni. Al-za'im is the guarantor, and by 'ghurm' is meant harm without distinction between kafalah for property and kafalah for the person. This was held by Ahmad, and it is the saying of 'Umar, 'Uthman, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn 'Umar, Damra ibn 'Amr al-Aslami, Jarir ibn 'Abdillah, Ubayy ibn Ka'b, 'Imran ibn al-Husayn, and al-Ash'ath ibn Qays. Delivery [of the person] is possible: in the case of a command, because it appears that he will comply; in the case of without command, because he can bring him by seeking help from the ruler's assistants. (Fath) And if [kafalah] is multiple, i.e., taking a guarantor and then another guarantor, and likewise it is valid if the persons guaranteed are multiple. ('Ayni) It admits three interpretations: first, that the pronoun refers to kafalah, i.e., taking a guarantor and then another, so they are two guarantors, and the right-holder may demand from whichever he wishes; second, that what is meant by multiplicity is when there is a guarantor of a guarantor; third, that the pronoun refers to the person, i.e., one person guarantees multiple persons, just as kafalah for multiple debts is valid. ('Ayni, Fath) 'Kafaltu bi-nafsihi' is connected to the saying 'And it is valid' because it is a literal expression for [the person]. ('Ayni) This begins the mention of the expressions used in kafalah. They are of two types: one that expresses the body literally, like nafs, jasad, and badan; and one that expresses it customarily, like saying 'I guarantee his face' or 'his head', for each is specific to a particular limb, so it does not include the whole literally, but includes it by custom, such that if he says 'I guarantee his body' or 'his spirit' or 'his head' or 'his neck' and the like, kafalah for the person is valid. And his saying 'or a common part' means kafalah is valid if he says 'I guarantee a third of him' or 'a quarter' etc., because the single person, with respect to kafalah, is indivisible, so mentioning a common part is like mentioning the whole. If the guarantor adds the part to himself, like 'I guarantee you half of [my] self' or 'a third of [my] self', al-Siraj said it is not valid, but if it is said that mentioning part of what is indivisible is like mentioning the whole, then the case does not differ. ('Ayni, Fath) And by 'damanthu': and it is valid also by his saying 'damanthu', because it is explicit about the requisite of kafalah. ('Ayni) i.e., if he says 'damanthu' for kafalah for the person, because this expression is explicit about the requisite of kafalah. And 'or 'alayya' means kafalah is also valid by his saying 'alayya' because it is a form of commitment, and from this Qari al-Hidaya gave a fatwa that if he says 'I commit myself to what is owed by so-and-so', it is kafalah. And 'or ilayya' means it is also valid by his saying 'ilayya' because it is in the meaning of 'alayya', and from it is his ﷺ saying: 'Whoever leaves wealth, it is for his heirs, and whoever leaves a dependant or family, then to me (ilayya).' Al-kall is the orphan, and al-'iyal are those under his care, i.e., he spends on them. ('Ayni, Fath, 'Inaya)
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage begins by affirming that kafalah for the person (kafalah bi al-nafs) is valid in the Hanafi school. It then records the objection of Imam al-Shafi'i, who argues that a person cannot guarantee the delivery of a free person because he has no authority over him, especially if he guarantees without his permission. However, the text notes that the well-known position of the Shafi'i school actually agrees with the Hanafi school. The Hanafi evidence is the Prophetic hadith 'al-za'im gharim' (the guarantor is liable), which is general and covers both property and person. This is supported by numerous Companions and Ahmad ibn Hanbal. The text then argues that delivery is possible: if the principal authorized the guarantee, he will likely comply; if not, the guarantor can enlist the help of the ruler's agents. The passage then discusses multiplicity of kafalah. If there are multiple guarantors for the same person, the creditor may demand from any of them. Alternatively, multiplicity can mean there is a guarantor for the guarantor, or that one person guarantees multiple persons. The last part details the valid expressions for kafalah. Literal expressions for the body (nafs, jasad, badan) are valid. Customary expressions (face, head, etc.) are also valid because custom includes the whole. A common fractional part (e.g., one-third) is valid because the person is indivisible in kafalah, so mentioning part is like mentioning the whole. There is disagreement when the guarantor adds the part to himself (e.g., 'I guarantee half of myself'): al-Siraj deems it invalid, but the text notes that if indivisibility is the rationale, it should be valid. The word 'damanthu' (I guaranteed him) is valid as it explicitly indicates the obligation. The term ''alayya' (upon me) is a form of commitment, and 'ilayya' (to me) is synonymous with ''alayya', as in the hadith about the Prophet taking responsibility for dependants.

Key Terms
جزء شائع (juz' shā'i'): A fractional, undefined part of an indivisible thing; here, mentioning a fraction of a person in kafalah is valid.
صيغة الكفالة (sighah al-kafalah): Verbal formula used to conclude a kafalah contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following expressions is NOT valid for kafalah bi al-nafs according to the Hanafi school?
A) Kafaltu bi-nafsihi
B) Kafaltu bi-wajhihi
C) Kafaltu bi-thuluthihi
D) Kafaltu bi-nisfi nafsi (half of myself)

Key Takeaway
Valid expressions for kafalah bi al-nafs include literal, customary, and fractional terms; the person's indivisibility justifies fractional expressions.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
8 min
Validity of Kafalah bi al-Nafs and Its Proof
Ruling on kafalah bi al-nafs with proof and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kafalah bi al-nafs, cite the primary proof (hadith al-za'imu gharim), and explain the refutation of the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
Kafalah bi al-nafs is valid because the surety can deliver the person through legal means, supported by the hadith.

This passage tackles a fundamental question: can a person be a surety for another person's physical presence? The Hanafi school affirms it, while Imam al-Shafi'i initially denied it. We will examine the proof from hadith and the practical reasoning that resolves the apparent difficulty of delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the key issue
The passage begins by stating that kafalah for the person is valid (sahih). Identify the core point: this is kafalah bi al-nafs, where the surety guarantees the physical presence of the principal (asil).
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil)
Locate the hadith cited: 'al-za'imu gharim' (the surety is held liable). Understand that the term 'za'im' means kafil (surety) and 'gharim' means liable or damaged. The hadith is general and covers both property and person.
The hadith is reported by al-Daraqutni and is considered ahad, but its meaning is clear.
3
Examine the Shafi'i objection and the Hanafi response
Imam al-Shafi'i argued that one cannot deliver a person without authority, so kafalah bi al-nafs is invalid. The Hanafi response: delivery is possible either by the principal's obedience to a command or through the assistance of the judge's officers.
Note that al-Shafi'i later adopted the Hanafi position.
4
Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The disagreement matters in practice: Hanafis enforce the contract, while early Shafi'is would not. The surety can be compelled to produce the person.

(عيني، فتح، عناية)
And it is valid for the person, even if multiple, by saying 'I am surety for his person' and by whatever expresses the body, and by a part (i.e., an undivided share of the surety), and by 'I guarantee him' and by 'upon me' and 'to me'.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the ruling that kafalah (suretyship) for the person is valid according to the Hanafi school. The author then lists the expressions that effect a valid kafalah: saying 'kafaltu bi nafsihi' (I am surety for his person) or any expression that denotes the body, or a part of the person (like one-third or one-fourth), or saying 'dhamintuhu' (I guarantee him), or 'alayya' (upon me), or 'ilayya' (to me). The commentary explains that these expressions are either literal (like 'nafs', 'jasad', 'badan') or customary (like 'wajh', 'ra's', 'ragaba'). The phrase 'wa in ta'addadat' refers to multiple sureties or multiple persons covered. The passage then presents the proof for the validity of kafalah bi al-nafs: the hadith 'al-za'imu gharim' (the surety is held liable) transmitted by al-Daraqutni, and the consensus of the Companions. Imam al-Shafi'i is reported to have invalidated kafalah bi al-nafs on the grounds of inability to deliver the person, but the Hanafi response is that delivery is possible, either by command (if the person obeys) or through the assistance of the judge's officers. The passage also covers the validity of multiple sureties and the interpretation of clauses like 'bi juz'in shayi'' (undivided share). The author emphasizes that mentioning a part of an indivisible whole (the person) is like mentioning the whole. The words 'dhamintuhu', 'alayya', and 'ilayya' are accepted because they explicitly indicate assumption of liability or commitment, as supported by prophetic practice.

Key Terms
كفالة بالنفس (kafalah bi al-nafs): A contract by which a person becomes surety for the physical presence of another person (the 'asil) before the claimant (makful lahu).
الزعيم غارم (al-za'imu gharim): A prophetic hadith meaning 'the surety is held liable/damaged' – used as proof for the validity of kafalah.
جزء شائع (juz'un sha'i'): An undivided share (e.g., one-third, one-fourth) of the person; mentioning such a part is considered as mentioning the whole because the person is indivisible in kafalah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 'al-kafilu za'imun'
C) 'man kafala nafsan fa-huwa damin'
D) 'al-damanu 'ala al-nafsi la yajuzu'

Key Takeaway
Kafalah bi al-nafs is valid in the Hanafi school because the surety can deliver the person through legal means, supported by the general hadith 'al-za'imu gharim'.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
10 min
Valid Wordings for Kafalah bi al-Nafs
Continues listing valid wordings and rules for delivery and imprisonment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid from invalid expressions for kafalah bi al-nafs and apply the rules when a delivery time is stipulated.

Core Idea
To create a valid kafalah bi al-nafs, use 'za'im' or 'qabil'; phrases guaranteeing only acquaintance are insufficient.

In kafalah bi al-nafs (suretyship of the person), the wording used is critical. The Hanafi school specifies which expressions create a binding obligation to produce the principal and which do not. This lesson examines the valid expressions 'za'im' and 'qabil', supported by the Quran, and the invalid expression 'damin li ma'rifa', along with the rules when the surety stipulates a delivery time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the valid expressions
The text states that saying 'ana za'imun bihi' (I am a surety for him) and 'qabilun bihi' (I am a surety for him) create a valid kafalah. Note the Quranic proof: 'And I am with him a za'im' (Surah Yusuf 72).
'Za'im' and 'qabil' are synonymous with 'kafil'.
2
Recognize the invalid expression
The phrase 'ana damin li ma'rifatihi' (I guarantee his acquaintance) does NOT constitute kafalah. It only guarantees knowledge, not the person's presence.
Abu Yusuf disagreed based on custom, but the majority (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad) hold it invalid.
3
Understand the rule when a delivery time is stipulated
If the surety agrees to deliver the principal at a specific time, he must bring him at that time when the claimant requests. If he fails, the judge may imprison him, but not immediately—similar to debt cases.
If the principal is absent, the surety is given the time of the journey; if unknown location, the surety is not liable.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf considered 'damin li ma'rifa' valid by custom. The majority rejected this because the literal meaning is clear and custom cannot override it. The praktical difference: under Abu Yusuf, the surety must produce the person; under the majority, he only provides information.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و"أنا زعيم "به وقبيل به، لا بـ"أنا ضامن لمعرفته"، فإن شرط تسليمه في وقت أي الكفيل في الكفالة بعينه، أحضره فيه إن طلبه، فإن أحضره، وإلا حبسه الحاكم، فإن غاب أمهله مدة ذلك الوقت المكفول له وإن لم يحضره أي الكفيل ذهابه وإيابه، فإن مضت ولم يُحضره حبسه ، وإن غاب ولم يعلم مكانه، لا يطالب به، وأنا زعيم به [ أي وتصح أيضاً بقوله: أنا زعيم به أي كفيل بفلان. (عيني)] أي تصح الكفالة بقوله: "أنا, بفلان"؛ لأن الزعامة هي الكفالة، والزعيم يسمى كفيلاً، قال تعالى: ﴿وَلِمَنْ جَاءَ بِهِ حِمْلُ بَعِيرٍ وَأَنَا بِهِ زَعِيمٌ (يوسف: ۷۲) أي كفيل، وقوله: "وقبيل" به" أي تصح أيضاً بقوله: قبيل بفلان؛ لأن القبيل هو الكفيل، يقال: قبل قبالة بفتحها في الماضي وكسرها في المضارع، ولهذا سمي الصك قبالة؛ لأنه يحفظ الحق، فمعناه القابل للضمان. (عيني، فتح) ضامن لمعرفته أي لا يصير كفيلا بقوله : أنا ضامن لمعرفة فلان وقال أبو يوسف: يصير ضامناً للعرف؛ لأنهم يريدون به الكفالة ولهما أنه التزم معرفته دون المطالبة، فصار كأنه قال: أوقفك عليه، وكذا لو قال: أنا ضامن؛ لأن أدلك عليه أو لأن أدل على منزله، لا يكون كفالة ولو قال: "أنا ضامن لتعريفه أو على تعريفه"، ففيه اختلاف المشايخ، والوجه اللزوم ولا يصير كفيلا بـ"أنا ضامن لك"؛ لأنه لم يبين المضمون، أهو نفس أو مال رمز الحقائق: ٩٥/٢] أحضره أي لو شرط الكفيل تسليم المكفول عنه في وقت معين أحضر المكفول عنه في الوقت المذكور إن طلب المكفول له في ذلك الوقت أو بعده؛ لأنه التزم الإحضار بالشرط في الكفالة، فيجب عليه الوفاء به كالدين المؤجل إذا طلبه صاحبه عند حلول الأجل أو بعده. (عيني، فتح) فإن أحضره: أي فإن أحضر الكفيل المكفول عنه عند طلب المكفول له فهو المراد (عيني) وإلا حبسه جزاء الشرط محذوف أي فإن أحضره فيها، وإلا أي وإن لم يحضره حبس الحاكم الكفيل، قال الزيلعي: ينبغي أن يفصل كما فصل في الحبس بالدين؛ فإنه فصل هكذا: إذا ثبت الحق بإقراره لا يعجل بحبسه ويأمره بدفع ما عليه؛ لأن الحبس دليل المماطلة ولم تظهره وإن ثبت بالبينة حبسه كما ثبت؛ لظهور مطله بالإنكار، فكذا هنا ينبغي أن يفصل؛ لأن الحبس لامتناع إيفاء ما وجب عليه، ولكن لا يحبسه أول مرة؛ لاحتمال أنه ما عرف لماذا يدعى فيمهله، حتى يظهر له مطله؛ لأن الحبس جزاء الظلم، وهو ليس بظالم قبل المماطلة. [رمز الحقائق: ٩٥/٢] أمهله: هذا إذا لم يمتنع من السفر، وإن أبى حبسه من غير إمهال، وهو مقيد بما إذا لم يكن بالطريق عذر؛ لأن الامتناع عن التسليم مع الإمكان يظهر ظلمه، إلا عند مالك لو سافر بعيداً لا من شأنه ذلك السفر يسقط طلبه، وفي كل موضع قلنا: إنه يؤمر بالذهاب إليه للطالب أن يستوثق الكفيل بكفيل آخر، حتى لا يغيب فيضيع حقه، وفي "الإيضاح": إن ظهر عجزه لا يحبس، إلا أنه لا يحال بينه وبين الكفيل فيلازمه ويطالبه، ولا يحول بينه وبين أشغاله. [رمز الحقائق: ٩٥/٢] وإن غاب إلخ أي لو غاب المكفول عنه ولم يعلم مكانه إما بتصديق الطالب أو ببينة أقامها الكفيل أنه غاب غيبة لا تدرى لا يطالب الكفيل بالمكفول عنه بالإجماع؛ لأنه عاجز وقد صدقه الطالب عليه، وإن اختلفا، فقال
And 'I am a surety (za'im) for him' and 'a surety (qabil) for him', not by 'I am a guarantor for his acquaintance'. Then if he (the surety) stipulates delivery at a specific time, he must bring him at that time if the claimant requests. If he brings him [fine], otherwise the judge imprisons him. If the [principal] is absent, he gives him time [equal to] the period of that [journey]. If it passes without bringing him, he imprisons him. If [the principal] is absent and his location unknown, he [the surety] is not held responsible. And 'ana za'imun bihi': [the commentary states] it is valid because za'amah is kafalah. And 'qabilun bihi' is also valid because qabil is the surety. He is not a surety by saying 'ana damin li ma'rifatihi'...
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of wording for kafalah bi al-nafs. The expressions 'ana za'imun bihi' (I am a surety for him) and 'qabilun bihi' (I am a surety for him) are valid, as supported by the Quranic verse 'And I am with him a za'im' (Surah Yusuf 72). The word 'qabil' is derived from 'qabala' meaning to accept responsibility. The phrase 'ana damin li ma'rifatihi' (I guarantee his acquaintance) does not create a kafalah, because it only guarantees knowledge, not the person. Abu Yusuf differed, considering it valid by custom. The text then explains the rules when a delivery time is stipulated: the surety must bring the principal at that time if the claimant requests; if he fails, the judge may imprison him. However, the judge should not imprison immediately; there should be a distinction similar to debt cases: if the surety acknowledges the obligation, he is given time; if established by evidence, he may be imprisoned for denial. If the principal is absent, the surety is given time equal to the journey. If the principal's whereabouts are unknown, the surety is not held accountable. The passage also mentions that the surety may be required to provide another surety to protect the claimant's right.

Key Terms
زعيم (za'im): A surety (kafil); used in the Quranic verse and valid expression for kafalah bi al-nafs.
قبيل (qabil): A surety; derived from 'qabala' meaning to accept responsibility; valid expression.
ضامن لمعرفته (damin li ma'rifatihi): 'I guarantee his acquaintance' – an expression that does not constitute kafalah bi al-nafs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, what is the ruling if someone says 'I guarantee his acquaintance' (ana damin li ma'rifatihi)?
A) It is a valid kafalah bi al-nafs.
B) It is a valid kafalah bi al-mal.
C) It is not a kafalah at all.
D) It is valid only if the principal agrees.

Key Takeaway
For a valid kafalah bi al-nafs, use 'za'im' or 'qabil'; phrases like 'damin li ma'rifa' are insufficient, and the surety's obligation to deliver is subject to nuanced procedural rules.

Kafalah — Surety

Valid and Invalid Formulas for Personal Suretyship
Identifies valid and invalid formulas for kafalah bi'l-nafs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which verbal formulas create binding kafalah bi'l-nafs and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding vague expressions.

Core Idea
Only explicit terms like 'za'eem' and 'qabil' create binding personal suretyship; vague promises of acquaintance do not.

The validity of kafalah bi'l-nafs (personal suretyship) hinges on the wording used. The Hanafi school scrutinizes the phrasing to ensure the surety clearly undertakes to produce the person. This lesson examines three common expressions—two valid and one invalid—and explores a notable disagreement within the school over the ambiguous phrase 'ana damin li-ma'rifatihi'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three expressions
From the matn, extract the three verbal formulas discussed: 'ana za'eem bihi', 'ana qabil bihi', and 'ana damin li-ma'rifatihi'. Note which are presented as valid and which as invalid.
The matn explicitly states that first two are valid, the third is not.
2
Understand the legal effect
For the valid phrases, the surety becomes obligated to produce the person (kafalah bi'l-nafs). For the invalid phrase, no such obligation arises.
The invalid phrase merely promises acquaintance, not delivery.
3
Locate the Quranic evidence
The commentator cites Surah Yusuf:72 where 'za'eem' is used. Recognize that this verse provides the primary textual basis for accepting 'za'eem' as a valid term.
Dalil: the word 'za'eem' in the Quran means surety/guarantor.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Compare the positions: Abu Yusuf holds that 'damin li-ma'rifatihi' creates kafalah due to custom; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold it does not, because the literal meaning only promises acquaintance.
The thamarah al-khilaf: difference in liability.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The majority of later Hanafi scholars (commentators like 'Ayni and Fath) adopt the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Conclude that the invalid phrase does not produce kafalah.
This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة
أحكام الكفالة
و"أنا زعيم به" وقبيل به، لا بـ"أنا ضامن لمعرفته" [هذا المتن]. ثم قال الشارح: [أي وتصح أيضاً بقوله: أنا زعيم به أي كفيل بفلان. (عيني)] أي تصح الكفالة بقوله: "أنا زعيم بفلان"؛ لأن الزعامة هي الكفالة، والزعيم يسمى كفيلاً، قال تعالى: ﴿وَلِمَنْ جَاءَ بِهِ حِمْلُ بَعِيرٍ وَأَنَا بِهِ زَعِيمٌ (يوسف: 72) أي كفيل، وقوله: "وقبيل به" أي تصح أيضاً بقوله: قبيل بفلان؛ لأن القبيل هو الكفيل، يقال: قبل قبالة بفتحها في الماضي وكسرها في المضارع، ولهذا سمي الصك قبالة؛ لأنه يحفظ الحق، فمعناه القابل للضمان. (عيني، فتح) ضامن لمعرفته أي لا يصير كفيلا بقوله : أنا ضامن لمعرفة فلان وقال أبو يوسف: يصير ضامناً للعرف؛ لأنهم يريدون به الكفالة ولهما أنه التزم معرفته دون المطالبة، فصار كأنه قال: أوقفك عليه، وكذا لو قال: أنا ضامن؛ لأن أدلك عليه أو لأن أدل على منزله، لا يكون كفالة ولو قال: "أنا ضامن لتعريفه أو على تعريفه"، ففيه اختلاف المشايخ، والوجه اللزوم ولا يصير كفيلا بـ"أنا ضامن لك"؛ لأنه لم يبين المضمون، أهو نفس أو مال. رمز الحقائق: 95/2
Book of Suretyship. Rulings of Suretyship. And 'I am a surety for him' and 'qabil for him', not by 'I am the guarantor of his acquaintance'. [The commentator said: 'And it is also valid by his saying: I am a surety for him, i.e., a guarantor for so-and-so. (Ayni)] That is, the suretyship is valid by his saying: 'I am a surety for so-and-so', because suretyship is kafalah, and the surety is called a guarantor. Allah Almighty said: 'And for whoever brings it, a camel load, and I am for it a guarantor' (Yusuf: 72) meaning a guarantor. And his saying: 'and qabil for him' means it is also valid by his saying: qabil for so-and-so, because qabil is the guarantor. It is said: he accepted (qabila) a guarantee (qabala) with a fatha in the past and kasra in the present, and for this reason the document is called qabala because it preserves the right, so its meaning is the one who accepts the guarantee. (Ayni, Fath) 'Guarantor of his acquaintance' means he does not become a guarantor by his saying: I am the guarantor of knowing so-and-so. And Abu Yusuf said: he becomes a guarantor by custom ('urf), because they intend by it suretyship. But for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) [the response is that] he undertook his acquaintance without the claim, so it is as if he said: I will point him out to you. Similarly, if he said: I am a guarantor because I will guide you to him or because I will guide you to his house, it is not kafalah. And if he said: 'I am a guarantor for his identification or on his identification', there is disagreement among the scholars, and the preferable view is that it is binding. And he does not become a guarantor by saying 'I am a guarantor for you' because he did not specify the subject matter, whether it is a person or property. Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 95/2
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, with commentary from 'Ayni and Fath al-Qadir, discusses the valid and invalid verbal formulas for kafalah bi'l-nafs (personal suretyship). The matn states that the phrases 'ana za'eem bihi' and 'qabil bihi' are valid, while 'ana damin li-ma'rifatihi' (I am a guarantor of his acquaintance) is not. The commentator explains that 'za'eem' means kafil (surety), supported by the Quranic verse where the Prophet Yusuf (as) says 'I am a guarantor for it'. Similarly, 'qabil' is synonymous with kafil, and the related term 'qabala' refers to the guarantee document. Regarding the invalid phrase, the author clarifies that saying 'I am a guarantor of knowing so-and-so' does not make one a surety for his person. Abu Yusuf argued that it does, based on custom ('urf), since people use it to mean suretyship. However, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) respond that the phrase only promises acquaintance, not subjection to claim, so it is like saying 'I will point him out to you.' The same applies to other oblique phrases like 'I guarantee to guide you to him' etc. The text also notes that saying 'I am a guarantor for you' is insufficient because the subject (whether a person or property) is unspecified. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that only clear terms like 'za'eem' and 'qabil' create binding kafalah bi'l-nafs.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where one person (the surety) guarantees the presence or debt of another.
زعيم (za'eem): A synonym for kafil (surety) used in Quran, forming a valid expression for kafalah.
قبيل (qabil): Another synonym for kafil, derived from 'qabala' meaning to accept guarantee.
ضامن (damin): Literally 'guarantor'; when used without specification (e.g., 'damin li-ma'rifatihi'), it does not create kafalah bi'l-nafs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, which of the following is a valid formulation for kafalah bi'l-nafs?
A) 'أنا ضامن لمعرفته'
B) 'أنا زعيم به'
C) 'أنا ضامن لك'
D) 'أنا كفيل بماله'

Key Takeaway
Only clear terms like 'za'eem' and 'qabil' create binding personal suretyship; ambiguous phrases like 'damin li-ma'rifatihi' do not, per the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Discharge by Delivery in a Litigable Place
Initial ruling on discharge by delivery in a city

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a surety (kafil) is discharged by delivering the principal (makful ‘anhu) and identify why a city (misr) must have a qadi and sultan.

Core Idea
Delivering the principal where the creditor can sue discharges the surety; a proper city needs a judge and ruler.

This lesson from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the key rule for discharging a surety in kafalah bil nafs. The surety must deliver the principal in a place where the creditor can initiate litigation—namely, a city with a qadi and sultan. If the contract stipulated a specific court, delivery must occur there.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is delivery of the principal by the surety. The ruling is that the surety is discharged (bari’).
2
Determine the condition for discharge
Delivery must be in a place where the creditor is able to litigate with the principal.
3
Recognize the example given
A city (misr) with a qadi and sultan is such a place.
Misr requires both a judge and a ruler.
4
Note the stipulation exception
If the creditor stipulated delivery in a specific court, the surety must deliver there.
5
Understand the Hanafi definition of misr
A valid misr must have both a qadi (judge) and sultan (ruler); otherwise, it is not a valid place for discharge.
This prevents abuse and ensures enforceability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن سلّمه بحيثُ يقدر المكفول له أن يخاصمه كمصر برئ، ولو شرط تسليمه في المكفول عنه مجلس القاضي يسلّمه تمه، .
If he delivers him in such a way that the creditor is able to litigate with him, as in a city, he is discharged. And if he stipulated delivery in the court, he delivers him there.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This is a matn (core text) ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq on kafalah bil nafs. It states two conditions under which the surety (kafil) is discharged: (1) by delivering the principal (makful 'anhu) to the creditor (makful lahu) in a place where the creditor can institute legal proceedings against him—such as a city (misr) with a qadi and sultan; (2) if the creditor stipulated that delivery take place in a specific court (majlis al-qadi), the surety must deliver him there. The commentary (not included in this short extract) clarifies that a misr requires both a qadi and a sultan; otherwise, it is not a valid place for discharge.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where the kafil guarantees the appearance of the principal (kafalah bil nafs) or payment (kafalah bil mal).
مصر (misr): A city with a qadi and sultan, enabling legal jurisdiction.
مجلس القاضي (majlis al-qadi): The court of the judge; the specific place where legal proceedings are conducted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what condition must a city meet for the kafil to be discharged by delivering the principal there?
A) It must be the city where the contract was made
B) It must have a qadi and sultan
C) It must be the creditor's hometown
D) It must be within a day's journey

Key Takeaway
A surety is discharged by delivering the principal in a city with a qadi and sultan (or the stipulated court); otherwise, liability remains.
