Specifying the Amount in Conditional Suretyship: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on amount specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding specification of amount in conditional suretyship and state the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
A conditional suretyship is valid even without prior amount specification; later specification retroactively validates it.

This lesson examines a nuanced disagreement within the Hanafi school between Imam Muhammad and the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) about whether the debt amount must be specified at the moment of a conditional suretyship. The dispute revolves around the validity of a guarantee where the claimant initially makes a vague claim but later clarifies the amount. Understanding this difference sharpens your appreciation of Hanafi legal reasoning and the role of custom and definiteness in contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Picture the hypothetical: a man claims an unspecified right against another, and a third party offers to be surety for the debtor's person, promising a specific amount (a hundred dinars) if he fails to produce him. The amount of the original debt is not specified at the time of the suretyship.
2
State Muhammad's position and his proof
Imam Muhammad argues that the suretyship is invalid because the claim was not specified, making it a risky absolute guarantee. Since the claim is invalid, the demand for the person is not obligatory, so the suretyship for the person fails, and consequently the property guarantee fails.
3
State the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf and their proof
The two shaykhs hold that the suretyship is valid. Their proof: the property is mentioned in a definite manner (ma'rif), referring to the actual debt, and custom allows vague claims. Later specification attaches retroactively to the original claim, validating both suretyships.
4
Analyze the underlying reasoning
The key difference lies in whether definiteness (ta'rif) and custom can cure the initial vagueness. Muhammad focuses on the risk and the invalidity of the underlying claim, while the majority give weight to the definite article and prevailing custom.
5
Identify the relied-upon position (mufta bihi)
The view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is the operative position for fatwa, as affirmed by later authorities like Al-Fath and al-'Inayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مائدة دينار: [سواء بين صفتها بأنها جيدة أوردية أو لم يبين. (معدن)] ذكر المائة ليس قيداً، ولا فرق بين أن يبين المائة أو لم يبينها بأن قال: لي عليك حق، ولم يدع مالاً مقدراً، فقال رجل آخر دعه فأنا كفيل بنفسه، فإن لم أواف لك به غداً فعلي مائة دينار .
فقال له رجل: إن لم يواف به غداً فعليه المائة، فلم يواف به غداً فعليه المائة، أي آخر لم يأت بالمدعى عليه أي على الرجل أي مائة دينار أي فعلى الرجل
ولا يجبر على الكفالة بالنفس في حدّ وقود، ولا يحبس فيهما ، حتى يشهد شاهدان المدعى عليه أي إعطاء الكفيل أي المطلوب أي في الحد والقود مستوران فادعى المدعي ،وأثبتها لزم الكفيل المائة التي بينها المدعي إما ببينة أو بإقرار المدعى عليه وقال محمد: إن لم يبينها حتى تكفل، ثم ادعى بعد ذلك، لم يلتفت إليه في دعواه؛ لأنه علق مالاً مطلقاً بخطر؛ ولأنه لم تصح الدعوى من غير بيان، فلم يجب إحضار النفس، فإذا لم يجب لا تصح الكفالة بالنفس، فلا تصح الكفالة بالمال؛ لابتنائها عليها، بخلاف ما إذا بين، لهما: أن المال ذكر معرفاً، فينصرف إلى ما عليه، والعادة جرت بالإجمال في الدعاوي، فيصح الدعوى على اعتبار البيان فإذا بين التحق البيان بأصل الدعوى، فتبين صحة الكفالة الأولى، فتترتب عليها الثانية. [رمز الحقائق: ۹۸/۲] (فتح) فلم يواف: أي لم يأت الرجل بالمدعى عليه في غد حتى مضى الغد. (عيني)
Table of dinar: whether he describes its quality as good or bad or does not describe. Mentioning the hundred is not a condition, and there is no difference between specifying the hundred or not specifying it, such as if he said: 'I have a right upon you' and did not claim a specific amount, and another man said: 'Leave him, I am a surety for his person; if I do not bring him to you tomorrow, I owe a hundred dinars.' Then a man said to him: 'If he does not bring him tomorrow, he owes the hundred.' And he did not bring him tomorrow, so he owes the hundred. i.e. another did not bring the defendant, i.e. upon the man, i.e. a hundred dinars, i.e. upon the man. And he is not compelled to give a surety for the person in a hadd or qisas, and he is not imprisoned in them until two witnesses, i.e. the defendant, i.e. giving the surety, i.e. the demanded, i.e. in the hadd and qisas, are upright. Then the claimant claimed and proved it. The surety becomes liable for the hundred that the claimant specified, either by evidence or by the confession of the defendant. And Muhammad said: If he did not specify it until he became surety, then claimed afterwards, his claim is not considered, because he attached an absolute property to a risk, and because the claim is not valid without specification, so bringing the person is not obligatory. And if it is not obligatory, the suretyship for the person is not valid, so the suretyship for property is not valid, because it is based on it. Contrary to if he specified. The two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) said: The property is mentioned as definite (ma'rif), so it refers to what is upon him, and custom has run with vagueness in claims, so the claim is valid on the basis of specification; when he specifies, the specification attaches to the original claim, so the validity of the first suretyship becomes clear, and the second is established upon it.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) regarding whether the amount of debt must be specified at the time of the conditional suretyship. The scenario: a man says 'I have a right upon you' without specifying the amount, then another person offers to be a surety for his person, and further promises to pay a hundred dinars if he fails to produce him. According to Muhammad, if the amount was not specified when the suretyship was undertaken, the claimant's later specification is not accepted, because the guarantee was absolute and risky, and the claim without specification is invalid, thus the suretyship for the person and consequently for property are invalid. However, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf argue that the property was mentioned in a definite manner (معرفاً) referring to the actual debt, and it is customary to make vague claims; when the claimant later specifies, the specification is connected back to the original claim, validating both suretyships. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms
المعرف (al-mu'arraf): A noun with the definite article (al-), indicating something known or specific

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is valid if specified later
B) It is invalid because the claim was not based on a specified amount
C) It is valid because of custom
D) It is valid only if the debtor confesses

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a conditional suretyship is valid even without prior specification of the debt amount; later specification retroactively validates it.

Compulsion of Suretyship in Hudud and Qisas
Ruling with proof on compulsion of suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on compelling suretyship in hudud and qisas, identify the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf, and explain the dalil for the prohibition.

Core Idea
No compulsion for suretyship in hudud or qisas; the surety cannot guarantee corporal punishment.

This passage addresses whether a defendant can be compelled to provide a surety (kafil) for his presence in cases involving fixed punishments (hudud) or retributive justice (qisas). Abu Hanifa holds that compulsion is not allowed, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad make exceptions for hadd of qadhf and qisas. The discussion also covers when imprisonment is permissible based on suspicion with two upright witnesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Locate the statement 'ولا يجبر على الكفالة بالنفس في حدّ وقود' and understand that according to Abu Hanifa, one cannot be forced to provide a surety for the person in any hadd or qisas case.
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) allow compulsion in hadd of qadhf (slander) and qisas, arguing these involve rights of the servant (haqq al-'abd). Contrast with Abu Hanifa's general prohibition.
3
Analyze the dalil
The proof cited is the statement 'لا كفالة في حد' reported from Shuraih or marfu'. Understand that this negation is taken as a general prohibition of suretyship in all hadd penalties, without distinction between rights of Allah and rights of servants.
4
Examine the meaning of compulsion
The text explains that for the Sahibayn, 'compulsion' does not mean imprisonment but rather ordering the defendant to accompany the claimant and not leave his presence.
5
Understand imprisonment with witnesses
The passage states that a person can be imprisoned on suspicion if two upright (mastur) witnesses testify to his bad character, based on the practice of Ali (RA). This is different from property claims where complete proof is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجبر على الكفالة بالنفس في حدّ وقود، ولا يحبس فيهما ، حتى يشهد شاهدان المدعى عليه أي إعطاء الكفيل أي المطلوب أي في الحد والقود مستوران فادعى المدعي ،وأثبتها لزم الكفيل المائة التي بينها المدعي إما ببينة أو بإقرار المدعى عليه وقال محمد: إن لم يبينها حتى تكفل، ثم ادعى بعد ذلك، لم يلتفت إليه في دعواه؛ لأنه علق مالاً مطلقاً بخطر؛ ولأنه لم تصح الدعوى من غير بيان، فلم يجب إحضار النفس، فإذا لم يجب لا تصح الكفالة بالنفس، فلا تصح الكفالة بالمال؛ لابتنائها عليها، بخلاف ما إذا بين، لهما: أن المال ذكر معرفاً، فينصرف إلى ما عليه، والعادة جرت بالإجمال في الدعاوي، فيصح الدعوى على اعتبار البيان فإذا بين التحق البيان بأصل الدعوى، فتبين صحة الكفالة الأولى، فتترتب عليها الثانية. [رمز الحقائق: ۹۸/۲] (فتح) فلم يواف: أي لم يأت الرجل بالمدعى عليه في غد حتى مضى الغد. (عيني) ولا يجبر إلخ: أي لا يجبر على إعطاء الكفيل عند أبي حنيفة، سواء كان الحد حد قذف أو لا؛ لأن الكفالة بنفسهما لا تجوز إجماعاً؛ إذ لا يمكن الكفيل ويلحق حد السرقة بحد القذف على المذهبين وقيد بالقصاص؛ لأنه في القتل والجراحة خطأ يجبر على الكفيل إجماعاً؛ إذ الموجب هو المال، وقالا يجبر في حد القذف والقصاص؛ لأن حد القذف مشتمل على حق العبد والقصاص يغلب فيه حق العبد، وفي غيرهما من الحدود لا يجبر ولو سمحت به نفسه من غير طلب يجوز بالاتفاق وله قوله عليها: "لا" كفالة في حد" من غير فصل بينما هو حق العبد وبينما هو حق الله تعالى، وقيل: هذا من كلام شريح، وقيل: روي مرفوعاً. وليس تفسير الجبر عندهما ههنا أن يجبر بالحبس وغيره من العقوبة، ولكن يأمره بالملازمة، ويدور معه حيث دار، وإذا أراد دخول داره استأذنه، فإن أذن له دخل معه، وإن لم يأذن له منعه من الدخول، وأجلسه في باب الدار كي لا يغيب بالخروج من موضع آخر. [رم,ز الحقائق: ۹۸/۲۲] وإنما يجبر في حد القذف والقصاص عندهما؛ لأن الدعوى شرط فيهما، والمدعي يحتاج أن يجمع بين شهوده ومطلوبه فربما يخفى المطلوب نفسه فستوثق الكفيل، بخلاف غيره من الحدود؛ لأن الدعوى ليس بشرط فيها، وإنما قيد بالحد؛ لأن في التعزير يجوز للقاضي أن يطلب منه كفيلا. (فتح، عناية) ولا يحبس فيهما إلخ لأن الحبس ههنا لتهمة الفساد وشهادة المستورين تصلح لإثبات الحكم، فتصلح لإثبات التهمة، وخبر الواحد حجة في الديانات والمعاملات فيثبت بشهادة الواحد العدل التهمة، وإن لم يثبت به أصل الحق، والحبس بتهمة الفساد مشروع؛ لأنه علي حبس رجلاً بتهمة، بخلاف الحبس في دعوى المال حيث لا يحبس فيه ما لم يثبت أصل الحق عليه؛ لأنه نهاية العقوبة فلا يثبت إلا بحجة تامة، وفي "نوادر" أبي "يوسف" رواية ابن سماعة في الذي يجمع الخمر ويشربه ويترك الصلاة أحبسه وأؤدبه ثم أخرجه ومن يتهم بالقتل والسرقة وضرر الناس، فإني أحبسه، وأخلده في السجن إلى أن يتوب؛ لأن شر هذا على الناس، وشر الأول على نفسه. رمز الحقائق: ۹۸/۲]
And he is not compelled to give a surety for the person in a hadd or qisas, and he is not imprisoned in them until two witnesses, i.e. the defendant, i.e. giving the surety, i.e. the demanded, i.e. in the hadd and qisas, are upright. Then the claimant claimed and proved it. The surety becomes liable for the hundred that the claimant specified... (repeated from earlier) And he is not compelled etc.: meaning he is not compelled to give the surety according to Abu Hanifa, whether the hadd is for slander or not, because suretyship for the person is not permissible by consensus, since the surety is not possible, and the hadd of theft is joined to the hadd of slander according to the two opinions. It is restricted to qisas because in unintentional killing and wounding, he is compelled to give surety by consensus, because the obligator is property. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said he is compelled in the hadd of slander and qisas, because the hadd of slander includes a right of the servant, and qisas is dominated by the right of the servant. In other hudud, he is not compelled. If he willingly offers it without being asked, it is permissible by agreement. He (the Prophet) said: 'No suretyship in a hadd' without distinction between the right of the servant and the right of Allah. It is said this is from the statement of Shuraih, and it is said it is reported as marfu'. And the interpretation of compulsion according to them (the two) here is not that he is compelled by imprisonment and other punishment, but he orders him to accompany and go where he goes, and when he wants to enter his house, he asks permission; if he permits, he enters with him; if he does not permit, he prevents him from entering, and seats him at the door of the house so that he does not disappear by exiting from another place. They only compel in the hadd of slander and qisas because the claim is a condition in them, and the claimant needs to gather his witnesses and the defendant, so perhaps the defendant hides himself, so the surety is taken as a security. Contrary to other hudud because the claim is not a condition in them. And it is restricted to hadd because in ta'zir, the judge may ask for a surety. And he is not imprisoned in them etc. because imprisonment here is for suspicion of corruption, and the testimony of the upright (musturan) is sufficient to establish the ruling, so it is sufficient to establish the suspicion. The report of a single upright witness is a proof in religious matters and transactions, so suspicion is established by the testimony of a single upright witness, even if the original right is not established. Imprisonment for suspicion of corruption is legislated because Ali imprisoned a man on suspicion. Contrary to imprisonment in a claim for property where he is not imprisoned unless the original right is established against him, because it is the ultimate punishment and is only established with complete proof. In the Nawadir of Abu Yusuf, from the narration of Ibn Sama'ah: regarding one who collects wine, drinks it, and abandons prayer, I imprison him, discipline him, then release him. One who is accused of murder, theft, and harming people, I imprison him and keep him in prison until he repents, because his harm is upon people, and the harm of the first is upon himself.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage deals with two related issues: (1) the compulsion (jabr) to provide a surety in cases of hadd and qisas, and (2) imprisonment based on suspicion with two upright witnesses. Abu Hanifa holds that one cannot be compelled to provide a surety for the person in any hadd or qisas, because suretyship for the person is not permissible by consensus in such cases. The reason is that the surety cannot guarantee the person's presence for corporal punishment. For unintentional killing or injury where the obligation is monetary, compulsion is allowed by consensus. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that compulsion is allowed in hadd of qadhf (slander) and qisas because these involve rights of the servant (haqq al-'abd). The compulsion they refer to is not imprisonment but rather ordering the defendant to accompany the claimant and not leave his presence. Regarding imprisonment, the text states that a person can be imprisoned on suspicion of corruption if two upright witnesses testify to their bad character, even if the original crime is not proven. This is based on the practice of Ali (RA) and the principle that a single upright witness can establish suspicion. The passage also cites a narration from Abu Yusuf's Nawadir about imprisoning those who harm others or abandon prayer.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): Fixed punishment prescribed by Allah for specified crimes (e.g., theft, slander, adultery).
القود (al-qawad): Qisas; retributive justice, e.g., eye for an eye, life for life.
المستور (al-mastur): An upright witness whose reliability is not explicitly known but presumed due to apparent uprightness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, in which cases is compulsion to provide a surety not allowed?
A) In all financial claims
B) In all hudud and qisas
C) Only in hadd of slander
D) Only in murder cases

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's ruling that compulsion of suretyship is invalid in hudud and qisas is based on a general hadith and is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
10 min
Suretyship with Property: Conditions, Unknown Amounts, and Creditor's Rights
Rulings on suretyship with property: conditions, wording, and creditor's remedies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on suretyship with an unknown amount, identify the conditions for a valid debt eligible for suretyship, and explain the creditor's right to choose between debtor and surety.

Core Idea
Suretyship tolerates unknown amounts due to expansiveness; only non-extinguishable 'valid debts' qualify.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary examines the core rules of suretyship with property (kafalah bi al-mal). Three critical issues are addressed: whether the amount must be known, what counts as a 'valid debt' (dayn sahih), and what rights the creditor gains. Understanding these distinctions is essential because they differentiate Hanafi practice from other schools and have direct practical consequences for bankers, lenders, and anyone arranging guarantees.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position on unknown amounts
The text states that suretyship is valid even if the property is unknown. Identify the principle (tawsi'ah – expansiveness) and note the analogy to suretyship for darak (indemnity) which enjoys consensus despite ignorance.
Opposing schools (Shafi'i, Hanbali) require known amounts; the Hanafi response is based on the gratuitous nature of the contract.
2
Define 'valid debt' and list exclusions
The passage restricts suretyship to 'dayn sahih' – a debt that cannot be extinguished except by payment or discharge. Identify the excluded debts: badal al-kitabah (mukatab’s ransom), badal al-sa'yah (disputed), zakat, and jizyah. Understand why each fails the definition.
The key test: can the debtor unilaterally drop the obligation? If yes, it is not a valid debt.
3
Analyze the required wording and its implications
The surety must use a binding formula like 'kafaltu' or 'daminitu'. Mere promises are not enough. Examine the example of 'ma ba'at' (whatever you sold): Abu Hanifa treats it as conditional (first sale only), while Abu Yusuf (in Nawadir) treats it as general (all sales).
The ambiguity of 'ma' (whether conditional or relative) is resolved by later preference (mufta bihi) – often following the general view to avoid dispute.
4
Examine creditor's rights and the effect of discharge stipulation
Once valid suretyship is established, the creditor may claim from debtor, surety, or both (since it is a joining of liabilities, not a transfer). However, if the contract stipulates the debtor’s discharge, it becomes a hawalah (transfer) – then the creditor cannot claim the debtor except if the surety defaults.
Malik holds the debtor is discharged automatically; this difference (thamarah) affects litigation strategy.

أو عدل وبالمال ولو مجهولاً إن كان ديناً صحيحاً بكفلت عنه بألف، وبمالك المال الذي تكفل به احتراز عن بدل الكتابة عليه وبما يدركك في هذا البيع، وما بايعت فلاناً فعلي،
أو عدل: أي أو يشهد شاهد عدل يعرفه القاضي بالعدالة. (ط، عيني) وبالمال: لما فرغ عن بيان الكفالة بالنفس شرع في بيان الكفالة بالمال، فقال: و"بالمال"، وهو عطف على قوله : "بالنفس" أي وتصح الكفالة بالمال.
وعند الكفالة
ولو مجهولاً واصل بما قبله أي ولو كان المال مجهولا عندنا؛ لأن مبنى الكفالة على التوسعة، فيجوز مطلقاً، الشافعي في الأصح وأحمد في رواية: لا يصح في المجهول، وأجمعوا على صحتها بالدرك مع أنه لا يعلم كم يستحق من المبيع. إن كان دينا إلخ : وهو كل دين لا يسقط إلا بالأداء أو الإبراء، وفيه احتراز عن بدل الكتابة، فإن الكفالة به لا تجوز؛ لأنه ليس بدين صحيح، ألا تری أن المكاتب يملك إسقاطه بتعجيز نفسه والتعجيز ليس بأداء ولا إبراء، وكذا لا تجوز ببدل السعاية عنده، خلافا لهما وكأنه ألحق ببدل الكتابة، وكذا لا تصح ) بدين على من دخل في مكاتبته وكذا دين الزكاة؛ لأنه ليس دينا حقيقة من كل وجه، ولهذا لا يؤخذ من تركته، وكذا الجزية؛ لسقوطها بالتداخل والإسلام. (عيني، فتح) بكفلت عنه: [ متعلق بمحذوف أي تصح الكفالة بالمال بقوله: كفلت (عيني)] أي تصح الكفالة بالمال بقوله: "كفلت" عن فلان بألف درهم في المال المعلوم، ونبه بالمثال على أنه لابد أن يأتي بصيغة تدل على الالتزام، ومن ذلك ضمنت بخلاف ما لو قال: دينك الذي على فلان أنا أدفعه إليك أنا أسلمه، أنا أقبضه، فإنه لا كفيلاً بهذه الألفاظ؛ لأن المواعيد لا تلزم إلا باكتساب صور التعليق، وقوله: "بما لك عليه" أي تصح أيضاً لو قال: كفلت عن فلان بما يثبت لك عليه في المال المجهول، وكذا بعض مالك عليه، ويلزمه أن يبين أي مقدار شاء. (عيني، فتح) بألف أي ألف درهم وهذا مثال للمال المعلوم. (عيني) وبما يدركك إلخ وتصح أيضاً لو قال رجل للمشتري: كفلت عن البائع بما يلحقك في هذا البيع من يعني إذا استحق المبيع من يدك، ولزمك غرامة الثمن؛ لأن مبنى الكفالة على التوسع فإنها تبرع ابتداء، فيتحمل فيها جهالة المكفول به سواء كانت يسيرة أو غيرها بشرط أن تكون متعارفة، فإذا استحق المبيع كان للمشتري أن يخاصم البائع أولا، فإذا ثبت عليه استحقاق المبيع كان له أن يأخذ الثمن من أيهما شاء، وليس له أن يخاصم الكفيل أولا. (عيني، فتح)

وما بايعت: أي لو قال: وما بايعت من فلان فإني ضامن لثمنه، تصح الكفالة، ولو قال: ما اشتريته منه فإني ضامن للمبيع، لا يكون كفيلاً؛ لأن الكفالة بالمبيع لا تجوز، واختلف في لفظة "ما"، فقيل شرطية، معناه إن بايعت فلاناً، فيكون في معنى التعليق كإذا أو متى أو إن ولا يلزمه الثمن إلا أول مرة، ولهذا نقل في "المجرد" قول أبي حنيفة: لو قال: ما بايعت فلاناً فعلي، فبايعه مرة بعد مرة يلزمه ثمن ما بايع أول مرة، ولا يلزمه ما بايع .

بعده، وقيل: كلمة "ما" في المثال المذكور عامة؛ لأنها توجب العموم، فإذا لم يوقت فذلك على جميع العمر، وما بايعه مرة بعد مرة، فذلك كله على الكفيل، سواء باع بالنقد أو بغيره، وهكذا رواه ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف في "النوادر". (عيني، فتح المعين)
وما ذاب لك عليه أي ثبت ووجب لك على فلان وهو ماض أريد به المستقبل، فيكون كفالة بمال يجب على الأصيل بعد عقد الكفالة لا قبله حتى لو كان المال واجباً قبل الكفالة لا يجب على الكفيل. (عيني، فتح المعين) وما غصبك قيد الأمثلة بذكر المكفول له بضمير الخطاب، والمكفول عنه بلفظ فلان، وهو علم للأناسي كما تقرر في موضعه إشعاراً بوجوب معلومية المكفول عنه والمكفول له في الكفالة؛ لأنه إذا كان أحدهما مجهولاً لا تصح الكفالة، مثلاً: لو قال لرجل: ما ذاب لك على أحد من الناس فأنا ضامن، فإنه لا تصح الكفالة؛ لجهالة المكفول عنه، وكذلك لو قال: ما ذاب عليك لأحد من الناس فهو علي، فإنه لا تصح الكفالة؛ لجهالة المكفول له. ولو قال لآخر: ما غصبك فلان أو ما سرقك فلان فإني ضامن تصح الكفالة؛ لمعلومية المكفول له وعنه، بخلاف ما لو قال: ما غصبك الناس، أو من غصبك من الناس أو بايعك أو قتلك، أو من غصبته أو قتلته فأنا كفيله، فإنه باطل حتى يسمي إنسانا بعينه وكذا قوله ما غصبك أهل هذه الدار فأنا ضامنه، فإنه باطل حتى شخصاً معيناً، ولو قال رجل للمودع: إن أتلف المودع وديعتك أو جحد فأنا ضامن لك، صح، وكذا لو قال: إن قتلك أو قطعك فلان خطأ فأنا ضامن للدية، صحت الكفالة، بخلاف قوله: إن أكلتك السبع فأنا ضامن لا تصح عيني، فتح، جامع الرموز) فطالب الكفيل: أي إذا صحت الكفالة فالمكفول له بالخيار بين أن يطالب الذي عليه الأصل أي الدين، ويسمى الدين أصلاً؛ لأن المطالبة مبنية عليه، فإن مطالبة الدين بغير الدين غير متصور، فكانت المطالبة فرعاً، وبين أن يطالب الكفيل، أو طالب كليهما، وهذا التخيير بناء على ما تقدم أن الكفالة ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة، وذلك يقتضي قيام الأولى لا البراءة عنها، وقال مالك: يبرأ المديون. (عيني، عناية، مسكين) إلا إذا شرط البراءة: [ أي براءة المديون عن الدين. (ط) (معدن)] يعني المكفول له مخير إلا فيما إذا شرط البراءة عن المديون في الكفالة فتصير الكفالة حوالة اعتباراً للمعنى، فلا يطالب الأصيل إلا بالتوى عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي، والتوى أحد الأمرين، أو أحد الأمور الثلاثة على ما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى. [رمز الحقائق: ١٠٠/٢] كما أن الحوالة إلخ وهي نقل الدين من ذمة إلى ذمة (عيني)] أي لو شرط في الحوالة أن لا يبرأ بها المديون تكون كفالة فحينئذ للطالب أن يطالب الكفيل أو المديون فيتخير بسبب الكفالة في طلب أيهما شاء،
=
Book of Suretyship. Rulings of suretyship. And [it is valid] with a just [witness] and with property, even if unknown, if it is a valid debt. [Example:] 'I have become surety for you for a thousand [dirhams]' and for what you have against him of property that he becomes surety for, as a precaution against the substitute for kitabah. And for what may befall you in this sale, and whatever you have sold to so-and-so is on me. 'Or a just [witness]' i.e., or that a just witness testifies whom the judge knows to be just. (T., 'Ayni) 'And with property': When he finished explaining suretyship for the person, he began explaining suretyship for property. He said: 'and with property' and it is conjuncted to his statement 'for the person' i.e., suretyship with property is valid. According to suretyship, 'even if unknown' is connected to what preceded it, i.e., even if the property is unknown in our view; because the basis of suretyship is on expansiveness, so it is absolutely permissible. Al-Shafi'i in the soundest opinion and Ahmad in one narration: it is not valid for the unknown. And they have consensus on its validity for indemnity even though it is not known how much is owed from the sold item. 'If it is a debt etc.': i.e., every debt that is not extinguished except by payment or discharge. In this there is a precaution against the substitute for kitabah, for suretyship for it is not permissible because it is not a valid debt. Do you not see that the mukatib has the power to drop it by incapacitating himself, and incapacitation is neither payment nor discharge. Similarly, it is not permissible for the substitute for sa'yah according to him, contrary to them two, and it seems he analogized it to the substitute for kitabah. Similarly, it is not valid for a debt on one who entered into his kitabah, and likewise the zakat debt because it is not truly a debt from every aspect, hence it is not taken from his estate. Likewise the jizyah because it falls away by overlap and conversion to Islam. ('Ayni, Fath) 'I have become surety for you' [relates to an omitted element i.e., suretyship with property is valid by his saying: 'I have become surety' ('Ayni)] i.e., suretyship with property is valid by his saying: 'I have become surety for so-and-so for a thousand dirhams' for the known property. And he indicated by the example that it is necessary to come with a formula indicating obligation, and from that is 'daman' (I guarantee) contrary to if he said: 'Your debt which is on so-and-so, I will pay it to you, I will deliver it, I will collect it' then he is not a surety by these phrases because promises are not binding unless they acquire forms of suspension. And his statement: 'for what you have against him' i.e., it is also valid if he said: 'I have become surety for so-and-so for what is proven to be owed to you in unknown property', and likewise part of what you have against him, and it is incumbent on him to specify whatever amount he wishes. ('Ayni, Fath) 'For a thousand' i.e., a thousand dirhams, and this is an example of known property. ('Ayni) 'And for what may befall you etc.' And it is valid also if a man says to the buyer: 'I have become surety for the seller for what may befall you in this sale' meaning if the sold item is claimed from your hand and you incur a loss of the price, because the basis of suretyship is on expansiveness, as it is a gratuitous contract from the outset, so the ignorance of the subject matter is borne, whether it is slight or otherwise, on condition that it is customary. Then if the sold item is claimed, the buyer may litigate with the seller first. Then if the claim of the sold item is established against him, he may take the price from whichever of them he wishes. And he may not litigate with the surety first. ('Ayni, Fath) ... 'And whatever you have sold' i.e., if he said: 'Whatever I have sold from so-and-so, I am guarantor for its price', the suretyship is valid. And if he said: 'Whatever I have bought from him, I am guarantor for the sold item', he is not a surety because suretyship for the sold item is not valid. And there is disagreement about the word 'ma': it is said to be conditional, meaning 'if you sell to so-and-so', so it is in the sense of suspension like 'idha' or 'mata' or 'in', and he is only liable for the price the first time. Hence it is narrated in 'al-Mujarrad' the statement of Abu Hanifa: If he says: 'Whatever I have sold to so-and-so is on me', then he sells to him again and again, he is liable for the price of what he first sold, and he is not liable for what he sold afterwards. And 'whatever is due to you from him is on me' and 'whatever so-and-so has wrongfully taken from you is on me.' Then the creditor may claim from the surety or the debtor, except when he stipulates discharge; then it becomes a hawalah just as hawalah with the condition that the debtor is not discharged by it...
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the rulings of suretyship with property (kafalah bi al-mal). The author begins by noting that suretyship with property is valid, even if the amount is unknown, because the basis of suretyship is expansiveness (tawsi'ah). This is the Hanafi position, differing from al-Shafi'i and Ahmad who require the amount to be known. The Hanafis argue by consensus that suretyship for indemnity (darak) is valid despite the unknown amount. The commentary then specifies that suretyship is only valid for a 'valid debt' (dayn sahih), which excludes the substitute for kitabah (ransom of a mukatab slave) because it can be dropped by the mukatab without payment or discharge. Similarly, suretyship is not valid for badal al-sa'yah, zakat debt, or jizyah due to their unique characteristics. The proper wording for suretyship must indicate obligation, like 'kafaltu' (I became surety) or 'daminitu' (I guarantee). Mere promises are not binding. Several valid formulas are given: 'kafaltu 'an fulan bi-alf dirham', 'bima laka 'alayh', 'bima yudrikuka fi hadha al-bay' (for what may befall you in this sale), 'ma ba'ata fulanan fa-'alayya' (whatever you sold to so-and-so is on me). For the last formula, Abu Hanifa holds that it covers only the first sale if 'ma' is conditional, while Abu Yusuf (in Nawadir) holds it covers all sales. The passage emphasizes that for suretyship to be valid, both the surety's principal (mukful 'anhu) and the beneficiary (mukful lahu) must be known; ambiguous phrases like 'anyone' invalidate the contract. Once valid suretyship is established, the beneficiary has the option to claim from either the principal debtor or the surety, or both. This is because suretyship joins two liabilities (damm dhimma ila dhimma), not a transfer. Malik held that the debtor is discharged. However, if the parties stipulate the creditor's discharge of the debtor in the suretyship contract, it becomes a hawalah (transfer of debt); then the creditor cannot claim from the debtor unless the surety defaults. This is the Hanafi view, contrary to al-Shafi'i.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of suretyship where one person (the surety) joins his liability to that of the principal debtor for a debt or obligation, without transferring the debt.
بدل الكتابة (badal al-kitabah): The ransom money that a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) owes to his master, which can be dropped by the slave's own action (incapacitation), hence not a 'valid debt' for suretyship.
مكفول له (mukful lahu): The person in whose favor the suretyship is made (the creditor or beneficiary). He must be known (ma'lum) for the contract to be valid.
توسعة (tawsi'ah): Expansiveness; a principle that allows flexibility in gratuitous contracts, tolerating ignorance in subject matter where necessary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, is suretyship valid if the amount of debt is unknown (e.g., 'I guarantee whatever debt you have on him')?
A) No, because gharar is prohibited.
B) Yes, because the basis of suretyship is expansiveness and it is a gratuitous contract.
C) Yes, but only if the amount is later specified before any claim.
D) No, because the debtor and creditor must know the amount.

Key Takeaway
Suretyship in Hanafi fiqh is expansive: it tolerates unknown amounts but strictly requires the debt to be a valid, non-extinguishable obligation, and the creditor may claim from either the debtor or the surety unless discharge is stipulated.

Creditor's Option to Sue Debtor or Guarantor: The Hanafi View
Explaining the creditor's choice and the effect of discharge stipulation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the creditor may sue either the debtor or the guarantor, and identify the exception that transforms kafalah into hawalah when discharge is stipulated.

Core Idea
In Hanafi kafalah, liability is joined, not transferred; the creditor retains choice until discharge is stipulated.

When a guarantee (kafalah) is concluded, does the creditor lose the right to sue the original debtor? The answer depends on the definition of kafalah. The Hanafi school views it as a joining of liabilities (damm dhimma ila dhimma), so the debtor remains liable. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position, contrasts it with the Maliki view that the debtor is discharged, and explains the critical exception that turns kafalah into hawalah (transfer of debt).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core Hanafi ruling
The passage states clearly that after a valid kafalah, the creditor (makful lahu) has the option (khiyar) to sue the original debtor, the guarantor, or both simultaneously.
This is the default Hanafi position, built on the definition of kafalah as joining liabilities.
2
Understand the Hanafi definitional basis
Kafalah is defined as 'damm dhimma ila dhimma' (joining a liability to another liability). This means the guarantor's liability is added to the debtor's, not replacing it. The creditor's claim against the debtor remains intact.
This definition is unique to the Hanafi school and is the foundation for the ruling.
3
Contrast with the Maliki position
Imam Malik holds that upon a valid kafalah, the debtor is discharged and the liability transfers solely to the guarantor. The passage notes this disagreement.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different definitions of kafalah.
4
Examine the exception: stipulated discharge
If the parties stipulate that the debtor is discharged (bara'ah), the contract ceases to be kafalah and becomes hawalah (transfer of debt). In that case, the creditor can only sue the debtor if the guarantor becomes insolvent (tawa).
This shows the Hanafi school's nuanced approach: the nature of the contract is determined by the conditions agreed upon.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: Under Hanafi, a creditor can always return to the debtor even after suing the guarantor. Under Maliki, once the guarantee is concluded, the debtor is free. This affects the creditor's recovery strategy.
In Hanafi, the creditor has maximum flexibility; in Maliki, the creditor must rely solely on the guarantor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فطالب الكفيل: أي إذا صحت الكفالة فالمكفول له بالخيار بين أن يطالب الذي عليه الأصل أي الدين، ويسمى الدين أصلاً؛ لأن المطالبة مبنية عليه، فإن مطالبة الدين بغير الدين غير متصور، فكانت المطالبة فرعاً، وبين أن يطالب الكفيل، أو طالب كليهما، وهذا التخيير بناء على ما تقدم أن الكفالة ضم ذمة إلى ذمة في المطالبة، وذلك يقتضي قيام الأولى لا البراءة عنها، وقال مالك: يبرأ المديون. (عيني، عناية، مسكين) إلا إذا شرط البراءة: [ أي براءة المديون عن الدين. (ط) (معدن)] يعني المكفول له مخير إلا فيما إذا شرط البراءة عن المديون في الكفالة فتصير الكفالة حوالة اعتباراً للمعنى، فلا يطالب الأصيل إلا بالتوى عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي، والتوى أحد الأمرين، أو أحد الأمور الثلاثة على ما سيجيء إن شاء الله تعالى. [رمز الحقائق: ١٠٠/٢] كما أن الحوالة إلخ وهي نقل الدين من ذمة إلى ذمة (عيني)] أي لو شرط في الحوالة أن لا يبرأ بها المديون تكون كفالة فحينئذ للطالب أن يطالب الكفيل أو المديون فيتخير بسبب الكفالة في طلب أيهما شاء، كفالة، ولو طالب أحدهما، له أن يطالب الآخر
So the creditor may sue the guarantor: that is, if the kafalah is valid, the beneficiary has the choice between suing the one who owes the original debt, and the debt is called the original because the claim is based on it, for claiming a debt without the debt is inconceivable, so the claim is a branch, and between suing the guarantor, or suing both of them. And this choice is based on what preceded, that kafalah is the joining of a liability to a liability in the claim, and that necessitates the subsistence of the first, not discharge from it. And Malik said: the debtor is discharged. (Ayni, Inayah, Miskeen) Except when he stipulates discharge: [that is, discharge of the debtor from the debt. (T) (Ma'din)] Meaning the beneficiary has a choice except when discharge of the debtor is stipulated in the kafalah, so the kafalah becomes a hawalah by consideration of the meaning, so the principal is not sued except in case of insolvency according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. And insolvency is one of two matters, or one of three matters, as will come. And just as hawalah, etc., it is the transfer of a debt from one liability to another (Ayni). That is, if it is stipulated in hawalah that the debtor is not discharged by it, it becomes a kafalah. Then the creditor may sue the guarantor or the debtor, and he has a choice due to the kafalah to sue whichever he wishes. And if he sues one of them, he may sue the other.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage explains the fundamental Hanafi ruling regarding the creditor's (makful lahu) right to choose whom to sue after a valid kafalah contract. The text begins by stating that the creditor has the option to sue either the original debtor (al-asil, also called al-madyun), or the guarantor (al-kafil), or both simultaneously. The reasoning is that kafalah in the Hanafi school is a 'joining of liability to liability' (damm dhimma ila dhimma), meaning the guarantor's liability is added to that of the debtor without canceling the debtor's liability. Therefore, the creditor's right against the debtor remains intact. The commentary contrasts this with the Maliki position, where the debtor is considered discharged upon the conclusion of kafalah. The crucial exception is when the parties stipulate that the debtor is discharged (sharat al-bara'ah). In that case, the contract becomes a hawalah (transfer of debt) rather than kafalah, because the guarantor's liability replaces the debtor's. Consequently, the creditor can only sue the debtor if the guarantor becomes insolvent (tawā diyat). This is specifically Hanafi, and the text notes disagreement with al-Shafi'i, who may have different rules. The passage also draws an analogy with hawalah: if in hawalah the parties stipulate that the debtor is not discharged, it becomes kafalah. This reciprocal transformation shows that the distinction between kafalah and hawalah is based on the presence or absence of a discharge condition. Finally, the text asserts that suing one party does not preclude suing the other later, because the essence of kafalah is the joining of liabilities, which is not fulfilled without the possibility of suing each individually. This differentiates it from cases of usurpation (ghasb), where choosing to sue one wrongdoer precludes suing the other.

Key Terms
ضم ذمة إلى ذمة (damm dhimma ila dhimma): Joining a liability to another liability; the Hanafi definition of kafalah where the guarantor's liability is added to the debtor's.
الحوالة (al-hawalah): Transfer of debt from the debtor's liability to the guarantor's liability, with the debtor being discharged.
التوى (al-tawa): Insolvency or inability to pay; the condition under which the creditor may revert to the debtor in a hawalah that was originally kafalah with discharge condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if a kafalah contract stipulates that the debtor is discharged?
A) The contract becomes invalid (batil).
B) The contract remains kafalah but the creditor can only sue the debtor if the guarantor is insolvent.
C) The contract becomes a hawalah (transfer of debt), and the debtor can only be sued if the guarantor is insolvent.
D) The debtor remains liable together with the guarantor.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi kafalah, the creditor retains the right to sue the debtor because the guarantor's liability is added to, not replacing, the debtor's liability.

Valid and Invalid Conditions for Suspending Kafalah
Detailing which conditions may suspend kafalah and which render it invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a condition for suspending kafalah as suitable (sahih) or unsuitable (batil) and justify your classification using Hanafi criteria.

Core Idea
A suspended kafalah is valid only if the condition directly relates to the debt's obligation or recovery.

Kafalah (guarantee) can be suspended on a future condition, but not all conditions are acceptable. The Hanafi school distinguishes between suitable (mulā'im) conditions—those affecting the obligation or recovery of the debt—and unsuitable (ghayr mulā'im) conditions that are mere chance. This lesson unpacks that distinction with classical examples from Kanz al-Daqaiq and its commentaries.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling
Recall that ta'liq (suspension) in kafalah is valid in principle, but only with certain conditions.
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Define 'suitable condition'
A suitable condition is one that is relevant to the purpose of kafalah: either it triggers the obligation of the debt or it facilitates its recovery.
The text gives three subcategories: wujūb al-ḥaqq, imkān al-istīfā', and ta‘adhdhur al-istīfā'.
3
Identify examples of suitable conditions
Review the examples: reclaiming the sold item (obligation), arrival of the debtor (recovery), absence of the debtor (difficulty of recovery).
Also note the condition that the beneficiary accepts the guarantee.
4
Identify unsuitable conditions
Unsuitable conditions are random events unrelated to the debt, like 'if the wind blows' or 'if it rains'.
These are invalid because they introduce chance (khaṭar), analogous to an invalid sale.
5
Analyze the reasoning
Understand why the distinction exists: kafalah is a binding commitment; attaching it to arbitrary events undermines its purpose and resembles gambling.
The analogy with sale (bay') reinforces the prohibition of gharar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq with commentary by al-Ayni and others discusses the permissibility of suspending (ta'liq) a kafalah contract on future conditions. The core ruling is that ta'liq is valid only if the condition is 'suitable' (mulā'im), meaning it relates to the obligation of the debt or to its recovery. The text identifies three categories of suitable conditions: (1) conditions related to the obligation of the right (wujūb al-ḥaqq), such as the seller guaranteeing the price if the sold item is reclaimed by its rightful owner; (2) conditions for the possibility of recovery (imkān al-istīfā'), such as the arrival of the debtor (Zaid) to the city, which facilitates payment; and (3) conditions of difficulty of recovery (ta‘adhdhur al-istīfā'), such as the debtor being absent. Examples of suitable conditions include: guaranteeing the price if the sold item is reclaimed, guaranteeing the deposit if the depositary denies it, and guaranteeing blood money if the principal kills someone. In all these cases, the condition is directly connected to the reason for the guarantee. Conversely, ta'liq is invalid if the condition is unsuitable (ghayr mulā'im), such as 'if the wind blows,' 'if the rain falls,' 'if a particular person enters the house,' or 'if so-and-so arrives while he is not the principal.' These are random events unrelated to the obligation or recovery, resembling chance (khāṭar), and are thus invalid by analogy with invalid sales. The commentary also includes a comparison with other contracts: like agency (wakālah) and guardianship (waṣāyah), and a clarification that suing the guarantor in kafalah does not entail transfer of ownership, unlike the case of usurpation (ghaṣb) where choosing to hold one usurper liable precludes suing the other.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending a contract on the occurrence of a future condition.
شرط ملائم (shart mulā'im): A suitable condition that is relevant to the purpose of the contract, e.g., obligation or recovery of the debt.
الخطر (al-khatar): Chance or gambling; an element that renders contract invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following conditions would make a suspended kafalah valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) 'If the wind blows, I am the guarantor for the debt.'
B) 'If the sold item is reclaimed by its rightful owner, I am the guarantor for its price.'
C) 'If the rain falls, I am the guarantor for the debt.'
D) 'If you enter the house, I am the guarantor for the debt.'

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a kafalah suspended on a condition is valid only if the condition directly affects the obligation or recovery of the debt; conditions based on chance are void.

Validity and Recourse in Kafalah (Suretyship)
States the validity of kafalah with/without order and the rule of recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of kafalah with and without the principal's order, and identify when the guarantor has the right of recourse.

Core Idea
A guarantor's right to recover payment depends on whether the debtor asked for the guarantee; without the order, it is a volunteer's gift.

This short passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq settles two key questions about suretyship (kafalah). First, is the contract valid even if the principal debtor never asked for it? Second, if the guarantor pays, can he recover from the debtor? The answer turns on one simple factor: the debtor's order (amr).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of kafalah
The text mentions kafalah bi amri (with the order of the principal debtor) and kafalah bi ghayri amri (without his order). Recognize these as the two scenarios.
The principal debtor is called al-makfūl ʻanhu.
2
Note the general validity
Both types are valid (sahih). The text opens with 'وتصح الكفالة' meaning the contract is sound in both cases.
Validity is not contingent on the debtor's order.
3
Identify the operative condition for recourse
The text conditions the right of recourse (rujūʻ) on the presence of the debtor's order. Only with the order does the guarantor have the right to reclaim what he paid.
The term 'أمره' (his order) is the key legal factor.
4
State the consequence of the condition
If the guarantee was with the debtor's order, the guarantor recovers; if without order, he does not. The second part of the text makes this clear.
Without order, the payment is considered a voluntary gift (tabarru').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتصح الكفالة بأمر المكفول عنه، وبغير أمره، فإن كفل بأمره، رجع بما أدى عليه، وإن كفل بغير أمره، لم يرجع
And the suretyship (kafalah) is valid with the order of the principal debtor (المكفول عنه), and without his order. If he became surety with his order, he has recourse for what he paid; and if he became surety without his order, he does not have recourse.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It establishes that a guarantee (kafalah) is valid whether the guarantor acts at the request of the principal debtor or without it. However, the right of recourse (ar-ruju') against the principal debtor depends on whether the guarantor acted with his order. If the guarantor paid the debt at the principal's request, he may recover what he paid. If he paid without being asked, he cannot recover because he is considered a volunteer (mutabarri'). This is the Hanafi position, as stated in the matn.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafālah): A contract whereby a person (guarantor) undertakes to be liable for a debt or obligation owed by another (principal debtor) to a creditor.
المكفول عنه (al-makfūl ʻanhu): The principal debtor on whose behalf the guarantee is made.
الكفيل (al-kafīl): The guarantor who assumes liability.
الرجوع (al-rujūʻ): The right of the guarantor to reclaim from the principal debtor the amount paid to the creditor.
بأمره / بغير أمره (bi-amrihī / bi-ghayri amrihī): With the order of the principal debtor or without it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person guarantees a debt without the debtor's request and then pays the creditor. According to the Hanafi school, does the guarantor have the right to recover the amount from the debtor?
A) Yes, because he paid a debt.
B) No, because he acted without the debtor's order.
C) Yes, but only if the debtor was notified after payment.
D) No, unless the debtor agreed to it in writing.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the validity of a guarantee does not depend on the principal's order, but the right of recourse does: no order means no recovery.

Legal Effects Between Guarantor and Principal in Kafalah
Matn stating four procedural rules between guarantor and principal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four key rules governing the relationship between guarantor and principal in Hanafi kafalah and apply them to a simple scenario.

Core Idea
The guarantor's obligation is derivative; discharge or deferral of the principal benefits the guarantor.

This lesson presents the core rules that define the legal relationship between the guarantor (kafil) and the principal debtor (asil) in a guarantee contract. These rules determine who can be sued first, when the guarantor can demand reimbursement, and how acts affecting the principal's debt impact the guarantor. Understanding these rules is essential for applying kafalah correctly in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Recognize the three parties: creditor (talib), guarantor (kafil), and principal debtor (asil). The rules concern only the guarantor and principal.
2
State the rule on direct suit
The creditor may not demand payment from the principal before the guarantor pays. The guarantor is the primary target of the claim.
Rule: 'ولا يطالب الأصيل بالمال قبل أن يؤدي عنه'
3
Explain mutual liability
If the creditor pressures the guarantor, the guarantor can pressure the principal to pay or indemnify. This right exists only if the guarantee was by the principal's order.
Condition: guarantee must be bi-amrih (by order) for mutual liability to apply.
4
Effect of payment by guarantor
When the guarantor pays, both the claim and the debt are extinguished for the principal. The principal is discharged.
Rule: 'وبرئ بأداء الكفيل عن المطالبة والمال الأصيل'
5
Effect of discharge or deferral of principal
If the creditor discharges (forgives) the principal or grants a deferral, the guarantor also benefits from the same discharge or deferral.
Rule: 'ولو أبرأ الأصيل أو أخر عنه برئ الكفيل وتأخر عنه'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يطالب الأصيل بالمال قبل أن يؤدي عنه فإن لوزم لازمه، وبرئ بأداء الكفيل عن المطالبة والمال الأصيل، ولو أبرأ الأصيل، أو أخر عنه برئ الكفيل، وتأخر عنه
And the principal (aṣīl) is not sued for the money before the guarantor pays on his behalf. If he is pressured, he (the guarantor) is pressured (to pay). And the guarantor's payment discharges him from the claim and the money, and the principal. And if the principal is discharged, or the payment deferred for him, the guarantor is discharged, and deferred for him.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage presents a sequence of rulings establishing the relationship between the guarantor and the principal debtor in a guarantee contract. First, the creditor (tālib) may not directly demand payment from the principal debtor before the guarantor has paid; the creditor's right of recourse is primarily against the guarantor. Second, if the creditor pressures the guarantor, the guarantor can then pressure the principal debtor to pay or to indemnify him, but only if the guarantee was made at the principal's order (as the commentary clarifies). Third, when the guarantor pays, both the debt and the right of claim against the principal are extinguished; the principal is also discharged. Fourth, if the principal debtor obtains a discharge from the creditor or a deferral of payment, the guarantor also benefits from the discharge or deferral. This is because the guarantor's obligation is derivative of the principal's debt. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the guarantor is not independently liable for the debt itself, but only for the right to claim (muṭālabah) the debt. Hence, if the debt is extinguished or deferred for the principal, it has the same effect for the guarantor.

Key Terms
الأصيل (al-aṣīl): The principal debtor on whose behalf the guarantee is made.
الملازمة (al-mulāzamah): Mutual coercion or liability: if the creditor pressures the guarantor, the guarantor can pressure the principal.
الإبراء (al-ibrā’): Discharge from debt; a unilateral act of the creditor forgiving the debt.
التأخير (al-ta’khīr): Deferral of payment; extending the deadline for the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the matn, if the principal debtor obtains a deferral of payment from the creditor, what is the effect on the guarantor?
A) The guarantor remains liable to pay immediately.
B) The guarantor also gets the deferral.
C) The guarantor is discharged entirely.
D) The deferral only applies if the guarantor agrees.

Key Takeaway
The guarantor's obligation is derivative: the principal cannot be sued first, mutual liability exists under condition, payment by guarantor discharges both, and discharge/deferral of principal benefits the guarantor.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
6 min
Surety Cannot Demand Payment from Principal Before Paying
Ruling that surety's right of recourse is conditional on payment, with proof via qiyas and a cross-madhhab comparison.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a surety's right of recourse against the principal debtor arises only after payment to the creditor, and explain the legal reasoning (qiyas) behind this rule.

Core Idea
A surety acquires the debt only by paying it; reimbursement follows payment, not the contract.

In Hanafi fiqh, kafalah (suretyship) creates a unique obligation. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies a key distinction: the surety does not automatically gain a right to claim from the principal debtor at the moment of signing. Instead, that right is contingent upon actual payment to the creditor. We will explore the legal reasoning, a cross-madhhab difference, and a practical stratagem.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the surety's claim for reimbursement from the principal debtor before paying the creditor. The ruling is that such a claim is not permissible; the surety cannot demand payment until he has paid the creditor.
The hukm is prohibition of claiming before payment, not a prohibition of the contract itself.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author states that 'transferring ownership of a debt from someone other than the one on whom it is due is not permissible.' This is a general legal principle (qiyas). The surety only owns the debt after paying it.
No explicit Quran or hadith is cited; the proof is aqli (rational) based on this principle.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof applies)
The surety's contract obligates him to be subject to demand (mutalaba) but does not transfer ownership of the debt. Ownership only occurs when he actually pays, because before payment the debt belongs to the creditor. Hence, he cannot claim from the principal before acquiring ownership.
Contrast with the agent (wakil) for purchase: ownership vests initially in the agent, allowing recourse before payment.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the surety can claim reimbursement immediately because he views kafalah as analogous to agency. The Hanafi response distinguishes the two: in agency, ownership transfers to the agent at the moment of purchase; in suretyship, ownership of the debt transfers only at payment.
This difference leads to practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf) in the timing of recourse.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is considered mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) due to the strength of the qiyas and the support of the Zahir al-Riwayah texts.
The stratagem (hila) for recourse without order is mentioned: the creditor gifts the debt to the surety and appoints him as agent to collect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يطالب الأصيل بالمال قبل أن يؤدي عنه فإن لوزم لازمه، وبرئ بأداء الكفيل عن المطالبة والمال الأصيل، ولو أبرأ الأصيل، أو أخر عنه برئ الكفيل، وتأخر عنه، الكفيل المال من يرجع المكفول به الطالب المطالبة أي المديون عن دينه المطالبة الأصيل والجواب: أن تمليك الدين من غير من عليه الدين لا يجوز ، فإذا نكل بأمره فبنفس الكفالة، كما يجب المال للطالب على الكفيل، يجب للكفيل على الأصيل، ولكن يؤخر إلى أدائه، وهذا لا يكون عند كفالته بغير أمره، فلا لتبرعه إلا إذا أجاز في المجلس، فيرجع وحيلة الرجوع بلا أمر: أن يهبه الطالب الدين، ويوكله بقبضه، وكذا لا يرجع إذا دفع المكفول به قبل وجوبه على الأصيل. (فتح، عناية) الأصيل بالمال: وهو المكفول عنه يعني المديون. عيني، معدن قبل أن يؤدي عنه: أي الكفيل لا يطالب المديون بالمال المكفول به قبل أدائه عن الأصيل إلى الطالب؛ لأنه التزم المطالبة، وإنما يتملك الدين بالأداء فلا يرجع قبل التملك، بخلاف الوكيل بالشراء حيث يرجع قبل الأداء؛ لأنه من الموكل بمنزلة البائع من المشتري فيما يرجع إلى الحقوق مثل التحالف والرد بالعيب وهو مبني على أن الملك يقع للوكيل ابتداء [ رمز الحقائق: ١٠٢/٢] (فتح)
And the principal debtor is not demanded with the money before he (the surety) pays on his behalf. So if he (the surety) is pressed, he presses him (the principal), and he is discharged by the payment of the surety from both the demand and the money, the principal. And if the creditor releases the principal, or postpones for him, the surety is discharged, and it is postponed for him. The surety: he recovers the money of the guaranteed debt from the principal debtor after demand. That is, the debtor regarding his debt, the demand of the principal. And the answer: that transferring ownership of a debt from someone other than the one on whom it is due is not permissible. So if he undertakes by his order, then by the suretyship itself, just as the money is owed to the creditor on the surety, it is owed to the surety on the principal, but it is postponed until his payment. And this does not occur when his suretyship is without his order, so he does not have recourse due to his voluntary act, unless he authorizes in the session, then he has recourse. And the stratagem for recourse without an order is that the creditor gifts the debt to him and appoints him to collect it. Likewise, he does not have recourse if he paid the guaranteed thing before it became obligatory on the principal. (Fath, 'Inayah) 'the principal with the money': he is the guaranteed person, meaning the debtor. 'Before he pays on his behalf': i.e., the surety is not to demand the debtor for the guaranteed money before his payment on behalf of the principal to the creditor; because he has undertaken the demand, and he only acquires ownership of the debt by payment, so he does not have recourse before acquisition, unlike the agent for purchase who has recourse before payment; because he is, regarding the principal, in the position of the seller vis-à-vis the buyer with respect to rights such as mutual rescission and return due to defect, and it is based on the fact that ownership occurs for the agent initially. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 102/2] (Fath)
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage states a fundamental ruling of kafalah (suretyship): a surety cannot demand reimbursement from the principal debtor until he has actually paid the debt to the creditor. The reason is that the surety only acquires ownership of the debt (tamalluk) at the moment of payment; before that, he has no right in the debt. The text distinguishes this from the case of an agent (wakil) for purchase, who can recover the price from the principal before paying because ownership initially vests in the agent. This distinction is based on a difference in the underlying legal construction. The passage also mentions a stratagem (hila) for the surety to secure recourse even without the principal's order: the creditor can gift the debt to the surety and appoint him as agent to collect it. The rulings for recourse are conditioned upon the suretyship being at the principal's request (bi amrihi).

Key Terms
الكفيل (al-kafil): The surety/guarantor – the person who undertakes to pay a debt or deliver an object if the principal debtor fails.
الأصيل (al-asil): The principal debtor – the person on whose behalf the surety undertakes the obligation.
المطالبة (al-mutalaba): The demand or claim – the surety's obligation to the creditor is to be subject to demand, not to owe the debt itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a surety (kafil) have the right to demand reimbursement from the principal debtor (asil)?
A) Immediately upon signing the suretyship contract.
B) Only after he has paid the creditor.
C) After one year from the contract.
D) Only if the principal debtor authorizes it.

Key Takeaway
The surety gains the right to reimbursement only after paying the creditor, because he acquires the debt by payment, not by the contract itself.

The Surety's Right to Press the Principal (Mulazama)
Ruling that surety can detain principal if creditor detains him

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mulazama and identify the two conditions required for the surety to press the principal.

Core Idea
The surety can press the principal before payment because the principal caused the predicament.

This lesson examines a unique Hanafi ruling: when a creditor pressures the surety, the surety may in turn pressure the principal debtor. This right, known as mulazama, is based on the principle of causation (tawrit) and is a departure from qiyas. We will explore the conditions, the evidence, and the opposing view of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act under discussion is the surety pressing or detaining the principal debtor when the creditor presses the surety. According to Hanafi fiqh, this is permissible under specific conditions.
This ruling is an exception (istihsan) to the general rule that the surety has no claim against the principal before payment.
2
List the Two Required Conditions
The text specifies two conditions: (1) the suretyship must have been at the principal's order, and (2) the surety must not owe the creditor a similar debt. If either condition is missing, mulazama is not permitted.
The first condition ensures the principal caused the surety's predicament; the second prevents circular claims.
3
Understand the Hanafi Justification
The Hanafi school justifies this using istihsan based on causation (tawrit): the principal's request for suretyship made the surety vulnerable, so the principal is obliged to rescue him.
This is a rational argument (dalil aqli) overriding the stricter qiyas.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i Position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the surety cannot press the principal before payment because he has no established right against the principal until the debt is paid. The Hanafi response emphasizes the principal's causative role.
This disagreement is a legitimate ikhtilaf rooted in different approaches to causation and analogy.
5
Apply the Rule to Hawala
The passage extends the same rule to a person upon whom a debt is transferred (muhal 'alayh) via hawala: if he is pressed or imprisoned and the hawala was by order, he has the same right to press the transferor.
This consistency shows the principle of causation applies across similar transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن لوزم إلخ [ أي الكفيل بالمال من جهة الطالب. (ط، عيني)] أي إذا لازم الطالب الكفيل لازم الكفيل المكفول عنه إذا لم يكن للمكفول عنه مثل الدين في ذمة الكفيل، وكفل بأمره؛ لأن الأصيل هو الذي أوقعه في هذه الورطة، فعليه خلاصه وإن كان على الكفيل دين للمطلوب مثله أو لم تكن الكفالة بأمره فلا ملازمة ولا حبس وكذا إذا حبس الكفيل من جهة الطالب له أن يحبس المطلوب إذا كانت الكفالة بأمره، وقال الشافعي: ليس له ذلك؛ لأنه لا يتعلق له حق على الأصيل قبل الأداء، قلنا: هو مورطه فعليه تخليصه، والمحال عليه إذا لوزم أو حبس وكانت الحوالة بالأمر حكمه حكم الكفيل. (فتح، عناية) : لازمه: أي لازم الكفيل الأصيل حتى يخلصه فإن حبس حبسه أيضاً. (ط) عيني)
So if he is pressed, etc. [i.e., the surety by money from the creditor's side. (T, 'Ayni)] Meaning: if the creditor presses the surety, the surety presses the guaranteed person (the principal) provided that the guaranteed person does not have a debt like the debt in the surety's liability, and the suretyship was by his order; because the principal is the one who caused him to fall into this predicament, so it is upon him to rescue him. But if the surety has a debt due from the claimant equal to it, or the suretyship was not by his order, then there is no pressing or imprisonment. Likewise, if the surety is imprisoned by the creditor, he may imprison the principal if the suretyship was by his order. Al-Shafi'i said: He does not have that right; because he has no right against the principal before payment. We said: He is the one who caused him to fall into this, so it is upon him to rescue him. And the one upon whom a hawala is made, if he is pressed or imprisoned and the hawala was by order, his ruling is the ruling of the surety. (Fath, 'Inayah) 'he presses him': i.e., the surety presses the principal so that he rescues him; if he is imprisoned, he may imprison him as well. (T, 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage discusses the rule of mulazama (pressing/compelling). If the creditor pressures the surety (e.g., by demanding payment or imprisoning him), the surety may in turn pressure the principal debtor, provided the suretyship was at the principal's request and the surety does not owe the creditor a similar debt. The rationale is that the principal caused the surety's predicament, so he is obliged to rescue him. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that the surety has no established right against the principal before payment. The Hanafi response is based on the principal's causative role (tawrit). The same rule applies to hawala (transfer of debt) when done by order.

Key Terms
الملازمة (al-mulazama): Pressing or compelling – the right of the surety to press the principal debtor when the creditor presses the surety, including imprisonment.
توريط (tawrit): Causing someone to fall into a predicament – the principal's act of requesting suretyship puts the surety at risk.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what is the basis for the surety's right to press the principal before payment?
A) The contract of kafalah itself
B) The principal's causative act (tawrit)
C) The creditor's demand
D) The surety's payment

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi doctrine of mulazama allows a surety to press the principal when pressed by the creditor, based on the principle of causation (tawrit), provided the suretyship was by the principal's order and the surety does not owe a similar debt.

Discharge of Surety by Release or Postponement of Principal
Ruling that surety is discharged when creditor releases or postpones principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that the surety is discharged when the creditor releases or defers the principal's debt, and explain the reasoning based on the ancillary nature of the surety's obligation.

Core Idea
The surety's obligation is a mere follower of the debt; when the principal is discharged or deferred, the surety is also discharged or deferred.

This passage establishes a key principle in kafalah: the surety's liability is entirely dependent on the underlying debt. We will examine how the creditor's release or postponement of the principal automatically releases or defers the surety's obligation, by consensus of the scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the opening statement: if the creditor releases (abra'a) the principal or defers (akhkhara) the debt, the surety is discharged (bari'a). Note that this is the main ruling.
2
Locate the proof
The text states this ruling is by ijma' (consensus). Even those who consider the surety to have a debt agree that the surety is discharged when the principal is discharged.
The proof is not from a specific text but from consensus.
3
Understand the reasoning
The reason is that the surety's obligation is merely a demand (mutalaba) that follows the debt. When the debt ceases or is deferred, the demand ceases or is deferred accordingly.
The surety does not have an independent debt; it is a follower (tabi').
4
Examine the exception
If the principal proves that he paid the debt before the suretyship contract, only the principal is discharged, not the surety. If payment occurred after suretyship, both are discharged.
Pre-suretyship payment means the surety's obligation was never validly tied to a debt.
5
Distinguish deferment by creditor vs. other deferment
The deferment that discharges the surety is only that granted by the creditor. Mere de facto postponement without creditor consent does not affect the surety.
This is analogous to a case where a slave is emancipated but the surety remains liable for obligations arising after emancipation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برئ بأداء الأصيل: لأن الأصيل يبرأ بالأداء، وبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل؛ لأنه ليس على الكفيل دين في الصحيح، وإنما عليه المطالبة بدون الدين وقد انتهت بانتهاء علتها فيبراً إجماعاً حتى عند من يقول بأن الكفيل عليه دين؛ لأن تعدد الدين عند القائل به أمر حكمي فيسقط بأداء واحد (عيني، فتح ولو أبرأ الطالب وهو رب المال. (عيني، مسكين) ولو أبر الأصيل قيد بإبراء الطالب؛ لأن براءة الأصيل بدون إبراء الطالب لا توجب براءة الكفيل، فلو كفل له ألفاً على فلان فبرهن فلان أنه كان قضاه إياها قبل الكفالة برئ الأصيل دون الكفيل، ولو بعدها برئا، وكذا قيد بتأخير الطالب المطالبة عن الأصيل؛ لأن تأخيرها لا بتأخير الطالب لا يسقطها عن الكفيل كما لو كفل بما يلزم العبد المحجور بعد عتقه، وقوله: "وتأخر عنه" أي عن الكفيل أيضاً؛ لأنه ليس عليه إلا المطالبة، وهي تبع للدين، فتسقط بسقوطه، وتتأخر بتأخيره. (عيني فتح عنه : أي عن الأصيل: بأن أجل دينه. (عيني) برئ الكفيل في الصورة الأولى أي لو أبرأ الأصيل. (عيني) وتأخر عنه: أي . الكفيل في الصورة الثانية أي لو أجل الدين على المطلوب وفيه لف ونشر. (عيني، مسكين)
He is discharged by the payment of the principal: because the principal is discharged by payment, and his discharge necessitates the discharge of the surety; because the surety has no debt according to the correct view, rather he is only subject to demand without the debt, and it has ended with the end of its cause, so he is discharged by consensus, even according to those who say that the surety has a debt; because the multiplicity of debt according to that holder is a juristic matter, so it falls with one payment. ('Ayni, Fath) And if the creditor releases, and he is the owner of the money. ('Ayni, Miskin) And if he releases the principal, it is restricted to the release of the creditor; because the discharge of the principal without the release of the creditor does not necessitate the discharge of the surety. So if he stood surety for one thousand for him against so-and-so, and so-and-so proved that he had paid it to him before the suretyship, the principal is discharged but not the surety; if after it, both are discharged. Similarly, it is restricted to the postponement of the creditor's demand from the principal; because its postponement not by the creditor's postponement does not drop it from the surety, as if he stood surety for what is obligatory on a restricted slave after his emancipation. And his statement: 'and it is postponed for him' means for the surety as well; because only the demand is upon him, and it is a follower of the debt, so it drops with its dropping and is postponed with its postponement. ('Ayni, Fath) 'for him': i.e., for the principal: by deferring his debt. ('Ayni) 'the surety is discharged' in the first case, i.e., if he released the principal. ('Ayni) 'and it is postponed for him': i.e., the surety in the second case, i.e., if he deferred the debt on the debtor, and in it there is laf wa nashr. ('Ayni, Miskin)
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage explains that the surety is automatically discharged when the principal debtor is released or given a deferment by the creditor. The basis is ijma' (consensus): the surety's obligation is merely a demand (mutalaba) that follows the underlying debt. When the debt ceases or is deferred, the demand ceases or is deferred accordingly. An important exception is noted: if the principal proves he had already paid the debt before the suretyship contract, only the principal is discharged, not the surety, unless the payment occurred after the suretyship. The commentary also clarifies that postponement ordered by the creditor alone affects the surety; mere de facto postponement without creditor consent does not.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Legal release or acquittal from a debt by the creditor.
التأخير (al-ta'khir): Postponement or deferment of the debt, giving the debtor more time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the creditor releases (yubri') the principal debtor from the debt, what happens to the surety according to all schools?
A) The surety remains fully liable.
B) The surety is also discharged.
C) The surety's liability is halved.
D) The surety must still pay but can recover from the principal.

Key Takeaway
The surety is discharged automatically by release or deferment of the principal because the surety's obligation is a follower of the debt.
