The Asymmetry of Release in Kafalah
Ruling with proof: asymmetry of release and deferment in kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that releasing the surety does not release the principal and explain the rationale based on the independent nature of the demand (mutalaba).

Core Idea
Releasing the surety does not release the principal — the surety's obligation is only the demand, not the debt itself.

In Hanafi fiqh, the relationship between the creditor (talib), the principal debtor (asil), and the surety (kafil) is carefully defined. One critical rule is that the surety's release does not extend to the principal. This lesson unpacks that rule, its reasoning, and an important exception that arises when the suretyship itself was granted with a term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling
Locate the statement: 'if the creditor releases the surety, he is released but not the principal'. This is the central legal rule.
2
Understand the Rationale
The text explains that the surety's obligation is merely the demand (mutalaba), not the debt itself. The debt survives independently on the principal. The creditor may abandon the demand against the surety while still pursuing the principal.
The Hanafi position relies on the separation of the surety's liability from the principal's.
3
Extend to Deferment
The same asymmetry applies to deferment: postponing the claim against the surety does not postpone it for the principal. The creditor can still demand immediate payment from the principal.
The deferment is a temporary release, treated like an absolute release in this context.
4
Recognize the Exception
If the suretyship was originally deferred (e.g., the surety guaranteed a debt payable after one month), then the deferment benefits both the surety and the principal. Why? Because there was never any immediate demand on the surety, so the term attaches to the debt itself.
The exception shows that the rule depends on the immediacy of the surety's obligation.
5
Apply to New Scenarios
Test your understanding: Suppose the creditor releases the surety but later demands payment from the principal. Is this valid? Yes, per the rule. What if the creditor grants the surety a three-month extension? The principal remains liable immediately unless the suretyship itself was conditional on that term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينعكس [ أي الحكم المذكور فلو أبرأ الطالب الكفيل برئ هو لا الأصيل، وكذا لو أخر المطالبة عن الكفيل لم يكن تأخيرا عن الأصيل فيطالب الأصيل في الصورتين. (ط) عيني)] أي لو أبرأ الكفيل برئ هو سواء قبل أو لم يقبل؛ لأن عليه المطالبة، وبقاء الدين بدونها جائز، وكذا لا يتأخر الدين على الأصيل بالتأخير عن الكفيل؛ لأنه إبراء مؤقت فيعتبر بالمؤبد، فيطالب الأصيل في الصورتين، وهذا إذا كان التأخير بعد ما كفل حالاً أما لو كفل بالمال مؤجلاً إلى شهر فإنه يتأجل عن الأصيل أيضاً؛ لأنه لا مطالبة على الكفيل حال فانصرف الأجل إلى الدين. (عيني، فتح)
And it does not reverse [i.e., the mentioned ruling: if the creditor releases the surety, he is released but not the principal; likewise, if he postpones the demand against the surety, it is not a postponement for the principal, so the principal is demanded in both cases. (T, 'Ayni)] Meaning: if he releases the surety, he is released whether he accepts or not; because the demand is upon him, and the survival of the debt without it is permissible. Similarly, the debt on the principal is not postponed by the postponement regarding the surety; because it is a temporary release, so it is considered like an absolute release, so the principal is demanded in both cases. But this is if the postponement occurs after the suretyship was immediate (halan). However, if he stood surety for the money deferred for a month, then it becomes deferred for the principal as well; because there is no demand on the surety at the time, so the term applies to the debt. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage establishes the asymmetry: releasing the surety does not release the principal, and deferring the claim against the surety does not defer it for the principal. The reason is that the surety's obligation is merely the demand (mutalaba), which is separate from the debt itself. The creditor can abandon the demand against the surety while still pursuing the principal. However, an exception is noted: if the suretyship itself was conditional on a term (e.g., deferred for a month), then that term also benefits the principal because the demand was never immediate. This shows the principle that the surety's obligation is subordinate but not identical to the principal's.

Key Terms
الانعكاس (al-in'ikas): The reversal of a ruling – here, the rule that releasing the surety does not reverse to release the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the principal debtor's obligation if the creditor releases the surety only?
A) The principal is also released.
B) The principal remains fully liable.
C) The principal's liability is halved.
D) The principal gets a deferment.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, releasing or deferring the surety does not affect the principal's liability because the surety's obligation is only the demand, not the debt itself.

Effects of Compromise (Sulh) in Kafalah: Discharge and Recourse
Rulings on compromise in suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule of compromise in suretyship, apply the four stipulation scenarios to determine discharge, and evaluate the surety's recourse conditions.

Core Idea
A compromise for half discharges both surety and principal, unless only the surety's release is stipulated.

This passage examines how a compromise (sulh) between the creditor and either the principal debtor or the surety affects their liabilities. The general rule is that both are discharged from the remainder. However, the surety's right to recover from the principal depends on whether the suretyship was ordered by the principal. Four distinct stipulation scenarios are outlined, along with an important exception when the compromise involves a different type of asset. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly structuring suretyship agreements and resolving disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the debt
The passage involves three parties: principal debtor (الأصيل), surety (الكفيل), and creditor (رب المال). The debt is 1000 dirhams, compromised for 500.
The compromise is for half the debt (نصفه).
2
Apply the default rule
If either party (principal or surety) compromises unconditionally with the creditor for half, both are discharged from the remaining 500. The surety's discharge is derivative of the principal's discharge.
Attribution: الصلح يبرئ الأصيل فتبرأ الكفيل.
3
Distinguish the four stipulation scenarios
The passage lists four possible stipulations in the compromise: (1) both releases mentioned → both discharged; (2) only principal's release → both discharged; (3) no stipulation → both discharged; (4) only surety's release → only surety discharged, principal remains liable for full 1000.
Crucial: only scenario 4 breaks the default.
4
Analyze the surety's recourse
If the surety pays the remaining 500, he can only recover from the principal if the suretyship was by the principal's order (بأمره). Otherwise, no recourse.
This is based on the principle of agency in suretyship.
5
Handle the exception: different asset type
If the surety compromises with a different type (جنس آخر), e.g., goods instead of cash, the compromise is an exchange (مبادلة). The surety owns the principal's debt and has full recourse for 1000.
Exception due to transformation of the contract's nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينعكس ولو صالح أحدهما ربّ المال عن ألف على نصفه برئا، وإن قال الطالب أي الأصيل أو الكفيل الذي هو دین وهو خمس مائة درهم للكفيل: برئت إلي من المال رجع على المطلوب، وفي برئت، حال كونك مؤديا الكفيل وهو المكفول عنه وجود الكفالة، برئا: أي إذا صالح أحدهما مطلقاً سواء كان كفيلا أو أصيلا رب المال عن ألف على نصفه برئ الكفيل والأصيل عن الخمس المائة الأخرى، فلا يرجع الكفيل إلا بنصف الألف على الأصيل فيما صالح، أما إذا صالح الأصيل فظاهر؛ لأنه بالصلح يبرأ، وبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل، وأما إذا صالح الكفيل، فلأن إضافة الصلح إلى الألف إضافة إلى ما على الأصيل فيبرأ الأصيل ضرورة فبراءته توجب براءة الكفيل، فإذا برئ عن خمس مائة، فإن أدى الكفيل الخمس مائة الباقية رجع على الأصيل بها إن كان بأمره، وإلا فلا يرجع. وهذه المسألة على أربعة أوجه: إما أن يذكر في الصلح براءتهما جميعاً فيبرئان جميعاً أو براءة الأصيل فكذلك، أو لم يشترط شيء فكذلك يبرءان، أو شرط أن يبرأ الكفيل لا غير فيبرأ هو وحده عن خمس مائة والألف على حاله على الأصيل، بخلاف ما إذا صالح الكفيل رب المال على جنس آخر حيث يرجع بكل الألف؛ لأنه مبادلة فملك ما في ذمة الأصيل فيرجع بكل ما عليه. (عيني، فتح)
And it does not reverse. And if one of them [the principal or the surety] compromises with the owner of the money regarding one thousand for half of it, both are discharged. And if the creditor says to the surety: 'I have released you from the money,' he has recourse against the debtor. And in [the case of] 'I have released you' [without 'to'], [and] while you are discharging, the surety – and he is the guaranteed person – existence of the suretyship. 'Both are discharged': i.e., if one of them unconditionally, whether he is surety or principal, compromises with the owner of the money regarding one thousand for half of it, the surety and the principal are both discharged from the other five hundred. So the surety only has recourse for half of the thousand against the principal in what he compromised. As for if the principal compromised, it is apparent; because he is discharged by the compromise, and his discharge necessitates the discharge of the surety. And as for if the surety compromised, it is because attributing the compromise to the thousand is attributing it to what is on the principal, so the principal is discharged necessarily, and his discharge necessitates the discharge of the surety. So if he is discharged from five hundred, and then the surety pays the remaining five hundred, he has recourse against the principal for that amount if it was by his order, otherwise he does not have recourse. This issue has four aspects: either that in the compromise both their discharges are mentioned, so both are discharged; or [only] the discharge of the principal, so likewise; or nothing is stipulated, so likewise both are discharged; or it is stipulated that only the surety is discharged, so he alone is discharged from five hundred, and the thousand remains as it was on the principal. This is contrary to the case where the surety compromises with the owner of the money for a different type (jins), where he has recourse for the full thousand; because it is an exchange, so he owns what was in the principal's liability, and thus has recourse for all that is upon him. ('Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage covers the effect of a compromise (sulh) between either the principal or the surety and the creditor. In general, a compromise for a reduced amount discharges both the surety and the principal from the remainder. However, the surety's right to recover from the principal depends on whether the suretyship was at the principal's order. If the surety compromised, his recourse is limited to half the original amount unless he paid a different type of asset, in which case he gets full recourse. Four scenarios are listed regarding stipulations in the compromise contract: if both releases are stipulated, both are discharged; if only the principal's release is stipulated, same; if no stipulation, same; if only the surety's release is stipulated, the surety is discharged from his share but the principal remains liable for the full debt. This shows the importance of clear contractual language.

Key Terms
الصلح (al-sulh): Compromise agreement between creditor and debtor/surety to settle a debt for less than the full amount, often to avoid litigation.
الأصيل (al-asil): The principal debtor, the original party liable for the debt.
الكفيل (al-kafil): The surety or guarantor, who assumes liability for the debt.
البراءة (al-bara'a): Legal discharge from liability, release from the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a compromise for half the debt, if the contract states 'the creditor releases the surety from the debt,' what is the effect on the principal?
A) The principal is also discharged from half.
B) The principal remains liable for the full original debt.
C) The principal is discharged from the entire debt.
D) The principal is discharged only if he agrees.

Key Takeaway
Unless otherwise stipulated, a compromise for half the debt discharges both surety and principal from the remainder, and the surety's recourse against the principal is limited to the amount paid and conditional on the principal's order.

Creditor's Wording and Surety's Recourse: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on creditor's release wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on surety's recourse when the creditor uses ambiguous release language and evaluate Abu Hanifa's and Abu Yusuf's opposing arguments.

Core Idea
The surety's recourse hinges on whether the creditor's ambiguous wording is interpreted as payment or remission.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute over the effect of a creditor's release wording on the surety's right of recourse. When the creditor explicitly says 'I have released you from the money' (using 'ila'), the surety can recover from the principal because the wording acknowledges payment. But if the creditor simply says 'I have released you' without 'ila', the meaning becomes ambiguous: it could be remission (ibra') or payment. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf disagree on whether this ambiguity blocks or allows recourse. Understanding their reasoning sharpens your ability to apply the Hanafi principle that certainty is not overcome by doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two wording formulas
First, distinguish the clear wording (برئت إلي من المال) from the ambiguous wording (برئت alone). The addition of 'ila' is legally significant because it indicates a transfer, implying payment.
The preposition 'ila' is the key to certainty.
2
State the agreed ruling for the clear wording
Both imams agree that when the creditor says 'برئت إلي من المال', the surety has recourse. The reason is that such phrasing necessitates payment – it is an acknowledgment of receipt.
3
Locate the dispute for the ambiguous wording
When the creditor says only 'برئت' (I have released you), the text presents a difference: Abu Hanifa denies recourse; Abu Yusuf grants it. Identify the basis of each view.
The dispute is purely semantic: what does 'برئت' imply?
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's argument
Abu Hanifa argues that 'برئت' is ambiguous: it could mean release by ibra' or by payment. Since we are certain only of release but doubtful of payment, payment cannot be proven by doubt (اليقين لا يزول بالشك). Recourse depends on payment, so it fails.
Note the usul maxim: certainty is not removed by doubt.
5
Analyze Abu Yusuf's counterargument
Abu Yusuf argues that 'برئت' can only mean release by payment because a debtor cannot be released without actually paying. The creditor's statement, by attributing release to the debtor's action, necessarily implies that the debtor paid. Thus, the surety can recover.
Abu Yusuf treats the absence of 'ila' as irrelevant to the inevitability of payment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجع على المطلوب: أي إذا قال الطالب للكفيل برئت إلي من المال الذي كفلت فلان رجع الكفيل على المكفول عنه بالمال إذا كفل بأمره وإلا لا ؛ لأن قوله: "برئت" إقرار منه بالإيفاء؛ لأن البراءة التي يكون ابتداؤها من الكفيل وانتهاؤها إلى الطالب لا تكون إلا بالإيفاء منه، فصار كأنه قال: دفعت إلي أو قبضته منك فلا يرجع الطالب على واحد منهما؛ لإقراره بالاستيفاء من الكفيل، ويرجع الكفيل على الأصيل. (عيني، فتح) وفي برئت إلخ : أي لو قال الطالب للكفيل: برئت من غير أن يقول: "إلى" لا الكفيل على الأصيل؛ لأن يرجع قوله: "برئت" محتمل، يحتمل أنه برئ بإبرائه، ويحتمل أنه برئ بالأداء فتيقنا بحصول البراءة بأي أمر كان، وشككنا في الأداء فلا يثبت الأداء بالشك، ولا يثبت له الرجوع؛ لأنه متفرع على الأداء، وعند أبي يوسف: يرجع في "برئت"؛ لأنه لا يحتمل إلا الإبراء بالقبض؛ لأنه أقر ببراءة ابتدائها من المطلوب؛ لأنه نسب البراءة إليه ولا يقدر المطلوب صنع، = به عن أن يبرأ إلا بأداء بأن يضع المال بين يديه ويخلي بينه وبين المال، فيبرأ بذلك وإن لم يوجد من الطالب
He has recourse against the debtor: i.e., if the creditor says to the surety: 'I have released you from the money for which you stood surety for so-and-so,' the surety has recourse against the guaranteed person for the money if he stood surety by his order, otherwise not; because his statement 'I have released you' is an acknowledgment from him of payment; because a release whose beginning is from the surety and whose end is to the creditor can only be by payment from him, so it is as if he said: 'I paid to you' or 'I received it from you.' So the creditor has no claim against either of them due to his acknowledgment of receipt from the surety, and the surety has recourse against the principal. ('Ayni, Fath) And in [the case of] 'I have released you' etc.: i.e., if the creditor says to the surety: 'I have released you' without saying 'to', the surety does not have recourse against the principal; because his statement 'I have released you' is ambiguous: it may mean he is released by his release, or he is released by payment. Since we are certain of the release occurring by some means, but we doubt about payment, payment is not established by doubt, and recourse is not established for him because it is dependent on payment. According to Abu Yusuf, he does have recourse in [the case of] 'I have released you' because it can only mean release by receipt (qabd); because he acknowledged a release whose beginning is from the debtor, and the debtor is not able to become released except by payment, such as by placing the money before him and vacating possession of it, so he is released thereby even if the creditor does not [accept]?
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about the interpretation of the creditor's words. If the creditor says 'I have released you from the money' (using the preposition 'ila'), it is an acknowledgment of payment, and the surety can recover from the principal. If the creditor simply says 'I have released you' (without 'ila'), the wording is ambiguous: it could mean release by ibra' (remission) or by payment. Abu Hanifa holds that the surety cannot recover because payment is not established (certainty is not overridden by doubt). Abu Yusuf holds that the phrase must refer to release by payment because the debtor cannot be released without payment, so the surety does have recourse. The text ends mid-sentence but the core dispute is clear.

Key Terms
برئت إلي (bari'tu ila): A formula of release used by the creditor that includes the preposition 'ila', indicating a transfer of the debt from the surety to the creditor, implying payment.
برئت (bari'tu): A simple formula of release without 'ila', ambiguous between ibra' and payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if the creditor says to the surety 'I have released you' (برئت) without adding 'from the money' (إلي), does the surety have recourse against the principal?
A) Yes, because the surety is released.
B) No, because the wording is ambiguous and may refer to remission without payment.
C) Yes, because the debt is transferred.
D) No, because the surety must have written proof.

Key Takeaway
The surety's recourse depends on the clarity of the creditor's wording; ambiguous wording creates a valid ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

8 min
Effect of Discharge and Conditional Suspension in Kafalah
Ruling on guarantor's discharge and conditional suspension

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a creditor says 'abra'atuk' vs. writes a receipt, and explain the Hanafi position on the invalidity of suspending the guarantor's discharge.

Core Idea
Oral discharge is a waiver (no recourse); written discharge is an admission of receipt (recourse allowed).

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentaries delves into two key issues in suretyship (kafalah). First, what is the effect of the creditor saying 'I discharge you' or 'you are free from the debt'? Does the guarantor then have recourse against the principal debtor? The answer hinges on a subtle distinction between oral statements and written acknowledgments. Second, can the discharge itself be suspended on a future condition? The Hanafi school firmly rejects such suspension because discharge is akin to ownership transfer, which cannot be conditional. Understanding these nuances prevents common errors in fatwa regarding guarantor rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the creditor's statements
The creditor may say 'abra'atuk' (I discharge you) or 'anta fi hill' (you are free), or write a document stating 'the guarantor is free from the property.' Distinguish the wording and its legal effect.
Oral statements are considered pure waiver (isqat); writing is considered an admission of receipt (iqrar bi al-qabd).
2
Distinguish the consequence for recourse
If the creditor says 'abra'atuk', the guarantor is discharged but has no recourse against the principal. If he writes a receipt, the guarantor has recourse because writing is customarily done upon payment.
This difference is based on 'urf (custom).
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani distinguishes: writing indicates receipt (recourse), oral discharge is pure waiver (no recourse). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no recourse in both cases because both wordings are discharge.
The mufta bihi is the general ruling (no recourse) unless custom clearly indicates otherwise.
4
Understand the ruling on conditional discharge
If the creditor says 'When Zayd arrives, you are discharged', the discharge is invalid because it is a suspension (ta'liq) of a right that has the meaning of tamlik (ownership transfer). Tamlik cannot be suspended, unlike talaq or itq.
A contrary view (that it is valid) is rejected because the underlying right is only a claim (mutalaba), not the debt itself.
5
Apply the principle to analogous cases
The same ruling applies to hawala (assignment) as to kafalah. Also, discharge from debt (ibra' al-dayn) is different from discharge of guarantor because the former involves tamlik.
This step solidifies the distinction between isqat (waiver) and tamlik (ownership).

Or 'I have discharged you,' no, and the suspension of discharge from suretyship by a condition is invalid. Because 'I have discharged you' means from the property for which you stood surety for so-and-so (Ma'din: no, i.e., the guarantor does not have recourse against the principal in both situations ('Ayni). For this reason, if he wrote and said: 'The guarantor is free from the property,' it is an admission of receipt by consensus, and so is this. As for his saying 'I have discharged you,' by agreement, and likewise his saying 'You are free from the property,' he does not have recourse by consensus; because his saying 'I have discharged you' is a waiver from him, and the word 'free' is used for discharge by waiver, not for discharge by receipt. And Muhammad distinguished between the two statements; because custom is that when payment occurs, the creditor writes a document, so writing is made an admission, and there is no custom at the time of discharge because he does not write a document to him. And hawala (assignment) is like suretyship in this. ('Ayni, Fath, Kifaya) And the suspension of discharge is invalid, and it is said: valid; because what is established for the guarantor is the claim, not the debt according to the correct view, so it is a waiver like divorce and emancipation. For this reason, the guarantor's discharge is not rejected by refusal, because the waiver is perfected by the waiving party; unlike postponement for the guarantor, where it is rejected by refusal, because it is not a waiver but rather the pure right of the demander, so it is rejected by refusal; unlike discharge from a debt, because it contains the meaning of ownership. And in al-Diraya, the transmission of the book is carried upon when the condition is not customary, and its meaning is that there is no benefit for the claimant. (Fath) Suretyship by condition: such as the creditor saying to the guarantor, 'When Zayd arrives, you are the guarantor,' then he is not discharged. It is not valid; because in discharge there is a meaning of ownership like discharge from a debt, and ownership cannot be suspended by a condition. ('Ayni)
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an intra-Hanafi dispute. It addresses two issues: (1) the effect of the creditor saying 'abra'atuk' (I discharge you) to the guarantor, and (2) the validity of suspending the discharge on a condition. The ruling states that if the creditor says 'abra'atuk' or 'anta fi hill' (you are free), the guarantor is discharged from the debt but does not have recourse against the principal debtor. However, if the creditor writes a statement like 'the guarantor is free from the property,' it is considered an admission of receipt (qabd), so the guarantor does have recourse. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani distinguished between the two based on custom: writing typically occurs upon actual payment, so it indicates receipt; oral discharge is a waiver. The second ruling is that discharging the guarantor conditionally (e.g., 'if Zayd arrives, you are discharged') is invalid because discharge has the meaning of tamlik (ownership transfer), which cannot be suspended. A contrary view that it is valid is rejected because the underlying right is only a claim (mutalaba), not the debt itself, making it a pure waiver like talaq and itq that are complete upon utterance and cannot be conditionally suspended.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibra'): Discharge or waiver from a debt or claim.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of an effect upon a future condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the creditor says to the guarantor, 'I discharge you from the debt,' what is the ruling regarding the guarantor's right of recourse against the principal?
A) The guarantor has recourse because the debt is extinguished.
B) The guarantor does not have recourse because it is a waiver.
C) The guarantor has recourse only if the creditor writes it down.
D) The guarantor automatically has recourse regardless of the wording.

Key Takeaway
Oral discharge is a waiver blocking recourse; written discharge is an admission of receipt allowing recourse; conditional discharge is invalid because discharge contains the meaning of ownership transfer.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
10 min
Invalid Objects of Suretyship: A Hanafi Analysis
Ruling on invalid objects of suretyship with proof via talil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi taxonomy of objects to identify which objects can be validly guaranteed by suretyship, and explain why suretyship for hadd, qisas, sold item (valid sale), mortgaged item, and trust is invalid.

Core Idea
Suretyship is valid only for objects guaranteed by themselves; for objects guaranteed by something else, trusts, and corporal punishments, it is invalid.

In this lesson, we explore which objects cannot be the subject of a valid suretyship (kafala). The Hanafi school distinguishes between objects that are guaranteed by themselves (like an item sold in an invalid sale) and those guaranteed by something else (like a sold item before delivery, which is secured by the price). We will examine why suretyship for hadd penalties, qisas, a sold item in a valid sale, a mortgaged item, and trust items is invalid, and how the ruling changes when the subject is the person or the delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objects in question
List the objects and acts for which suretyship is ruled invalid: hadd, qisas, sold item (valid sale), mortgaged item, and trust items (deposit, borrowed item, etc.).
Also note the valid cases: suretyship for the person liable for hadd/qisas, and for delivery of a sold item.
2
Understand the two-fold classification of objects
Objects are either guaranteed (mamun) or trust (amana). Guaranteed objects are further divided into those guaranteed by themselves (mamun bi-nafsihi) and those guaranteed by something else (mamun bi-ghayrihi).
Examples: an item sold in an invalid sale is mamun bi-nafsihi; a sold item before delivery is mamun bi-ghayrihi (guaranteed by the price).
3
Apply the rule: suretyship for substance is valid only for mamun bi-nafsihi
Suretyship for the substance (dhat) is invalid for trusts and objects guaranteed by something else because the principal is not directly liable for the object. It is valid for objects guaranteed by themselves.
This rule distinguishes the Hanafi school from the Shafi'i, who do not permit suretyship for the substance of any object.
4
Recognize exceptions: suretyship for delivery and for the person
Suretyship for the delivery (taslim) of a sold item in a valid sale is valid because delivery is obligatory. Similarly, suretyship to surrender the person liable for hadd/qisas is valid because surrendering the person is itself obligatory.
In these cases, the subject of the guarantee is an act (delivery/surrender) that can be performed by a proxy, not the substance of the object.
5
Note the Shafi'i disagreement
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that suretyship for the substance of any object, even if guaranteed by itself, is invalid. The Hanafi school uses a broader definition of what can be guaranteed.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf rooted in different usul regarding the scope of kafala.

(عيني، فتح، عناية) وأمانة: أي وكذا تبطل بأمانة كالوديعة والمستعار ومال المضاربة والشركة والمستأجر. (ط، عيني)
And suretyship for a hadd penalty and blood-wit: (in the nominative as a conjunction to 'suspension of discharge,' i.e., suretyship is invalid ('Ayni)) i.e., suretyship for hadd and qisas is invalid; because suretyship is only valid for a guaranteed thing in which agency (niyaba) can be exercised for its fulfillment, and agency is not valid in corporal punishments; because the purpose of their legislation is to deter corrupters from corruption, so they cannot be carried out on someone other than the perpetrator; due to lack of benefit. (Tabiyin al-Haqaiq 44/5) This is if he guarantees the hadd or qisas itself. If he guarantees the person who is liable for hadd or qisas, it is valid; because the consequence can be arranged upon him; since surrendering the person (taslim al-nafs) is obligatory in both, the guarantor can be demanded for it, and validity is established. ('Ayni, Fath, Zaylai) And a sold item etc.: Know that physical objects (a'yan) regarding the permissibility of suretyship for them are divided into two categories: guaranteed (mamun) and trust (amana). Then the guaranteed is divided into what is guaranteed by something else (mamun bi-ghayrihi), like a sold item and a mortgaged item, and what is guaranteed by itself (mamun bi-nafsihi), like an item sold in an invalid sale and property received with intent to purchase. And trust items are like deposits, partnerships, companies, and loans for use. Suretyship for these things is either for their substance (dhawatiha) or for their delivery (taslimiha). If it is for their substance, then suretyship is invalid for what is a trust or guaranteed by something else, and it is valid for what is guaranteed by itself according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. On this basis, suretyship for a sold item in the hands of the seller in a valid sale is invalid; because before delivery it is not guaranteed by itself, such as the guarantor saying to the buyer, 'If the sold item perishes, I am liable for its substitute'; because it is an item guaranteed by something else, namely the price. = [PAGE_385] Book of Suretyship 384 Rulings of Suretyship. And a trust, and it is valid if for a price, usurped property, property received with intent to purchase, property sold in an invalid sale, and the load of a specific hired animal. i.e., suretyship on behalf of the buyer. And the intended meaning of suretyship for a sold item is suretyship for the sold item itself. And if he undertakes to deliver the sold item, it is valid in the correct sale; because it is possible, since delivery is obligatory upon the principal, so the meaning of suretyship is realized. Likewise, suretyship for a mortgaged item is invalid, whether he guarantees it for the mortgagor or for the mortgagee in the mortgagee's hand; because it is not guaranteed by itself but by something else, namely the debt. For if it perishes, the debt is extinguished, so it is not possible to impose liability on the guarantor, and it is not obligatory upon the principal. Likewise, suretyship is invalid for a trust, neither for its substance nor for its delivery; because it is not guaranteed against the principal, neither its substance nor its delivery. The obligation regarding it is to not prevent when demanded, not to deliver. ('Ayni, Fath, Inaya) And a trust: i.e., similarly it is invalid for a trust like a deposit, borrowed item, property of mudaraba, company, and hired item. (T, 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining which objects cannot be the subject of a valid kafala (suretyship). It first addresses suretyship for hadd (fixed corporal punishment) and qisas (retaliation): such suretyship is invalid because these penalties cannot be carried out by a proxy (niyaba); the purpose is deterrence of the actual offender. However, if one guarantees to surrender the person who is liable for the hadd or qisas, it is valid because surrendering the person is an obligation and can be performed by a guarantor. The passage then discusses suretyship for physical objects. Objects are divided into two categories: guaranteed (mamun) and trust (amana). Guaranteed objects are further divided into those guaranteed by something else (e.g., a sold item before delivery is guaranteed by the price; a mortgaged item is guaranteed by the debt) and those guaranteed by themselves (e.g., an item sold in an invalid sale, property received with intent to purchase). Suretyship for the substance of an object is invalid if the object is a trust or guaranteed by something else; it is valid if the object is guaranteed by itself (contrary to al-Shafi'i). Thus, suretyship for a sold item in a valid sale before delivery is invalid because the seller's liability is only secondary to the price. Similarly, suretyship for a mortgaged item is invalid regardless of whether it is for the benefit of the mortgagor or mortgagee, because the mortgage is merely security for the debt; if the item perishes, the debt is discharged, so any liability would be redundant and not established. Suretyship for a trust (e.g., deposit, borrowed item) is also invalid because the principal is not liable for the item itself; his duty is to not prevent the owner from taking it, not to deliver it or guarantee it.

Key Terms
مضمون (mamun): An object that is guaranteed, i.e., the possessor is liable for its value if it perishes.
أمانة (amana): A trust: an object held without liability for its loss, e.g., deposit, borrowed item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because hadd is too severe to be guaranteed.
B) Because hadd cannot be carried out by a proxy (niyaba).
C) Because hadd is only for specific crimes.
D) Because the guarantor would be punished instead.

Key Takeaway
Suretyship is invalid for objects that are not guaranteed by themselves; the key is whether the principal is liable for the object's substance.

Valid and Invalid Objects of Kafalah: The Guarantee Principle
Lists objects for which kafalah is valid or invalid with reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of dhaman (liability) to classify whether kafalah is valid for a given object.

Core Idea
Kafalah is valid only when the object is guaranteed upon the principal.

In this passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, the author enumerates the objects for which kafalah (suretyship) is valid and invalid according to the Hanafi school. The key is whether the object is 'madhmun' (guaranteed) upon the principal. If the principal is liable to compensate for the object, then it can be guaranteed by another; if not, the guarantee is meaningless. We will work through the classification and its underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object of kafalah
Determine what the guarantor is guaranteeing: a debt, a specific item, a service, etc.
The object must be clearly defined.
2
Determine if the object is guaranteed (madhmun) upon the principal
Consider whether the principal would be liable to pay compensation if the object perishes. If yes, the object is guaranteed; if not, it is a trust (amanah) or a service that cannot be delegated.
Guaranteed objects include thaman (price), maghsab (usurped item), maqbud 'ala sawm al-shira' (item taken with intent to purchase), and mabi' fasid (corruptly sold item). Non-guaranteed objects include amanah (deposit, loan for use), specific services, and the load of a specific beast.
3
Check if the guarantor is capable of performing
Even if the object is guaranteed, if the guarantor cannot possibly fulfill (e.g., loading a specific beast that may perish), the kafalah is invalid. If the object is unspecified (e.g., load on any beast), it is valid.
Capability is a condition for validity; the guarantor must be able to deliver the object or its equivalent.
4
State the ruling and reason
Conclude whether the kafalah is valid (sahih) or invalid (batil) and explain using the dhaman principle.
For guaranteed objects: sahih. For non-guaranteed or specific impossible objects: batil.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Test your understanding by classifying a novel object not mentioned in the text, using the same criteria.
Example: guaranteeing that someone will return a borrowed car (amanah) is invalid; guaranteeing payment for a sold car is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني، فتح) ومغصوباً أي وكذا يصح الكفالة إذا كان المكفول به مغصوبا؛ لأنه مضمون عليه بعينه. (عيني) ومقبوضاً : أي وكذا إذا كان المكفول به مقبوضاً على طلب الشراء؛ لأنه عليه. (عيني) ومبيعا: أي وكذا إذا كان المكفول به مبيعاً بيعا فاسداً؛ لأن المقبوض في البيع الفاسد مضمون مضمون عليه. (عيني) به من وحمل دابة :[بالجر عطف على قوله : " بحد" أي وبطل الكفالة بحمل دابة بعينها. (عيني)] أي لا تصح الكفالة بحمل دابة معينة؛ لأن الكفيل عاجز عن الحمل على الدابة المعينة؛ لأنه عاجز عن الحمل إليها بتقدير هلاكها، وإذا كانت غير معينة يجوز؛ لأنه قادر على ذلك بأن يتحمله على أي دابة شاء، والحمل هو المستحق عليه، ولو تكفل بتسليم الدابة فيما إذا كانت معينة جاز للقدرة عليه، وكذا لا تجوز الكفالة بخدمة عبد معين؛ لعدم قدرة الكفيل عليه على تقدير هلاكه. وفي "المبسوط": ولو تكارى دابة أو عبداً أو عجل الأجرة ولم يقبض العبد ولا الدابة، وكفل له كفيل بذلك حتى يدفعه إليه، فإن الكفيل يؤخذ ما دام حياً؛ لأن التسليم مستحق على الأصيل وهو مما تجري فيه النيابة فتصح الكفالة به فإن ملك المستأجر لم يكن له على الكفيل شيء؛ لأن الإجارة انفسخت وخرج الأصيل من أن يكون مطالباً بتسليم العين، وإنما عليه رد الأجرة، والكفيل ما كفل بالأجرة. (فتح) مستأجرة : بأن استأجرها رجل للحمل عليها فكفل رجل بالحمل فإنه لا يجوز. (عيني) وخدمة عبد استؤجر للخدمة أي وبطلت الكفالة أيضاً وخدمة أي وبطل الكفالة أيضاً بخدمة عبد معين. (عيني) للخدمة لعدم القدرة عليه على تقدير هلاكه. (عيني)
Book of Suretyship. Rulings of suretyship: And [invalid] with a trust, and valid if for a price, and a usurped item, and a thing taken with intent to purchase, and a corruptly sold item, and the load of a specific hired animal. And what is meant by suretyship for the sold item is suretyship for the sold item itself. And if he fails to deliver the sold item, it is valid in the correct view because it is possible; because delivery is obligatory upon the principal, so the meaning of suretyship is realized. Likewise, suretyship is not valid for a mortgaged item, whether he guarantees it to the mortgagor or the mortgagee in the hand of the trustee, because it is not guaranteed upon him in itself but by something else, which is the debt, for the debt falls if the item perishes, so imposing liability on the guarantor is not possible, and it is not obligatory upon the principal. Likewise, it is not valid for a trust not in its specific form, nor for its delivery, because it is not guaranteed upon the principal—neither its essence nor its delivery, for the obligation concerning it is not to prevent upon request, not delivery. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah) And a trust: i.e., likewise it is invalidated by a trust like a deposit, a borrowed item, the capital of mudarabah, partnership, and a leased item. (T, Ayni) And valid if for a price: i.e., it is permissible to guarantee if what is guaranteed is the price of the sold item, because it is a valid debt upon the buyer, so the meaning of suretyship is realized, unless the buyer is a minor under guardianship, then the guarantor is not bound following the principal. Likewise, suretyship is permissible if the guaranteed item is usurped, because it is guaranteed upon him in itself. Likewise, if it is taken with intent to purchase, because it is guaranteed upon him by its value, so if it perishes with him, liability is obligatory upon him, since the value stands in its place, so imposing it upon the guarantor is possible. Likewise, in the case where the sale is corrupt, because what is taken in a corrupt sale is guaranteed upon him in itself, so if it perishes with him, he must pay its value, not the price. And among things for which suretyship is not valid are intentional [acts], and among them are the dower and the ransom for khul', because these items are compensation for blood money for things that do not become void with the destruction of the item. (Ayni, Fath) And usurped: i.e., likewise suretyship is valid if the guaranteed item is usurped, because it is guaranteed upon him in its essence. (Ayni) And taken: i.e., likewise if the guaranteed item is taken with the intention of purchase, because it is upon him. (Ayni) And sold: i.e., likewise if the guaranteed item is sold corruptly, because what is taken in a corrupt sale is guaranteed, guaranteed upon him. (Ayni) And the load of a beast: [in the genitive, conjoined to his saying 'by a limit' i.e., and suretyship for the load of a specific beast is invalid. (Ayni)] i.e., suretyship is not valid for the load of a specific beast, because the guarantor is incapable of loading that specific beast, since he is unable to load it if it perishes. And if it is unspecified, it is valid, because he is able to do that by loading it on any beast he wishes, and the load is what is owed. And if he guarantees delivery of the beast when it is specific, it is valid due to ability. Likewise, suretyship for the service of a specific slave is not valid, due to the guarantor's inability over him if he perishes. And in al-Mabsut: If one hires a beast or a slave and pays the hire in advance but does not take possession of the slave or beast, and a guarantor guarantees for him that until he hands it over to him, then the guarantor is taken as long as he lives, because delivery is owed by the principal and it is something in which substitution is possible, so suretyship for it is valid. But if the lessor becomes owner, there is nothing for him against the guarantor, because the lease is annulled and the principal is no longer required to deliver the item; rather, he owes the return of the hire, and the guarantor only guaranteed the hire. (Fath) Hired: i.e., if a man hires a beast for loading and a man guarantees the load, it is not valid. (Ayni) And the service of a slave hired for service, i.e., suretyship is also invalidated for the service of a specific slave. (Ayni) For service due to inability over him if he perishes. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage from the Kitab al-Kafalah of Kanz al-Daqa'iq lists the objects for which kafalah (suretyship) is valid and invalid according to the Hanafi school. The passage begins by stating the general rule: kafalah is valid for a price (thaman), a usurped item (maghsab), an item taken with intent to purchase (maqbud 'ala sawm al-shira'), and a corruptly sold item (mabi' fasid), but invalid for a trust (amanah), the load of a specific hired beast, and the service of a specific hired slave. The commentator explains the reasoning: kafalah is valid only when the object is guaranteed (madhmun) upon the principal (asil) in itself, meaning that if it perishes, the principal must compensate. For example, the price of a sale is a debt owed by the buyer, so it is guaranteed. A usurped item and an item taken with intent to purchase are guaranteed by their value. A corruptly sold item is also guaranteed by its value. On the other hand, a trust like a deposit or borrowed item is not guaranteed upon the principal; the principal's obligation is simply not to prevent the owner from taking it, not to deliver it. Therefore, suretyship for the trust itself or its delivery is invalid. Similarly, the load of a specific beast is invalid because the guarantor cannot guarantee loading that specific beast if it perishes; but if the beast is unspecified, the guarantor can load any beast, so it is valid. The same applies to the service of a specific slave. The passage also includes a reference to al-Mabsut explaining that if someone hires a beast and pays in advance, and a person guarantees delivery of the hired item, the guarantor is liable as long as the hirer lives, because delivery is an obligation that can be delegated. However, if the lessor dies, the lease is annulled and the guarantor is not liable for the hire. This shows the nuance of the Hanafi position on kafalah.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where a person (kafil) guarantees an obligation owed by another (asil) to a third party (makful lahu).
الأمانة (al-amanah): A trust; property held by one person for another without liability for loss, such as deposits, loans for use, partnership capital.
المغصوب (al-maghsub): A usurped item; property taken wrongfully from its owner, which is guaranteed upon the usurper (ghasib) in itself.
المقبوض على سوم الشراء (al-maqbud 'ala sawm al-shira'): An item taken with the intent to purchase; the taker is liable for its value if it perishes before contract.
المبيع الفاسد (al-mabi' al-fasid): A corrupt sale; a sale that is invalid in essence but the item is taken; the buyer is liable for the value if it perishes.
الحمل (al-haml): The load; the cargo to be carried on an animal. Kafalah for the load of a specific beast is invalid because the guarantor cannot guarantee loading that specific beast if it perishes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is valid for kafalah (suretyship)?
A) A trust (amanah) like a deposit
B) The load of a specific hired beast
C) A usurped item (maghsab)
D) The service of a specific hired slave

Key Takeaway
The validity of kafalah depends on whether the object is guaranteed upon the principal; if not, the kafalah is void.

The Requirement of Acceptance in Kafalah
Dispute over acceptance in kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the requirement of acceptance in kafalah and explain the disagreement with Abu Yusuf and the three other imams.

Core Idea
Kafalah requires acceptance of the beneficiary because it is a disposition affecting his right.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the condition of acceptance (qabul) in kafalah. The majority Hanafi position requires the beneficiary's acceptance, but there is a notable disagreement with Abu Yusuf and the three other imams, who consider acceptance unnecessary. Understanding this dispute is essential for determining the validity of suretyship contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Examine the passage to determine what ruling is given for kafalah without acceptance. The text states that suretyship is invalid without acceptance. Identify the hukm level: it is a condition for validity.
The ruling is that kafalah is invalid (batil) without acceptance.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited by the Hanafi school. The text says: 'because it is a disposition concerning the right of the guaranteed party, so it is not valid except with his acceptance and consent.' This is a rational proof (dalil 'aqli) based on the nature of contracts.
The Hanafi dalil is that kafalah is a disposition (tasarruf) affecting the beneficiary's right, so his consent is necessary.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the dalil proves the ruling. Since kafalah transfers a liability to the guarantor, the beneficiary's right to be paid by the principal is affected. Thus, his acceptance is required for the contract to be valid.
The link is that a contract that alters rights requires the consent of the person whose rights are affected.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the opposing view. Abu Yusuf and the three imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that kafalah is valid without acceptance because it is a beneficial act for the beneficiary.
The dispute is whether acceptance is a condition (shart) or not.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Determine the mufta bihi position. The passage indicates that the relied-upon view is that acceptance is required, but it need not be in the same session; delayed acceptance suffices. This is the position adopted for fatwa.
The tarjih is based on the principle that rights cannot be transferred without consent.

And we restricted it to 'guarantee for me' because if the heir voluntarily guarantees what is upon him to the creditors in their absence, it is not valid, although analogy would reject it according to them, because the claimant is absent, and suretyship is not completed without his acceptance. Then Abu Qatada said: 'They are upon me, O Messenger of Allah,' and he prayed over him.' This fell [as proof]. The validity of suretyship requires the existence of the debt in terms of worldly rulings; suretyship means joining a liability to a liability, for one does not join the existent to the non-existent except that in legal judgment it is wealth because it returns to it, and he is unable to pay himself, and it is replaced by wealth and the guarantor. So the objective, which is recovery, is lost. And voluntary charity does not depend on the existence of the debt, because it acquits him in the Hereafter. (Ayni)] Its form: if a man appoints an agent to sell something, and the agent sells it, then he guarantees to the principal regarding the buyer, the suretyship is not valid, because the right to receive payment belongs to the agent, so he becomes a guarantor for himself. Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses the condition of acceptance (qabul) in kafalah and several special cases. The majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is that kafalah requires the acceptance of the beneficiary (makful lahu). The passage notes a dispute: Abu Yusuf and the three other imams (likely Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that kafalah is valid and binding without acceptance in the session, because it is a beneficial act for the beneficiary. However, the relied-upon position is that acceptance is required, but it need not be in the same session; delayed acceptance suffices. Next, an exception is made for a sick person's heir: if a sick person tells his heir to guarantee his debt, the guarantee is valid even without the creditors' acceptance, by istihsan (juristic preference). This is because it is treated as a will, and ignorance of the debt does not invalidate a will. The restriction to an heir is because a stranger's guarantee without acceptance is disputed. Then, the passage addresses kafalah for a deceased bankrupt: Abu Hanifa says it is invalid, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the three other imams say it is valid. Abu Hanifa argues that the debt is effectively fallen because payment from a deceased person is impossible in this world, so the guarantor is guaranteeing a non-existent debt. The disciples cite a hadith where Abu Qatada guaranteed the debt of a deceased and the Prophet prayed, implying validity. However, Abu Hanifa distinguishes that the guarantor's payment is a charity that acquits the deceased in the Hereafter, not a worldly obligation. Finally, the passage states that an agent's guarantee for the price to the principal is invalid because the agent has the right to receive payment, so he would be guaranteeing himself. This is a brief overview of the key disputes and rulings.

Key Terms
القبول (al-qabul): Acceptance; the consent of the beneficiary (makful lahu) for the suretyship to be valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, which of the following is true regarding acceptance in kafalah?
A) Acceptance is not required because it is beneficial.
B) Acceptance is required and must be given in the same session.
C) Acceptance is required but may be given after the session.
D) Acceptance is required only for suretyship for property, not for person.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah requires the beneficiary's acceptance, but it may be given after the contract session.

Special Cases in Kafalah: Heir, Deceased Bankrupt, and Agent
Special cases in kafalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the validity of kafalah in three special cases: a sick person's heir, a deceased bankrupt, and an agent, and apply the rule of istihsan.

Core Idea
Kafalah may be valid without acceptance in special cases like a sick person's heir by istihsan, but it is invalid for a deceased bankrupt according to Abu Hanifa.

After establishing the general rule requiring acceptance, the text presents exceptions and special cases. The first is a sick person instructing his heir to guarantee his debt, which is valid by istihsan even without the creditors' acceptance. The second is kafalah for a deceased bankrupt, where Abu Hanifa holds it invalid while the disciples and other imams consider it valid. The third is an agent's guarantee for the price, which is invalid because he would be guaranteeing himself. This lesson explores these cases to deepen understanding of how istihsan and reasoning shape Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three special cases
The passage discusses three distinct scenarios: (1) a sick person telling his heir to guarantee his debts, (2) a guarantor for a deceased bankrupt, and (3) an agent guaranteeing the price to his principal.
Each case tests the boundaries of the acceptance requirement.
2
Analyze the heir case: istihsan over qiyas
The ruling that the heir's guarantee is valid despite the creditors' absence is based on istihsan (juristic preference). Qiyas would require acceptance, but istihsan treats it as a will (wasiyyah), which does not require acceptance.
The key is that the sick person's instruction to his heir is considered a will, and wills are valid even without the beneficiaries' knowledge.
3
Analyze the deceased bankrupt case: Abu Hanifa vs. the disciples
Abu Hanifa invalidates the guarantee because the debt is considered fallen for worldly purposes; the disciples and other imams hold it valid because death does not extinguish debt. The hadith of Abu Qatada is cited but interpreted differently.
The dispute hinges on whether the debt remains legally existent after death.
4
Analyze the agent case: self-guarantee
An agent who guarantees the price to his principal is guaranteeing himself because the right to receive payment belongs to the agent. Thus, the guarantee is invalid.
The agent cannot be both guarantor and guaranteed party simultaneously.
5
Determine the mufta bihi for each case
For the heir case: valid by istihsan (mufta bihi). For the deceased bankrupt: fatwa is on the disciples' view (valid). For the agent: invalid.
The later Hanafi practice follows the disciples in the bankrupt case.

And without acceptance of the claimant in the session of contract, unless he guarantees at both parties i.e., suretyship of both types is invalid without acceptance, etc. (Ayni) At both parties, because it is a disposition concerning the right of the guaranteed party, so it is not valid except with his acceptance and consent. Abu Yusuf said: It is valid and binding, because the presence of the guaranteed person and the principal are not conditions, so the presence of the guaranteed party is not a condition, and the three [imams] said the same. The dispute is the same for suretyship for the person and for property. And it is said that according to him acceptance is required but it is not required in the session; rather, if the news reaches him after leaving the session and he accepts, it is valid. (Miftah al-Haqa'iq 2:104, Fath) Except if he guarantees: its form is that a sick person says to his heir: 'Guarantee for me what I owe in debt to my creditors,' and he guarantees it in the absence of the creditors; it is valid by istihsan. And we restricted it to 'guarantee for me' because if the heir voluntarily guarantees what is upon him to the creditors in their absence, it is not valid, although analogy would reject it according to them, because the claimant is absent, and suretyship is not completed without his acceptance. The basis of istihsan is that this is a will from him to his heirs to pay his debt, therefore it is valid even if the sick person did not specify the debt and his creditors, because ignorance does not prevent the validity of a will. And it is restricted to the heir because if he said to a stranger: 'Guarantee' and the stranger guaranteed his debt, it is said it is valid because the sick person intended benefit for himself, and if the stranger pays his debt by his order, he can recover from his estate. And it is said it is not valid because the stranger is not required to pay his debt without a commitment, so the sick person's right and the validity are the same, and validity is more apparent. (Ayni, Fath) The sick person: i.e., for the sick person for what he owes in debt to his creditors in their absence and without their acceptance, it is valid by istihsan. (T, Ayni) And for a deceased bankrupt: i.e., if a debtor dies insolvent and a man guarantees for him, the suretyship is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. The two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said it is valid, because it is suretyship for a pre-existing debt, since death does not cause it to fall. And the three [imams] said the same. And it is narrated that 'A funeral of a man from the Ansar was brought, and he asked: 'Does he have any debt?' They said: 'Yes, one or two dinars.' So he refrained from praying. Then Abu Qatada said: 'They are upon me, O Messenger of Allah,' and he prayed over him.' This fell [as proof]. To validate verification: And his [Abu Hanifa's] argument is that he guaranteed a fallen debt, because debt is an act; it is said: debt became obligatory upon him meaning its payment, and payment is not conceivable from the deceased whether he has wealth or not in terms of worldly rulings. The validity of suretyship requires the existence of the debt in terms of worldly rulings; suretyship means joining a liability to a liability, for one does not join the existent to the non-existent except that in legal judgment it is wealth because it returns to it, and he is unable to pay himself, and it is replaced by wealth and the guarantor. So the objective, which is recovery, is lost. And voluntary charity does not depend on the existence of the debt, because it acquits him in the Hereafter. (Ayni, Fath) For the price to the principal: [i.e., and the agent's suretyship for the price is invalid. (Ayni)] Its form: if a man appoints an agent to sell something, and the agent sells it, then he guarantees to the principal regarding the buyer, the suretyship is not valid, because the right to receive payment belongs to the agent, so he becomes a guarantor for himself. And according to the three -
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses the condition of acceptance (qabul) in kafalah and several special cases. The majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is that kafalah requires the acceptance of the beneficiary (makful lahu). The passage notes a dispute: Abu Yusuf and the three other imams (likely Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that kafalah is valid and binding without acceptance in the session, because it is a beneficial act for the beneficiary. However, the relied-upon position is that acceptance is required, but it need not be in the same session; delayed acceptance suffices. Next, an exception is made for a sick person's heir: if a sick person tells his heir to guarantee his debt, the guarantee is valid even without the creditors' acceptance, by istihsan (juristic preference). This is because it is treated as a will, and ignorance of the debt does not invalidate a will. The restriction to an heir is because a stranger's guarantee without acceptance is disputed. Then, the passage addresses kafalah for a deceased bankrupt: Abu Hanifa says it is invalid, while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the three other imams say it is valid. Abu Hanifa argues that the debt is effectively fallen because payment from a deceased person is impossible in this world, so the guarantor is guaranteeing a non-existent debt. The disciples cite a hadith where Abu Qatada guaranteed the debt of a deceased and the Prophet prayed, implying validity. However, Abu Hanifa distinguishes that the guarantor's payment is a charity that acquits the deceased in the Hereafter, not a worldly obligation. Finally, the passage states that an agent's guarantee for the price to the principal is invalid because the agent has the right to receive payment, so he would be guaranteeing himself. This is a brief overview of the key disputes and rulings.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling.
المريض (al-marid): The sick person; a person on his deathbed whose statements may be considered a will.
الميت المفلس (al-mayyit al-muflis): A deceased person who dies insolvent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is invalid because the creditors are absent.
B) It is valid by istihsan, treating it as a will.
C) It is valid only if the heir is also a creditor.
D) It is invalid unless the sick person specifies the debt amount.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan permits an heir's guarantee for a sick person's debt without creditor acceptance, treating it as a will.

Kafala of a Partner in a Joint Sale
Ruling on invalidity of partner's suretyship for share in joint sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a partner's suretyship in a single joint sale is invalid and distinguish the case of separate sales.

Core Idea
A guarantee that divides an undivided debt before receipt is invalid.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a subtle point in the law of suretyship (kafala) when partners sell jointly owned property. You will learn why one partner cannot guarantee the other's share of the price in a single transaction, and how a separate sale avoids the problem.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the kafala (suretyship) of one partner for the other partner's share of the price in a joint sale. The ruling is that it is invalid (batil).
The matn states: '...la yajuz' (not permissible).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas: the kafala leads to dividing the debt before receipt (qismat al-dayn qabl al-qabd), which is forbidden by analogy to the prohibition of selling a debt before receipt.
No explicit text, but the 'illa is clear.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
In a single joint sale, every payment made by the buyer is proportionately owned by both partners. Guaranteeing one partner's share effectively splits the undivided debt before the buyer pays it fully, which is the prohibited act.
The key phrase: 'ما من جزء يؤديه المشتري من الثمن إلا ولشريكه فيه نصيب'
4
Note the ikhtilaf
This ruling is mukhtalaf fih; other schools may have different views on dividing debt before receipt, but the Hanafi school holds it invalid.
5
Evaluate the exception
If the partners sell in two separate transactions (each sells his share independently), the shares are distinct. Then the guarantee is valid because each partner's share is a separate debt.
The exception is stated: 'بخلاف ما إذا باعه صفقتين'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللشريك إذا بيع عبد صفقة، أي في عقد واحد. [شرح: أي وتبطل كفالة الشريك بالثمن للشريك الآخر.] أي إذا باع رجلان عبداً مشتركاً بينهما من رجل صفقة واحدة وضمن أحدهما لشريكه نصيبه من الثمن، لا يجوز؛ لأنه ما من جزء يؤديه المشتري من الثمن إلا ولشريكه فيه نصيب؛ ولأنه يؤدي إلى قسمة الدين قبل القبض وأنه لا يجوز، بخلاف ما إذا باعه صفقتين أي باع كل منهما حصته بعقد ثم ضمن أحدهما للآخر؛ لأن نصيب كل واحد منهما ممتاز عن نصيب الآخر من الثمن، حيث لا شركة. رمز الحقائق: ١٠٤/٢](فتح)
And for the partner: if a slave is sold in one transaction, meaning in a single contract. [Explanation: the suretyship of one partner for the other's share of the price is invalid.] That is, if two men sell a jointly owned slave to a man in a single transaction and one of them guarantees the other's share of the price, it is not permissible, because there is no part of the price that the buyer pays except that the other partner has a share in it; and because it leads to dividing the debt before receipt, which is not permissible. Unlike if they sold it in two transactions, i.e., each sold his share in a separate contract, then one guarantees the other, because each one's share is distinct from the other's share of the price, with no partnership.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explaining the invalidity of a partner's kafala (suretyship) in a joint sale. The original matn states: 'And the partner, if a slave is sold in one transaction...' The commentator clarifies: the suretyship of one partner for the other's share is invalid. The reasoning is twofold: first, every portion of the price paid by the buyer belongs proportionately to both partners, so the guarantee involves an undefined debt; second, it entails dividing a debt before it is received (qismat al-dayn qabl al-qabd), which is prohibited in the Hanafi school. The exception: if the partners sell in two separate transactions (each sells his share independently), then the shares are distinct and the guarantee is valid. The evidence is based on the legal principle that a debt cannot be divided before possession, established by qiyas (analogy) on the prohibition of selling a debt before receipt.

Key Terms
صفقة واحدة (safqah wahidah): A single transaction or contract covering multiple items or shares.
قسمة الدين قبل القبض (qismat al-dayn qabl al-qabd): Dividing a debt that is still owed before it is actually received.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is the kafala (suretyship) of one partner for another partner's share of the price valid?
A) When the jointly owned item is sold in one single transaction.
B) When the jointly owned item is sold in two separate transactions.
C) It is always invalid.
D) It is always valid.

Key Takeaway
A partner's suretyship in a joint sale is invalid because it splits an undivided debt before receipt, but it is valid if the shares are sold separately.

Kafala bi al-'Uhda Invalid Due to Ambiguity
Ruling and explanation of invalidity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that kafala bi al-'uhda is invalid and explain why the term 'uhda causes fatal uncertainty (jahala).

Core Idea
A guarantee is void if its subject is ambiguous—'uhda has multiple meanings causing jahala.

In this lesson, we examine a short but important ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: the invalidity of kafala bi al-'uhda. The text explains that this form of guarantee is not permissible because the term 'uhda is shared (mushtarak) among several meanings without specification. This ruling illustrates the Hanafi insistence on clarity in the subject matter of contracts, especially in guarantees (kafalah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is kafala bi al-'uhda (guarantee of warranty). The ruling is that it is invalid (batil).
The text opens with the ruling and then explains the scenario.
2
Identify the reason for invalidity
The reason given is jahala (uncertainty). The text explicitly states 'fala tajuz hadhihi al-kafala li al-jahala'.
Jahala here arises from the ambiguity of the term 'uhda.
3
Analyze the term 'uhda as mushtarak
The text lists multiple meanings of 'uhda: the old document, the contract, rights of the contract, liability (darak), and the option of stipulation. Because it is shared, without specification it is impossible to know what is being guaranteed.
The hadith 'al-'uhda thalatha ayyam' is used to illustrate one meaning (khiyar al-shart).
4
Understand the consequence of jahala
Uncertainty in the subject matter of a guarantee makes the contract void (batil). The guarantee is not merely voidable; it has no legal effect.
Hanafi principle: kafala requires a known, established obligation.
5
Apply the ruling to similar cases
Any guarantee using an ambiguous term without clarification falls under this ruling. The parties must specify what exactly is guaranteed to avoid jahala.
This connects to the broader principle that contracts must be free from gharar (uncertainty).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالعهدة: أي ولا تصح الكفالة بالعهدة، وصورتها: أن يشتري عبداً من رجل مثلاً فيضمن للمشتري رجل بالعهدة وسكتا عن ذلك ولم يبينا ما هي؟ فلا تجوز هذه الكفالة للجهالة؛ لأن العهدة اسم مشترك يقع على الصك القديم ويطلق على العقد؛ لأنه مأخوذ من العهد والعقد والعهد واحد ويطلق على حقوق العقد؛ ثمرات العقد، وعلى الدرك، وعلى خيار الشرط، ففي الخبر عهدة الرقيق ثلاثة أيام أي خيار الشرط فيه، فتعذر العمل بها قبل البيان، فبطل الضمان للجهالة. رمز الحقائق: ١٠٤/٢](فتح)
And regarding 'uhda: the kafala for 'uhda is not valid. Its form: for example, a person buys a slave from a man, and another man guarantees the buyer for the 'uhda, and they remain silent about it and do not clarify what it is. This kafala is not permissible due to jahala (uncertainty), because 'uhda is a shared noun that applies to the old document, and is used for the contract, because it is derived from 'ahd and 'aqd which are the same. It also applies to the rights of the contract, its fruits, to liability (darak), and to the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart). In the hadith, 'the 'uhda of the slave is three days' meaning the option of stipulation. Therefore, it is impossible to act upon it before clarification, so the guarantee is void due to uncertainty.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling that kafala bi al-'uhda (guarantee of warranty or liability) is invalid. The reason is jahala (uncertainty) because the word 'uhda is ambiguous (mushtarak) with multiple meanings: it can refer to the old sale deed, the contract itself, the rights and obligations arising from the contract, the liability for defects (darak), or the option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart). The hadith 'the 'uhda of the slave is three days' refers to the option period. Since the parties did not specify which meaning is intended, the guarantee is void due to uncertainty in the obligation. The Hanafi ruling is based on the principle that kafala requires a clear and known debt or obligation.

Key Terms
العهدة (al-'uhda): A term with multiple possible meanings including the contract, its rights, liability, and option conditions; used here generically.
جهالة (jahala): Uncertainty or ignorance about a material term, rendering a contract invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is kafala bi al-'uhda invalid?
A) Because 'uhda is forbidden in the Quran.
B) Because the term 'uhda is ambiguous and creates uncertainty.
C) Because it involves a debt that is not known.
D) Because the hadith requires a three-day option.

Key Takeaway
Kafala bi al-'uhda is invalid due to ambiguity in the term 'uhda; clarity of subject matter is essential for a valid guarantee.

Kafala bi al-Khalas: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Guarantee of Delivery
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of kafala bi al-khalas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on kafala bi al-khalas, identify the mufta bihi position, and apply the ruling to a practical scenario.

Core Idea
Kafala bi al-khalas: valid if khalas means delivery or compensation; invalid if only delivery.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the validity of a guarantee to free a sold item from a third-party claim (kafala bi al-khalas). The dispute hinges on the interpretation of the term 'khalas' – does it mean only physical delivery, or does it include the option of refunding the price? Understanding this nuance clarifies when such a guarantee is enforceable.

Guided Path
1
State the legal issue
Identify the specific type of guarantee (kafala bi al-khalas) and the point of disagreement: whether it is a valid contract of kafala.
The guarantee is given by a surety to the buyer that a purchased item will be freed from any prior claim.
2
Identify the two Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa holds it invalid; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it valid. Note the reasoning for each.
Abu Hanifa: khalas = only delivery, impossible to guarantee. Sahibayn: khalas = delivery or refund, possible.
3
Trace the linguistic dispute
Examine how the definition of 'khalas' drives the ruling. Abu Hanifa restricts it; Sahibayn expand it.
The Sahibayn's interpretation makes the guarantee equivalent to daman al-darak (liability guarantee), which is accepted.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, ambiguous 'khalas' makes the guarantee void; under Sahibayn, it is valid.
Also note the consensus point: if explicitly worded as 'delivery or refund', it is valid by all.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify which position is relied upon for fatwa, and why. The text supports the Sahibayn's view.
Majority of Hanafi scholars and other madhahib agree with the Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخلاص: [أي وبطلت الكفالة بتخليص المبيع عند الاستحقاق؛ لعجزه عنه.] أي ولا تصح الكفالة بالخلاص عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا تجوز. والخلاف مبني على تفسير الخلاص، فهما فسراه بتخليص المبيع إن قدر عليه، ورد الثمن إن لم يقدر، وهذا ضمان الدرك في المعنى، وبه قالت الثلاثة، وفسره الإمام بتخليص المبيع فقط ولا قدرة له عليه. ولو ضمن تخليص المبيع أو رد الثمن صح إجماعاً؛ لأنه ضمن ما يمكنه الوفاء به، وهو تسليم المبيع إن أجاز المستحق البيع ورد الثمن إن لم يجز. رمز الحقائق: ١٠٥/٢](فتح)
And regarding khalas: [the kafala for delivering the sold item at the time of claim is invalid because he is incapable of it.] That is, kafala for khalas is not valid according to Abu Hanifa, but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is permissible. The difference is based on the interpretation of khalas. They (the two) interpret it as delivering the sold item if possible, and returning the price if not possible; this is effectively guarnatee of darak (liability), and the majority hold this view. The Imam (Abu Hanifa) interpreted it as only delivering the sold item, and he has no capacity for that. If he guarantees delivering the sold item or returning the price, it is valid by consensus, because he guarantees what he can fulfill: handing over the sold item if the claimant validates the sale, and returning the price if he does not.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of kafala bi al-khalas (guarantee to free the purchased item from a third-party claim). Abu Hanifa rules it invalid because he interprets khalas as only specific performance (delivering the item), which the guarantor cannot guarantee if the item is not in his possession. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) interpret khalas more broadly: the guarantor either delivers the item if possible, or refunds the price if not. This meaning of khalas is essentially a guarantee of darak (liability), which is valid. The three Imams (including Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad) agree with the Sahibayn. The text notes that if the parties explicitly guarantee either delivery or refund of the price, it is valid by consensus. The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is that under Abu Hanifa's view, such a guarantee is invalid unless the terms are specified; under the Sahibayn's view, the ambiguous phrase 'khalas' is sufficient because it implicitly includes both options.

Key Terms
الخلاص (al-khalas): Freeing the sold item from a third-party claim. Dispute over scope: delivery only vs. delivery or compensation.
ضمان الدرك (daman al-darak): Guarantee of liability for defects or eviction; the guarantor compensates if the item is lost or claimed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is kafala bi al-khalas valid by consensus according to the text?
A) When the guarantor explicitly guarantees both delivery and refund of the price.
B) When the guarantor is the seller himself.
C) When the item is movable property.
D) When the guarantor is a relative of the buyer.

Key Takeaway
The validity of kafala bi al-khalas in Hanafi fiqh depends on interpreting khalas as either delivery-only (Abu Hanifa) or delivery-or-refund (Sahibayn); the mufta bihi is the latter.
