Kafala bi Mal al-Kitaba Invalid
Ruling on invalidity of kafala for kitaba debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on kafala for kitaba debt, explain the reasoning (ta'lil) based on debt stability, and distinguish between valid and invalid suretyship cases involving a mukatib.

Core Idea
Kafala for kitaba debt is invalid because the debt is unstable and may be dropped.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh regarding suretyship (kafala) for the debt owed by a mukatib (a slave under contract of manumission). The source text clearly states that such a guarantee is invalid due to the inherent instability of the kitaba obligation. Understanding the reasoning (ta'lil) behind this ruling helps clarify the broader Hanafi principle that a valid subject of kafala must be a stable, established debt. Additionally, the text distinguishes the kitaba debt from other debts of the mukatib (e.g., a regular loan), for which kafala is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is kafala (suretyship) for the kitaba debt (mal al-kitaba). The ruling is that it is invalid (batil) in the Hanafi school.
Ensure you understand the terms: kafala = guarantee, kitaba = contract of manumission by payment, mukatib = slave under that contract.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The invalidity is because the kitaba debt is not a valid (stable) debt. It is on the verge of being dropped if the mukatib declares his inability to pay. Therefore, it cannot be established against the surety.
Stability of the debt is a key condition for the validity of kafala.
3
Distinguish from valid cases
The text notes two valid cases: (1) kafala for the price of freedom (badal al-‘itq) of a free person – that debt is stable because it is owed after freedom; (2) kafala for a separate debt owed by the mukatib (e.g., a loan) – that is also a stable debt independent of the kitaba contract.
The invalidity is specific to the kitaba debt itself, not all debts of the mukatib.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
A rare narration from Imam Ahmad (Hanbali) reportedly allows kafala for kitaba debt. The Hanafi school does not rely on this view.
Knowing the disagreement helps understand that the ruling is based on the stability principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومال الكتابة: أي ولا تصح الكفالة بمال الكتابة لعدم كونه ديناً صحيحاً؛ لأنه على شرف السقوط بتعجيزه نفسه، فلا يمكن إثباته على الكفيل. إلا في رواية شاذة عن أحمد أنه قال: يصح، وقيد بالكتابة؛ لأن بدل العتق تجوز الكفالة به؛ لأنه دين وجب عليه بعد الحرية، فلا يؤدي إلى التنافي. وكذا إذا كان على المكاتب دين لرجل فكفل به إنسان يجوز. (عيني، فتح)
And regarding the kitaba money: the kafala for the kitaba money is not valid because it is not a valid debt, since it is on the verge of being dropped by the mukatib declaring his incapacity. Therefore it cannot be established against the surety. Except in a rare narration from Ahmad that he said it is valid. It is qualified by kitaba: because the price of freedom (badal al-‘itq) – kafala for it is valid, as it is a debt owed after freedom, so it does not lead to inconsistency. Similarly, if a mukatib owes a debt to a man and a person guarantees it, that is valid.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage rules that kafala (suretyship) for the kitaba debt (money owed by a mukatib – a slave under contract of manumission) is invalid. The reason is that the kitaba debt is not a valid (stable) debt because the mukatib can terminate it by declaring his inability to pay, causing the debt to drop. Therefore, it cannot be the subject of a binding guarantee. The text notes an exception: a rare report from Imam Ahmad (Hanbali) allows it. The ruling is specifically about the kitaba debt; the price of emancipation (badal al-‘itq) owed by a free person is valid for kafala because it is a stable debt. Also, if the mukatib owes a separate debt to someone (e.g., a loan), kafala for that debt is valid. The distinction is based on the stability of the obligation.

Key Terms
مال الكتابة (mal al-kitaba): The money a mukatib owes to his master under a contract of kitaba (manumission by payment).
المكاتب (al-mukatib): A slave who has entered a kitaba contract to earn freedom by paying installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A man guarantees the kitaba debt of a mukatib.
B) A man guarantees a regular loan owed by a mukatib.
C) A man guarantees the price of freedom of a free man.
D) Both B and C are valid.

Key Takeaway
Kafala for the kitaba debt is invalid because it is not a stable debt; other debts of the mukatib can be guaranteed.

Kafalah — Surety

When the Principal Gives the Surety the Guaranteed Amount: Reclaiming Rules
Ruling with analogy to early zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a principal debtor giving money to the surety before debt payment, identify the exceptions, and apply the qiyas to early zakat.

Core Idea
Once given, the principal cannot reclaim because the surety's right attaches, like early zakat.

When a principal debtor gives the surety the guaranteed amount before the surety pays the creditor, a peculiar situation arises: can the principal take it back? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling, its rationale via qiyas to early zakat, and the important exceptions where reclaiming becomes permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
From the passage, the general rule is that the principal cannot reclaim the money given to the surety before the surety pays the debt.
This is the starting point for all analysis.
2
Understand the Rationale
The reason is that the surety's right (and indirectly the creditor's) attaches to the money because of the expectation that the surety will pay. This is a legal attachment (ta'alluq al-haqq).
The text explicitly says: 'because the right of the recipient is attached to it in anticipation of his paying the debt.'
3
Apply the Qiyas
The ruling is analogized to paying zakat early to the collector. Once paid, it cannot be reclaimed. Identify the effective cause (illa): the attachment of a right based on expectation of payment.
The qiyas is jali (clear) because both cases share a clear common cause.
4
Recognize Exceptions
Three exceptions: (1) if the principal pays the debt himself before the surety, he can reclaim; (2) if the creditor defers the debt to either party, the principal can reclaim; (3) if the money was given as an agency (the surety is a trustee), the principal still cannot reclaim until the debt is paid, but the surety's ownership does not transfer.
Each exception removes the expectation that the surety will pay, thus dissolving the right attachment.
5
Generalize to New Cases
Apply the same principle to any case where the surety holds the money in anticipation of paying a debt. The key is whether the possibility of the surety's payment remains.
For example, if the surety dies before paying, the principal might reclaim? (not in text, but analogous).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في بيان المسائل المتفرقة من جنس هذا الباب. (عيني) لا يسترد منه [أي المكفول عنه من الكفيل] فإن أداه بنفسه قبل أداء الكفيل يسترد منه ما أخذ. (ط) يعني إذا أعطى المكفول عنه (المطلوب) من الكفيل ما ضمن مما لا يتعين كالدراهم أو مما يتعين كالحنطة قبل أن يقضي الكفيل دينه لا يسترد المكفول عنه من الكفيل ما أعطاه؛ لأنه تعلق به حق القابض على احتمال قضائه الدين، فلا يسترد ما دام هذا الاحتمال باقياً، كمن عجل الزكاة ودفعها إلى الساعي. وعدم الاسترداد مقيد بما إذا لم يؤخره الطالب عن الأصيل أو الكفيل، فإن أخر الطالب الدين له أن يسترده. وإذا كان إعطاء الأصيل على وجه الرسالة بأن قال للكفيل: خذ هذا المال وادفعه إلى الطالب، لا يصير المؤدى ملكاً للكفيل، بل أمانة في يده، ولكن لا يكون للأصيل أن يسترده من الكفيل؛ لأنه تعلق بالمؤدى حق الطالب، والمطلوب بالاسترداد يريد إبطاله، فلا يمكن منه ما لم يقض دينه. (عيني، فتح)
Chapter: On miscellaneous issues of this chapter. He cannot reclaim from him [i.e., the principal from the surety]; if he pays (the debt) himself before the surety pays, he can reclaim what he gave. (T) Meaning: if the principal gives to the surety what he guaranteed, whether fungible (like dirhams) or non-fungible (like wheat), before the surety pays his debt, the principal cannot reclaim from the surety what he gave, because the right of the recipient (the surety) is attached to it in anticipation of his paying the debt, so he cannot reclaim as long as this possibility exists, like one who pays zakat early to the collector. The inability to reclaim is conditional on the creditor not having given a deferment to the principal or the surety. If the creditor defers the debt, the principal can reclaim it. If the principal gives the money as an agency (by saying: take this money and pay it to the creditor), then the given money does not become the property of the surety but is a trust in his hand, yet the principal cannot reclaim it from the surety because the right of the creditor has attached to it, and the person seeking to reclaim wants to invalidate that right, so he cannot do so until he pays the debt.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive ruling from the 'miscellaneous issues' section on transactions between the principal debtor (matalub/makful 'anhu) and the surety (kafil). The general rule: if the principal gives money (specifically the guaranteed amount) to the surety before the surety pays the creditor, the principal cannot subsequently reclaim that money from the surety. This is because the surety now holds the money in anticipation of paying the creditor, and a right of the surety (and indirectly of the creditor) attaches to that money. The analogy is drawn to paying zakat early to the collector – once paid, it cannot be reclaimed. However, there are exceptions: (1) If the principal himself pays the debt before the surety pays, the principal can reclaim from the surety the money he gave (since the surety no longer needs it). (2) If the creditor grants a deferral to either party, the principal can reclaim the money. (3) If the principal gave the money to the surety as an agent (wakala) – instructing him to pay the creditor – then the surety holds it as a trust, not as owner. Yet even then, the principal cannot reclaim it from the surety because the creditor's right has attached to that money; reclaiming would mean voiding that right. The principal must wait until the debt is paid.

Key Terms
المطلوب (al-matlub): The principal debtor or the person who is the subject of the guarantee (the 'demanded' party).
المكفول عنه (al-makful 'anhu): Same as al-matlub: the principal debtor.
الكفيل (al-kafil): The surety or guarantor.
الرسالة (al-risalah): Agency; the principal instructs the surety to pay on his behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can the principal debtor reclaim the money he gave to the surety before the surety paid the creditor?
A) Never, once given.
B) Only if the surety has paid the debt.
C) If the principal himself pays the debt before the surety.
D) If the surety dies.

Key Takeaway
The principal cannot reclaim money given to the surety as long as the surety's obligation to pay remains; exceptions apply only when the debt is discharged by the principal or the creditor defers.

Profit Earned by Surety on Principal's Money
Ruling on profit earned by surety on money received before paying debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on profit earned by a surety on the principal's money and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A surety's profit on money from the principal is halal; Abu Hanifa recommends return if principal pays, Sahibayn say fully lawful.

This passage addresses a subtle kafalah issue: what happens to profit earned by the surety on funds he received from the principal before settling the debt? The Hanafi school is internally divided – Abu Hanifa sees a taint, while the Sahibayn consider the profit fully lawful. Understanding this dispute clarifies when a surety may keep such profit and when piety recommends returning it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act
The act is earning profit on money received by the surety from the principal before paying the debt. This is a kafalah-related transaction.
Focus on the surety's receipt of money for debt repayment (qada).
2
State the common ground
Both Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree that the surety owns the money upon receipt for qada, so the profit is initially halal. The surety does not need to give it in charity.
This is the starting point before the dispute on khubth.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's position on khubth
Abu Hanifa holds that if the principal later pays the debt, there is a taint (khubth) in the profit, especially if the received money was specific (like wheat). He recommends returning the profit to the principal or giving it in charity.
The taint arises because the principal's payment retroactively makes the profit earned on effectively his money.
4
Examine the Sahibayn's counter-position
The Sahibayn argue that ownership is absolute, so no taint exists; the profit is fully halal and the surety may keep it without any recommended action.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the Sahibayn's view, as cited in Fath al-Qadir.
5
Note the practical difference and nuance
The only practical difference is whether a pious surety should return/charity the profit when the principal pays for specific items. Under the mufta bihi, it is not required.
Additionally, if the money was received as an agency (risala), profit is never lawful by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ربح الكفيل: [من المال الذي قبضه من المطلوب قبل أن يعطي الدين. (عيني)] يعني طاب للكفيل ما ربح في المال الذي قبضه من المطلوب قبل أن يعطي هو للطالب؛ لأنه ملكه بالقبض فكان الربح بدل ملكه ولا يتصدق به، سواء قضى الدين هو أو الأصيل. إلا أنه في الربح الحاصل للكفيل نوع خبث إذا قضى الأصيل الدين عند أبي حنيفة؛ لكنه لا يظهر مع الملك فيما لا يتعين كالدراهم. وإن قضى الكفيل فلا خبث فيه بالإجماع. هذا إذا قبضه على وجه اقتضاء الدين، وإن قبضه على وجه الرسالة لا يطيب له الربح على قولهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف، وأصل الخلاف في الربح بالدراهم المغصوبة. [رمز الحقائق: ١٠٥/٢] (فتح) وندب ردّه أي استحب رد الربح على الأصيل لو كان المقبوض مما يتعين، وهذا إذا قضى الأصيل الدين؛ لأن الخبث لحق الأصيل فإذا رده إليه وصل إليه حقه، وهو قول أبي حنيفة. وعنه أنه يتصدق به. وقالا: يطيب له الربح، وهو رواية عنه؛ لأنه ملكه بالقبض وربح في ملكه فيسلم له. وأن الخبث تمكن مع الملك فيما يتعين، فيتصدق به في رواية على الفقراء، وفي رواية: يرده على الأصيل؛ ثم إن كان الأصيل فقيراً يطيب له، وإن كان غنياً ففيه روايتان والأشبه أن يطيب له. هذا إذا أعطاه على وجه القضاء لدينه، وإن أعطاه على وجه الرسالة، لا يطيب له الربح بالاتفاق؛ لأنه لا يملكه، سواء كان المدفوع مما يتعين أو لا. [رمز الحقائق: ١٠٦/٢] (فتح) لو شيئاً: أي لو كان الذي آخذ منه. (عيني) يتعين كالحنطة والشعير، وهذا إذا قضى الأصيل الدين. (عيني)
And what the surety profits: [from the money he received from the principal before he paid the debt. (Ayni)] meaning, it is lawful for the surety to keep the profit earned on the money he received from the principal before he himself pays the creditor, because he owns the money upon receipt, so the profit is in exchange for his ownership; he does not give it in charity regardless of whether the debt is paid by him or the principal. However, there is a type of taint (khubth) in the profit earned by the surety if the principal paid the debt according to Abu Hanifa, but it does not appear with ownership in fungible items like dirhams. If the surety paid the debt, there is no taint by consensus. This applies if he received the money for the purpose of paying the debt (qada). If he received it as an agency (risala), the profit is not lawful for him according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf's other view. The original dispute is about profit on usurped dirhams. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 105/2] (Fath) And it is recommended (nadb) to return it, i.e., returning the profit to the principal if the received money is specific (like wheat or barley), and this is when the principal paid the debt, because the taint attaches to the principal; if he returns it, the principal receives his right. This is the saying of Abu Hanifa. According to another narration from him, he gives it in charity. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: the profit is lawful for him, and there is a narration from him (Abu Hanifa) as well, because he owns it upon receipt and earned profit on his property, so it remains his. And the taint is established with ownership in specific items, so according to one narration he gives it in charity to the poor, and in another narration he returns it to the principal. Then if the principal is poor, it is lawful for him; if rich, there are two narrations, and the more apparent is that it is lawful for him. This is when he gave the money for the purpose of paying his debt. If he gave it as an agency, the profit is not lawful by agreement, because he does not own it, whether the money is specific or not. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 106/2] (Fath) 'If something specific': meaning if what he took is specific, like wheat and barley, and this is when the principal paid the debt. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on profit (ribh) earned by the surety (kafil) on money he received from the principal (matalub) before paying the creditor (talib). The general rule: the profit is permissible (halal) for the surety because he becomes the owner of the money upon receipt, and profit on one's own property is lawful. However, if the principal later pays the debt himself, Abu Hanifa holds that there is a certain taint (khubth) in the profit for the surety, though this taint is not apparent in fungible currencies (like dirhams). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider the profit absolutely lawful. The text further distinguishes how the surety received the money: if it was as payment for the debt (qada), the rule applies as above; if it was as an agency (risala) – i.e., the principal told him 'take this and pay the creditor' – then the surety does not own the money (it is a trust), so any profit is not lawful by consensus. There is an additional recommendation (nadb) that if the principal paid the debt and the money given was specific property (like wheat), the surety should return the profit to the principal (per Abu Hanifa) because the taint is more manifest. There are variant narrations from Abu Hanifa: charity or return. The Sahibayn hold it is lawful regardless, though the taint exists. The final fatwa leans toward the profit being halal, especially if the principal is poor.

Key Terms
الربح (al-ribh): Profit earned by the surety on money received from the principal, e.g., by trading with it before paying the debt.
الخبث (al-khubth): Spiritual taint or moral impurity in the profit, requiring purification through charity or return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what is the ruling on profit earned by the surety on money received as payment (qada)?
A) It must be returned to the principal
B) It is halal and may be kept
C) It must be given in charity
D) It is haram

Key Takeaway
The surety's profit on money received for debt payment is halal; internal dispute only affects recommendation.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate
6 min
Principal Cannot Recover Money Given to Surety Before Debt Payment
Ruling on recovery of money given to surety before debt settlement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a principal debtor's attempt to recover money given to a surety before the surety pays the creditor, and identify the conditions that permit recovery.

Core Idea
Money given to a surety is tied to the creditor's right until the debt is paid or the creditor defers.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from the chapter on kafalah (suretyship). The principal debtor, having voluntarily given money to the surety before the surety pays the creditor, later tries to reclaim it. The Hanafi school, reasoning by analogy to early zakat payment, rules that the principal cannot recover it as long as there is a possibility the surety will pay. However, if the creditor defers the debt, the principal may reclaim. This ruling protects the creditor's interest while allowing flexibility when the creditor voluntarily relinquishes his claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the opening line of the matn: 'If the principal debtor gives the surety (the money) before the surety gives the creditor, he cannot recover it from him.' This is the general rule. Note that the ruling applies whether the money is fungible (dirhams) or specific (wheat).
2
Trace the Legal Reasoning ('illa)
The commentary explains the 'illa: 'because the right of the recipient (creditor) has attached to it, due to the possibility that he (the surety) will pay the debt.' As long as that possibility exists, the principal cannot reclaim. This is compared to early payment of zakat to the collector.
The qiyas is to early zakat: once paid, the payer cannot revoke.
3
Identify the Condition (Exception)
The commentary adds: 'And the non-recovery is conditional upon the creditor not having deferred the debt from the principal or the surety. If the creditor defers the debt, he (the principal) can reclaim it.' This shows that the ruling is not absolute.
Deferment (ta'khir) by the creditor removes the creditor's imminent right.
4
Distinguish the Agency Case (Risalah)
The passage also covers when the principal gives the money as an agent (risalah), instructing the surety to pay the creditor. In that case, the surety holds it as a trust (amanah), not as owner. Even so, the principal cannot recover it because the creditor's right has attached. This reinforces the principle that the creditor's right prevails.
5
Summarize the Legal Outcome
The principal cannot recover money given to the surety before payment unless the creditor defers the debt. The money is considered encumbered by the creditor's potential claim. The analogy to early zakat clarifies that once a right attaches to a payment, revocation is prohibited.
This ruling safeguards the creditor's interest and ensures the surety's role is not undermined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أعطى المطلوب الكفيل قبل أن يعطي الكفيل الطالب لا يسترد منه. فصل: في بيان المسائل المتفرقة من جنس هذا الباب. (عيني) لا يسترد منه [ أي المكفول عنه من الكفيل فإن أداه بنفسه قبل أداء الكفيل يسترد منه ما أخذ. (ط) يعني إذا أعطى المكفول عنه من الكفيل ما ضمن مما لا يتعين كالدراهم أو مما يتعين كالحنطة قبل أن يقضي الكفيل دينه لا يسترد المكفول عنه من الكفيل ما أعطاه؛ لأنه تعلق به حق القابض على احتمال قضائه الدين، فلا يسترد ما دام هذا الاحتمال باقياً كمن عجل الزكاة ودفعها إلى الساعي، وعدم الاسترداد مقيد بما إذا لم يؤخره الطالب عن الأصيل أو الكفيل، فإن أخر الطالب الدين له أن يسترده، وإذا كان إعطاء الأصيل على وجه الرسالة بأن قال للكفيل: خذ هذا المال وادفعه إلى الطالب لا يصير المؤدى ملكاً للكفيل، بل أمانة في يده ولكن لا يكون للأصيل أن يسترده من الكفيل؛ لأنه تعلق بالمؤدى حق الطالب، والمطلوب بالاسترداد يريد إبطاله، فلا يمكن منه ما لم يقض دينه. (عيني، فتح)
If the principal debtor gives the surety (the money) before the surety gives the creditor, he cannot recover it from him. Chapter: On explaining miscellaneous rulings of this kind. (Ayni) He cannot recover it from him, meaning the principal debtor from the surety. For if he (the surety) pays it himself before the surety pays, he recovers what he took. (T) means if the principal debtor gives the surety what he guaranteed, whether non-specific like dirhams or specific like wheat, before the surety pays his debt, the principal debtor cannot recover from the surety what he gave; because the right of the recipient (creditor) has attached to it, due to the possibility that he (the surety) will pay the debt. So he cannot reclaim as long as this possibility remains, like one who pays zakat early and gives it to the collector. And the non-recovery is conditional upon the creditor not having deferred the debt from the principal or the surety. If the creditor defers the debt, he (the principal) can reclaim it. And if the principal's giving is in the capacity of agency, such that he says to the surety: 'take this money and pay it to the creditor', then the money given does not become the property of the surety, but rather a trust in his hand. However, the principal cannot recover it from the surety because the right of the creditor has attached to the money given, and the one seeking recovery wants to nullify it, so he cannot do so until he pays the debt. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the situation where a principal debtor (المكفول عنه) voluntarily gives money to the surety (الكفيل) before the surety has paid the creditor. The opening line of the matn states the general rule: the principal cannot recover that money. The commentary then clarifies that this applies whether the money is fungible (e.g., dirhams) or specific (e.g., wheat). The legal reasoning ('illa) is that once the principal gives the money to the surety, a right of the creditor attaches to it because of the possibility that the surety will use it to pay the debt. As long as that possibility exists, the principal cannot reclaim it. This is analogous to giving zakat early to the collector: once paid, the payer cannot take it back even if the collector has not yet distributed it. However, the commentary adds an important condition: if the creditor grants a deferment (تأخير) of the debt to either the principal or the surety, the principal may reclaim the money because the creditor's right is no longer imminent. The passage also distinguishes the case where the principal gives the money as an agent (رسالة) instructing the surety to pay it to the creditor. In that case, the surety holds the money as a trust (أمانة), not as owner. Still, the principal cannot recover it because the creditor's right has already attached to the money. This nuanced ruling shows the Hanafi concern for protecting the creditor's interest while recognizing the principal's right when the creditor voluntarily relinquishes his claim.

Key Terms
الكفيل (al-kafīl): The surety or guarantor who undertakes to pay the debt if the principal debtor fails.
المكفول عنه (al-makfūl ʿanhu): The principal debtor on whose behalf the surety guarantees the debt.
الطالب (al-ṭālib): The creditor who is owed the debt.
الرسالة (al-risālah): Agency or trust arrangement where the principal gives money to the surety with instructions to deliver it to the creditor, so the surety does not own the money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition can the principal debtor recover the money he gave to the surety before the surety paid the creditor?
A) If the surety has not yet paid the creditor.
B) If the debtor gives the money as an agency (رسالة).
C) If the creditor grants a deferment (تأخير) of the debt.
D) The principal can never recover the money.

Key Takeaway
The principal debtor cannot recover money given to the surety before the surety pays the creditor, unless the creditor defers the debt.

Profit from Surety's Possession of Principal's Money
Ruling on profit earned by surety on money before paying creditor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a surety's profit from the principal's money and apply the conditions that determine when the profit is tainted or recommended to be returned.

Core Idea
Surety owns the money upon receipt; profit is his, but taint (khubth) arises if principal pays the debt.

This lesson delves into a nuanced case in suretyship (kafalah): when a surety receives money from the principal debtor before settling the debt with the creditor, and then uses that money to generate profit. The key question is who owns that profit. The Hanafi school holds that the surety owns the money upon possession, so the profit belongs to him, but ethical considerations may require him to return it or give charity, depending on who ultimately pays the debt and the nature of the property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage states: the profit earned by the surety from money received from the principal before paying the creditor is permissible (halal) for the surety. Because he owns the money by taking possession, the profit is his substitute for ownership.
The matn is clear: 'فهو له' (it belongs to him).
2
Distinguish based on who pays the debt
If the surety pays the debt, there is no taint (khubth) at all (by consensus). If the principal pays, Abu Hanifa says there is khubth in the profit, so it should be returned or given in charity. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) say the profit remains completely permissible.
The taint arises because the principal's payment effectively frees the surety's obligation, yet the surety keeps profit from the principal's money.
3
Consider the nature of the property
For non-specific items (like dirhams), the taint does not become apparent with ownership. For specific items (like wheat or barley), the taint is attached to ownership, so Abu Hanifa recommends returning the profit to the principal or giving charity.
If the principal is poor, some narrations say it is permissible for the surety to keep it.
4
Differentiate the mode of receipt
If the surety received the money as an agent (رسالة) to simply pay the creditor, he does not own it, so any profit is not permissible by agreement. If he received it as debt collection (اقتضاء الدين), he owns it.
This distinction affects the fundament: ownership vs. agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ربح الكفيل: [من المال الذي قبضه من المطلوب قبل أن يعطي الدين. (عيني)] يعني طاب للكفيل ما ربح في المال الذي قبضه من المطلوب قبل أن يعطي هو للطالب؛ لأنه ملكه بالقبض فكان الربح بدل ملكه ولا يتصدق به سواء قضى الدين هو أو الأصيل إلا أنه في الربح الحاصل للكفيل نوع خبث إذا قضى الأصيل الدين عند أبي حنيفة؛ لكنه لا يظهر مع الملك فيما لا يتعين كالدراهم، وإن قضى الكفيل فلا خبث فيه بالإجماع، هذا إذا قبضه على وجه اقتضاء الدين، وإن قبضه على وجه الرسالة لا يطيب له الربح على قولهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف، وأصل الخلاف في الربح بالدراهم المغصوبة. [ رمز الحقائق: ١٠٥/٢] (فتح) وندب ردّه أي استحب رد الربح على الأصيل لو كان المقبوض مما يتعين، وهذا إذا قضى الأصيل الدين؛ لأن الخبث لحق الأصيل فإذا رده إليه وصل إليه حقه وهو قول أبي حنيفة وعنه أنه يتصدق به، وقالا: يطيب له الربح، وهو رواية عنه؛ لأنه ملكه بالقبض وربح في ملكه فيسلم له وله أن الخبث تمكن مع الملك فيما يتعين، فيتصدق به في رواية على الفقراء، وفي رواية: يرده على الأصيل؛ ثم إن كان الأصيل فقيراً يطيب له، وإن كان غنياً ففيه روايتان والأشبه أن يطيب له، هذا إذا أعطاه على وجه القضاء لدينه، وإن أعطاه على وجه الرسالة، لا يطيب له الربح بالاتفاق؛ لأنه لا يملكه، سواء كان المدفوع مما يتعين أولا [ رمز الحقائق: ١٠٦/٢] (فتح) لو شيئاً: أي لو كان الذي آخذ منه. (عيني) يتعين كالحنطة والشعير، وهذا إذا قضى الأصيل الدين. (عيني)
And what profit the surety earns: [from the money he received from the principal debtor before giving the debt. (Ayni)] Meaning: the profit in the money that the surety received from the principal debtor before he himself gives to the creditor is permissible (good) for the surety; because he owns it by taking possession, so the profit is a substitute for his ownership, and he does not have to give charity with it, regardless of whether he pays the debt or the principal pays. However, in the profit acquired by the surety, there is a type of impurity (khubth) if the principal pays the debt, according to Abu Hanifa; but this does not become apparent with ownership in non-specific items like dirhams. If the surety pays the debt, there is no impurity by consensus. This is if he received the money on the basis of debt collection; if he received it as an agency, the profit is not permissible for him according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf (a variant). The origin of the disagreement is in the profit from usurped dirhams. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/105] (Fath) And it is recommended to return it, i.e., it is recommended to return the profit to the principal debtor if the received property was specific (like wheat or barley), and this is when the principal pays the debt; because the impurity pertains to the principal. If he returns it to him, his right reaches him. This is the saying of Abu Hanifa. From him (another narration) that he gives it in charity. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: the profit is permissible for him, and it is a narration from him; because he owns it by taking possession and made a profit in his ownership, so it becomes his. And he (Abu Hanifa) said that the impurity is attached to ownership in specific items, so he gives it in charity in one narration to the poor, and in another narration he returns it to the principal. Then if the principal is poor, it is permissible for him (to keep it? unclear), and if he is rich, there are two narrations, and the more appropriate is that it is permissible for him. This is if he gave him on the basis of debt settlement; if he gave him as an agency, the profit is not permissible for him by agreement, because he does not own it, whether the money paid is specific or not. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/106] (Fath) 'If something specific': meaning if what he took from him is specific like wheat and barley, and this is when the principal pays the debt. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage addresses a complex issue: when a surety receives money from the principal debtor before settling the debt, and the surety invests or uses that money to generate profit, who owns that profit? The matn states that the profit belongs to the surety ('فهو له'), and it is recommended (مندوب) to return it to the principal if the money given was a specific good (معين). The commentary delves into the reasoning. The surety takes possession (قبض) of the money, so he becomes its owner and any profit is his. However, if the principal later pays the debt himself, the profit is considered 'tainted' (خبث) according to Abu Hanifa because the principal's money was used to pay the debt, while the surety kept the profit. This taint does not affect fungible items (like dirhams) because they are not distinct; it does affect specific items (like wheat). Therefore, Abu Hanifa says the profit should either be returned to the principal or given in charity. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani disagree, holding that the profit is absolutely permissible for the surety because he owned the money. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) seems to be that if the principal pays, the profit is tainted and should be returned or given in charity when the property is specific. If the surety pays, there is no taint by consensus. Another distinction: if the principal gave the money as an agency (رسالة) for the surety to pay the creditor directly, then the surety does not own the money, so the profit is not permissible at all. This disagreement mirrors the broader issue of profit from usurped goods (ربح الدراهم المغصوبة). The Hanafi school thus balances ownership rights with ethical considerations.

Key Terms
الخبث (al-khubth): Legal impurity or taint that attaches to a transaction, making the profit disliked (makruh) or requiring purification through charity.
اقتضاء الدين (iqtidāʾ al-dayn): Receiving money on the basis of collecting a debt; the surety takes the money as his own to settle the debt.
الرسالة (al-risālah): Agency; the principal gives money to the surety with instructions to pay the creditor, so the surety is merely a messenger and does not own the money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, what should the surety do with the profit if the principal pays the debt and the received property was a specific item (e.g., wheat)?
A) Keep the profit absolutely because he owned the money.
B) Return the profit to the principal or give it in charity.
C) Give the profit to the creditor.
D) Destroy the profit.

Key Takeaway
The surety's profit from the principal's money is permissible, but when the principal pays the debt and the property is specific, it is recommended to return or give the profit in charity to remove taint.

Ruling on 'Aynah Transaction Between Principal and Surety
Ruling with proof on an 'aynah transaction between principal and surety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a principal orders a surety to engage in an 'aynah transaction and explain why the purchase belongs to the surety.

Core Idea
The surety, not the principal, owns the purchase because both the guarantee of loss and agency are invalid.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in suretyship (kafalah) where the principal debtor instructs the surety to buy silk using an 'aynah (legal trick) sale. The key legal question: who owns the purchased item and bears the profit or loss? The Hanafi school's answer, grounded in the principles of guarantee and agency, is that the surety becomes the owner because the principal's instructions cannot create a valid guarantee or agency under these circumstances. We will walk through the reasoning and the intra-school disagreement on the permissibility of 'aynah itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and parties
Recognize the scenario: principal (debtor) orders surety to buy silk through an 'aynah sale. The surety complies. Determine the legal relationship created by the principal's instruction.
The 'aynah sale involves two sales: one credit, one cash, to provide a loan with disguised interest.
2
Analyze the two possible legal relationships
The principal's instruction could be interpreted as either a guarantee (ضمان) by the principal of any loss, or an agency (وكالة) for the surety to purchase on his behalf. Examine each.
Hanafi law requires a guarantee to be for a known, established debt, not a potential loss. An agency requires certainty in the subject matter and price.
3
Examine why both are invalid
The guarantee of loss (ضمان بالخسران) is invalid because it lacks a specific, existing obligation. The agency is invalid due to jahalah (uncertainty) regarding the silk and its price.
Jahalah makes a contract fasid (invalid), not batil (void).
4
Conclude the purchase is for the surety
Since neither the guarantee nor agency is valid, the transaction is treated as if the surety acted on his own behalf. Therefore, the purchased silk belongs to the surety, and any profit or loss is his.
This holds regardless of the principal's promise to cover losses.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on 'aynah
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider 'aynah makruh tahrimi. Abu Yusuf holds it is not disliked, citing practice of Companions. The purchase ruling is unaffected.
The dispute is about the moral/legal status of 'aynah, not the ownership of the purchased item.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أمر كفيله أن يتعين عليه حريراً، ففعل فالشراءُ للكفيل، والربح عليه، ومن أي الأصيل على الأصيل كفل عن رجُلٍ بما ذاب له عليه أو بما قضي له عليه، فغاب المطلوب، فبرهن المدعي على الرجل كفل عن رجل أي لفلان أي على الرجل وهو المكفول . على الكفيل أن يتعين عليه أن له على المطلوب ألفا، لم تقبل للمدعي الغائب أي ألف درهم عنه أقام المدعي البينة وهو حريراً: أي يشتري ببيع العينة، وهي بكسر العين السلف وأعين الرجل اشترى شيئاً نسيئة و أن يقول له: اشترلي من الناس حريراً أو غيره من الأنواع، ثم بعه، فما ربح البائع منك، وخسرت به أنت فعلي، وصورته: أن يأتي هو إلى تاجر فيطلب منه القرض، ويطلب منه التاجر الربح ولكن يخاف من الربا، فيبيعه التاجر ثوباً يساوي عشرة مثلاً بخمسة عشر نسيئة ثم يبيعه هو في السوق بعشرة، فيحصل له عشرة، ويجب عليه للبائع خمسة عشر إلى أجل، أو يقرضه خمسة عشر درهماً، ثم يبيع المقرض منه ثوباً يساوي عشرة بخمسة عشر، فيأخذ الدراهم التي أقرضه على أنها ثمن الثوب، فتبقى عليه خمسة عشر قرضاً. ففعل: أي الكفيل حسب ما أمره الأصيل فالشراء حينئذ يكون للكفيل؛ لأنه العاقد والربح الذي ربحه التاجر يلزم على الكفيل، ولا يلزم على الأمر شيء من ذلك؛ لأن الكفيل إما أن يكون ضامنا لما يخسر نظراً إلى قوله: "علي"، والضمان بالخسران لا يجوز؛ لأن الضمان لا يكون إلا بمضمون، وإما أن يكون وكيلا بالشراء نظراً إلى الأمر به فلا يجوز أيضاً لجهالة الحرير وثمنه وإذا فسدت الكفالة والوكالة كان المشترى للعاقد وهو الكفيل، والربح أي الزيادة على الدين يكون عليه وهذا النوع من البيع يسمى عينة؛ لما فيه من السلف، وقيل: 1 : لأنها العين بالربح، وقيل: هي شراء ما باع بأقل مما باع. هذا وقيل: يسمى البيع عينة لما فيها من الإعراض عن الدين إلى العين، وهو مكروه، حتى قيل: إياك والعينة؛ فإنها لعينة؛ لما فيه من الإعراض عن مبرة الإقراض مطاوعة لشح النفس، وهذا النوع مذموم شرعاً، اخترعه آكلة الربا، وقال عليه: "إذا تبايعتم بالعين، واتبعتم أذناب البقر ذللتم، وظهر عليكم عدوكم والمراد باتباع أذناب البقر الزراعة، وقال أبو يوسف لا يكره؛ لأنه فعله كثير من ا الصحابة وحمدوا عليه. (عيني، فتح) والربح أي الربح الذي ربحه التاجر على الكفيل ولا يلزم الأمر شيء؛ لأن العاقد هو الكفيل. (ط، فتح) بما ذاب : أي بالذي ظهر ووجب لفلان. (عيني) لم تقبل أي من كفل عن رجل بما وجب للمكفول له على المكفول عنه أو قضي له عليه فغاب المكفول عنه، وأقام المدعي البينة على الكفيل أن لي على المكفول عنه ألف درهم، لم تقبل بينته على الكفيل حتى يحضر المكفول عنه، فيقضى عليه والمراد بـــذاب وقضي" الاستقبال، وإن كانا ماضيين، ووجه عدم القبول : أنه كفل مالاً يجب في المستقبل بالقضاء، أو بأي سبب كان، وذلك لم يوجد، فالقضاء على الغائب لا يجوز؛ إذ لم ينتصب عنه خصم، والكفيل لا يصلح خصماً هنا؛ لأنه إنما تكفل بمال يقضى به بعد الكفالة، فلم يوجب هذه البينة شيئاً على الكفيل، ولو قال الطالب إني قدمت المطلوب بعد الكفالة =
And if he (the principal) orders his surety to engage in an 'aynah transaction for silk, and he does so, then the purchase is for the surety, and the profit is upon him, and from (the principal) upon the principal. (Then the text continues with the next ruling: 'If someone guarantees a man for what has become established for him or what has been adjudged for him, and the debtor is absent, then the plaintiff proves...' but the 'aynah ruling is the focus here.) (Commentary:) 'That he engage in an 'aynah transaction for silk' meaning he buys through an 'aynah sale. 'Aynah (with kasra on the 'ayn) is a loan, and 'a'ana al-rajul means he bought something on credit. And (the principal) says to him: 'Buy for me from people silk or other items, then sell it, and whatever profit the seller makes from you, and you incur a loss, that is on me.' Its form: He (the principal) goes to a merchant and requests a loan, and the merchant wants profit but fears usury, so he sells him a cloth worth ten (for example) for fifteen on credit, then he (the principal) sells it in the market for ten, thus he gets ten and owes the seller fifteen deferred. Or the merchant lends him fifteen dirhams, then the lender sells him a cloth worth ten for fifteen, taking the dirhams he lent as the price of the cloth, so fifteen remains as a loan. 'So he does': i.e., the surety does as the principal ordered. Then the purchase is for the surety, because he is the contracting party, and the profit that the merchant earns is binding upon the surety, and nothing is binding upon the orderer (principal) of that; because the surety either is a guarantor for the loss by virtue of his saying 'upon me', and guarantee of loss is not valid because guarantee is only for a guaranteed thing (like a debt), or he is an agent for the purchase by virtue of the order, which is also invalid due to ignorance of the silk and its price. When the surety and agency become invalid, the purchased item is for the contracting party, i.e., the surety, and the profit, i.e., the increase over the debt, is upon him. This type of sale is called 'aynah because it involves a loan (salaf). It is said: because it is the transaction of 'ayn (cash) with profit. It is said: it is buying what one sold for less than what one sold. It is also said: it is called 'aynah because of turning away from debt to cash, and it is disliked (makruh). It is even said: Beware of 'aynah, for it is cursed (la'inah), because it involves turning away from the virtue of lending, yielding to the miserliness of the soul. This type is blameworthy in Shari'ah, invented by the eaters of riba. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'When you engage in 'aynah transactions, follow the tails of cows, you will be humiliated and your enemy will prevail over you.' The meaning of following the tails of cows is agriculture. Abu Yusuf said: it is not disliked, because many Companions did it and were praised for it. (Ayni, Fath) And the profit: i.e., the profit that the merchant earned is upon the surety, and nothing binds the principal, because the contracting party is the surety. (T, Fath)
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific transaction known as 'aynah, which is a legal device to circumvent riba while appearing as a sale. The scenario: the principal debtor instructs his surety to purchase silk on his behalf through an 'aynah arrangement. The surety complies. The ruling: the purchase is for the surety (not the principal), and the profit or loss from that transaction falls on the surety. The commentary explains why: the principal's instruction could be interpreted either as a guarantee (ضمان) of losses or as an agency (وكالة) to purchase. Both are invalid in this context. The guarantee is invalid because guaranteeing a loss (as opposed to a fixed debt) is not permitted in Hanafi law; a guarantee must be for a well-defined obligation. The agency is invalid due to uncertainty (جهالة) in the subject matter (silk) and its price. Therefore, the transaction is treated as if the surety acted on his own behalf, so he owns the purchased item and bears the profit/ loss. The passage then provides a lengthy description of the 'aynah sale, distinguishing its forms and noting its strong disapproval (كراهة). It quotes a hadith warning against 'aynah and following the tails of cows (i.e., preoccupation with agriculture at the expense of religious duties). However, Abu Yusuf holds that it is not disliked because many Companions practiced it. The Hanafi school generally discourages 'aynah as it is a legal trick to permit riba, but the ruling here is about the legal consequences: the surety becomes the owner. This shows the Hanafi approach of upholding formal legal validity while disapproving of the substance.

Key Terms
بيع العينة (bayʿ al-ʿīnah): A sale that involves a loan with interest disguised as two sales: one on credit at a higher price and one for cash at a lower price, enabling the borrower to get cash while the lender gets profit.
الضمان بالخسران (aḍ-ḍamān bi al-khusrān): Guarantee of a loss, which is invalid in Hanafi law because guarantee must be for a known, established debt, not a potential loss.
جهالة (jahālah): Legal uncertainty or ignorance regarding the subject matter or price, which renders a contract invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the purchase of silk in an 'aynah transaction become the responsibility of the surety and not the principal?
A) Because the surety is the one who physically bought the silk.
B) Because the principal's instruction is void due to uncertainty in the silk and price.
C) Because the surety explicitly agreed to bear the loss.
D) Because the principal revoked the instruction after the purchase.

Key Takeaway
When a principal orders a surety to engage in an 'aynah transaction, the purchase belongs to the surety because the agency is invalid due to uncertainty, and the guarantee of loss is not recognized.

Evidence Against Surety for Future Debt Not Accepted When Debtor Absent
Ruling on accepting evidence against surety for debt of absent principal debtor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting evidence against a surety when the principal debtor is absent and explain the underlying qiyas reasoning.

Core Idea
A surety cannot be sued for a future debt until the debt is established against the absent debtor.

In suretyship (kafalah), the surety's liability is contingent on the debt being established against the principal debtor. This lesson examines a procedural ruling: if the debtor is absent and the debt is yet to be adjudged, the creditor's evidence against the surety is not accepted. The reasoning is rooted in the principle that judgment cannot be passed on an absent person without a proper opponent, and the surety does not represent the debtor for this purpose.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Recognize that the case involves a surety who guaranteed a debt that is still future or not yet adjudged, and then the principal debtor becomes absent. The creditor attempts to prove the debt directly against the surety.
2
Understand the ruling
The Hanafi ruling is that the evidence against the surety is not accepted until the principal debtor is present and the debt is established against him. This is a procedural bar.
3
Grasp the reason (talil)
The rationale: the debt is future and not yet fixed, so the surety's liability is not yet triggered. Also, passing judgment on an absent person is invalid because no opponent has been appointed. The surety is not a suitable opponent because his guarantee is for a future obligation.
The text states 'al-qada' ala al-gha'ib la yajuz'.
4
Recognize the temporal aspect
The terms 'dhaba' (established) and 'qudiya' (adjudged) are in past tense but refer to future events. That is, the debt has not yet become due; it will be established in the future.
5
Apply the procedural principle
This ruling protects the surety from being held liable for a debt that may never be proven against the principal. Ensure that suretyship for future debts is valid, but enforcement requires prior establishment of the debt against the debtor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بما ذاب : أي بالذي ظهر ووجب لفلان. (عيني) لم تقبل أي من كفل عن رجل بما وجب للمكفول له على المكفول عنه أو قضي له عليه فغاب المكفول عنه، وأقام المدعي البينة على الكفيل أن لي على المكفول عنه ألف درهم، لم تقبل بينته على الكفيل حتى يحضر المكفول عنه، فيقضى عليه والمراد بـــذاب وقضي" الاستقبال، وإن كانا ماضيين، ووجه عدم القبول : أنه كفل مالاً يجب في المستقبل بالقضاء، أو بأي سبب كان، وذلك لم يوجد، فالقضاء على الغائب لا يجوز؛ إذ لم ينتصب عنه خصم، والكفيل لا يصلح خصماً هنا؛ لأنه إنما تكفل بمال يقضى به بعد الكفالة، فلم يوجب هذه البينة شيئاً على الكفيل، ولو قال الطالب إني قدمت المطلوب بعد الكفالة =
('Bima dhaba') meaning: with what has appeared and become obligatory for someone. (Ayni) 'It is not accepted': i.e., whoever guarantees a man for what has become obligatory for the guaranteed creditor upon the principal debtor, or what has been adjudged for him, and then the principal debtor is absent, and the plaintiff brings evidence against the surety that 'I have one thousand dirhams owed to me by the principal debtor', his evidence against the surety is not accepted until the principal debtor is present, so that a ruling can be passed against him. The intended meaning of 'dhaba' and 'qudiya' is the future, even though they are in the past tense. The reason for non-acceptance: he guaranteed wealth that is to become obligatory in the future through judgment or any other cause, and that has not yet occurred. So passing a judgment against an absent person is not permissible because no opponent has been appointed for him, and the surety is not suitable as an opponent here because he only guaranteed wealth that would be adjudged after the guarantee, so this evidence does not establish anything against the surety. And if the plaintiff says: 'I have presented the defendant after the guarantee...' (text cuts off)
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage clarifies a procedural point in suretyship: if someone guarantees a debt that will be established in the future (e.g., by a court judgment), and then the principal debtor becomes absent, the creditor cannot bring evidence of that debt directly against the surety. The evidence must be presented against the debtor himself. The reason is that the surety's liability is contingent on the debt being established against the debtor; the debt does not yet exist in a fixed form. Therefore, the surety cannot represent the absent debtor in court. The text uses the terms 'dhaba' (established) and 'qudiya' (adjudged) in the past tense, but they refer to future obligations. The ruling is that the court cannot accept evidence against the surety until the debtor is present and the debt is proved against him. This protects the surety from being held liable for a debt that may not be proven against the principal.

Key Terms
ذاب (dhāba): Literally 'melted' or 'became manifest'; in legal context, it means a debt that has become established or obligatory.
قضي له عليه (quḍiya lahu ʿalayhi): A debt that has been adjudged in favor of the creditor against the debtor.
الغائب (al-ghāʾib): The absent debtor who is not present in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the reason that evidence against the surety is not accepted in this scenario?
A) Because the surety did not sign the guarantee.
B) Because the debt is future and judgment cannot be passed on an absent person without a representative.
C) Because the plaintiff must first prove the debt against the surety directly.
D) Because the surety has immunity from lawsuits.

Key Takeaway
A surety for a future debt cannot be sued until the debt is established against the principal debtor and the debtor is present in court.

The Guarantor's Purchase by Order: Who Bears Profit and Loss?
Ruling on guarantor's purchase by principal's order in 'aynah sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who bears the profit or loss when a principal orders his guarantor to buy silk on credit in an 'aynah transaction, and explain why both the guarantee of loss and the agency are invalid.

Core Idea
The guarantor bears the profit because the guarantee of loss and the agency are both void, making him the sole contracting party.

When a principal tells his guarantor, 'Buy silk on credit and sell it; any profit or loss is on me,' the Hanafi school does not uphold this arrangement. The guarantor ends up owning the goods and bearing the outcome. This lesson walks through the legal reasoning: why the attempted guarantee of loss fails and why the agency fails, leading to the purchase resting with the actual contracting party.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and the parties
The principal (al-asil) orders the guarantor (al-kafil) to buy silk on credit and sell it, with the principal promising to cover any loss. The guarantor complies. Determine who is the actual contracting party.
The guarantor physically makes the purchase from the merchant.
2
Analyze the attempted guarantee of loss
The principal's statement 'loss is upon me' attempts to create a guarantee (kafalah) of an uncertain future loss. Under Hanafi law, a valid guarantee requires a determinate, enforceable debt. An unknown loss cannot be guaranteed.
This is called 'daman bil-khusran' (guarantee of loss) and is invalid.
3
Analyze the attempted agency
The principal's order could be interpreted as an agency (wakalah) to buy silk. However, agency requires the subject matter (silk and its price) to be known at the time of contract. Here, the silk and price are unspecified, making the agency invalid due to ignorance (jahalah).
Agency with majhul (unknown) subject matter is fasid (void).
4
Apply the Hanafi rule on contracting party
Since both the guarantee and the agency are invalid, the transaction stands as a direct purchase by the guarantor. The guarantor is the sole contracting party (al-aqid). Therefore, he owns the goods and is responsible for any profit or loss.
The maxim: 'The purchase belongs to the one who contracted it (shira' lil-aqid).'
5
State the final ruling
The purchase is for the guarantor, and any profit from the onward sale is his. The principal owes nothing. The hadith warning against 'aynah does not affect this specific ruling.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أمر كفيله أن يتعين عليه حريراً، ففعل فالشراءُ للكفيل، والربح عليه، ومن على الأصيل على الكفيل أن يتعين عليه حريراً: أي يشتري ببيع العينة، وهي بكسر العين السلف وأعين الرجل اشترى شيئاً نسيئة و أن يقول له: اشترلي من الناس حريراً أو غيره من الأنواع، ثم بعه، فما ربح البائع منك، وخسرت به أنت فعلي، وصورته: أن يأتي هو إلى تاجر فيطلب منه القرض، ويطلب منه التاجر الربح ولكن يخاف من الربا، فيبيعه التاجر ثوباً يساوي عشرة مثلاً بخمسة عشر نسيئة ثم يبيعه هو في السوق بعشرة، فيحصل له عشرة، ويجب عليه للبائع خمسة عشر إلى أجل، أو يقرضه خمسة عشر درهماً، ثم يبيع المقرض منه ثوباً يساوي عشرة بخمسة عشر، فيأخذ الدراهم التي أقرضه على أنها ثمن الثوب، فتبقى عليه خمسة عشر قرضاً. ففعل: أي الكفيل حسب ما أمره الأصيل فالشراء حينئذ يكون للكفيل؛ لأنه العاقد والربح الذي ربحه التاجر يلزم على الكفيل، ولا يلزم على الأمر شيء من ذلك؛ لأن الكفيل إما أن يكون ضامنا لما يخسر نظراً إلى قوله: "علي"، والضمان بالخسران لا يجوز؛ لأن الضمان لا يكون إلا بمضمون، وإما أن يكون وكيلا بالشراء نظراً إلى الأمر به فلا يجوز أيضاً لجهالة الحرير وثمنه وإذا فسدت الكفالة والوكالة كان المشترى للعاقد وهو الكفيل، والربح أي الزيادة على الدين يكون عليه وهذا النوع من البيع يسمى عينة؛ لما فيه من السلف، وقيل: لأنها العين بالربح، وقيل: هي شراء ما باع بأقل مما باع. هذا وقيل: يسمى البيع عينة لما فيها من الإعراض عن الدين إلى العين، وهو مكروه، حتى قيل: إياك والعينة؛ فإنها لعينة؛ لما فيه من الإعراض عن مبرة الإقراض مطاوعة لشح النفس، وهذا النوع مذموم شرعاً، اخترعه آكلة الربا، وقال عليه: "إذا تبايعتم بالعين، واتبعتم أذناب البقر ذللتم، وظهر عليكم عدوكم والمراد باتباع أذناب البقر الزراعة، وقال أبو يوسف لا يكره؛ لأنه فعله كثير من ا الصحابة وحمدوا عليه. (عيني، فتح) بيع والربح أي الربح الذي ربحه التاجر على الكفيل ولا يلزم الأمر شيء؛ لأن العاقد هو الكفيل. (ط، فتح)
If the guarantor ordered [the principal] to anticipate [buy on credit] silk for him, and he did so, the purchase is for the guarantor, and the profit is upon him, and from the principal. Upon the guarantor to anticipate silk for him: that is, to buy by way of 'aynah sale, which is, with kasra on 'ayn, a loan. A man anticipates: he buys something on credit and says to him: Buy for me from people silk or other types, then sell it, and whatever profit the seller makes from you, and loss you incur, it is upon me. Its form: he goes to a merchant and asks for a loan, and the merchant seeks profit but fears riba, so the merchant sells him a garment worth ten for example for fifteen on credit, then he sells it in the market for ten, so he obtains ten, and owes the seller fifteen to a term, or he lends him fifteen dirhams, then the lender sells him a garment worth ten for fifteen, and takes the dirhams he lent him as the price of the garment, so fifteen remain as a debt upon him. He did: that is, the guarantor as the principal ordered. So the purchase then is for the guarantor; because he is the contracting party, and the profit that the merchant earned is binding upon the guarantor, and nothing of that is binding upon the principal; because the guarantor is either a guarantor for loss in view of his saying 'upon me', and guarantee of loss is not permissible; because guarantee is only for a guaranteed [debt], or he is an agent for purchase in view of the order, so it is also not permissible due to ignorance of the silk and its price. When the guarantee and agency become void, the purchase is for the contracting party, who is the guarantor, and the profit, that is, the increase over the debt, is upon him. This type of sale is called 'aynah; because it contains a loan. It is said: because it is ready money for profit. It is said: it is buying what one sold for less than what he sold. This, and it is said: the sale is called 'aynah because it involves turning away from debt to ready money, and it is disliked, so it is said: Beware of 'aynah, for it is accursed; because it involves turning away from the virtue of lending, following the miserliness of the soul. This type is blameworthy in Shari'ah, invented by the consumers of riba. He said: 'When you deal in 'aynah and follow the tails of cows, you will be humiliated and your enemy will overcome you.' The meaning of following the tails of cows is agriculture. Abu Yusuf said: It is not disliked; because many of the Companions did it and were praised for it. (Ayni, Fath) Sale and the profit, i.e., the profit that the merchant earned, is on the guarantor, and nothing is binding upon the principal; because the contracting party is the guarantor. (T, Fath)
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the legal consequences when a guarantor is ordered by the principal to engage in an 'aynah transaction (selling goods on credit to obtain cash). The text first defines 'aynah as a sale that involves a loan, often used as a legal trick to circumvent riba. The scenario is that the principal asks the guarantor to buy silk on credit and then sell it, with the profit or loss to be borne by the principal. However, the Hanafi ruling is that the purchase is for the guarantor, not the principal, because the guarantor is the actual contracting party. The attempted guarantee of loss is invalid because guarantee (kafalah) can only cover a determinate debt, not an uncertain loss. Similarly, the agency is invalid due to ignorance of the exact silk and price. Thus, the guarantor bears the profit or loss. The next section discusses the ruling of 'aynah itself: it is generally disliked (makruh) for evading riba, with a hadith warning against it. However, Abu Yusuf reportedly did not dislike it, citing the practice of many Companions. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that it is makruh tanzihi. The profit from the transaction is due on the guarantor, not the principal.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafālah): Guarantee; a contract where one person becomes responsible for a debt or obligation of another.
الضمان بالخسران (al-ḍamān bil-khusrān): Guarantee of an uncertain future loss, considered invalid in Hanafi law.
الوكالة (al-wakālah): Agency; a contract where one person authorizes another to act on his behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The purchase is for the principal because he ordered it.
B) The purchase is for the guarantor because he is the contracting party.
C) The purchase is invalid (batil) due to the involvement of 'aynah.

Key Takeaway
The guarantor owns the goods and bears all profit or loss because both the guarantee of loss and the agency are void, leaving the guarantor as the sole owner.

'Aynah Sales: Definition, Hanafi Ruling, and Proof
Ruling on the legal status of 'aynah sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'aynah, state the Hanafi ruling that it is makruh tanzihi, cite the hadith proof, and explain Abu Yusuf's differing view.

Core Idea
'Aynah is a legal trick to get cash on credit with profit disguised as a sale; the Hanafi school deems it makruh based on a warning hadith, though Abu Yusuf permitted it.

An 'aynah sale (bai' al-'aynah) is a transaction where a person buys a commodity on credit and then sells it for cash to obtain liquidity, often used as a stratagem to avoid riba. The Hanafi school considers this practice disliked (makruh tanzihi) because it involves turning away from the virtue of lending and follows the path of riba-eaters. This lesson unpacks the definition, the hadith proof, and the intra-madhhab difference with Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Define 'aynah and understand its structure
'Aynah refers to a sale where the buyer buys a good on credit and immediately sells it for cash to get money, including an implied loan with profit. The text gives two common forms.
The term comes from 'ayn (ready money) because the seller turns away from credit to cash.
2
Identify the Hanafi ruling on 'aynah
The Hanafi school holds that 'aynah is makruh tanzihi (disliked) because it is a legal device to circumvent the prohibition of riba. The text uses strong language: 'it is blameworthy, invented by the consumers of riba.'
Makruh tanzihi means not haram but better avoided.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The text cites a hadith: 'When you deal in 'aynah and follow the tails of cows, you will be humiliated and your enemy will overcome you.' This warns against 'aynah and attaches a spiritual penalty.
The hadith is ahad and zanni al-dalalah, but sufficient for establishing dislike.
4
Note Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf reported that many Companions engaged in 'aynah and were praised, so he did not consider it disliked. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the dislike, following the majority.
5
Apply the mufta bihi ruling
In practice, a Hanafi mufti will advise that 'aynah is makruh tanzihi and to be avoided. It is not invalid (batil) but best to avoid.
Shafi'is consider it permissible; the disagreement is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

intermediate
8 min
Guarantee of a Future Debt – Non-Acceptance of Evidence
Ruling on non-acceptance of proof for guarantee of future debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school does not accept evidence against a guarantor when the guarantee was for a debt that was not yet due, and apply this ruling to similar cases.

Core Idea
A guarantee of a future debt creates no liability; evidence against the guarantor is rejected until the debtor is present.

In this lesson, we examine a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq on kafalah: if a person guarantees a debt that is described as 'what has become due' or 'what has been adjudged' in the future tense, and later the creditor attempts to prove the debt against the guarantor while the principal debtor is absent, the evidence is not accepted. The commentator explains the reasoning: the debt had not yet come into existence, and the guarantor cannot serve as the debtor's legal opponent. This ruling illustrates a key principle in Hanafi kafalah: the guaranteed obligation must be existing at the time of guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the creditor presenting evidence against the guarantor for a debt described as 'what became apparent' (bimā dhāba) or 'what was adjudged' (quḍiya lahu 'alayhi). The ruling: the evidence is not accepted until the debtor is present.
The ruling is from the commentary of 'Ayni on Kanz al-Daqa'iq.
2
Understand the key terms
The terms 'dhāba' and 'quḍiya' are in the past tense, but in this context they refer to a future debt. This interpretation is crucial because the guarantee was for a non-existent obligation.
Hanafi usul allows such interpretation based on contextual clues (qarā'in).
3
Trace the reasoning (ta'lil)
The reason is twofold: (1) the guarantee was for a future debt that never became a present obligation, so the guarantor has no liability; (2) the guarantor is not a suitable legal opponent (khasm) for the absent debtor, as he did not guarantee an existing debt.
This reasoning is based on the principle that kafalah requires an existing debt at the time of contract.
4
Note the practical consequence
The creditor cannot sue the guarantor directly. He must either bring the principal debtor to court or wait until the debt actually becomes due and then sue the debtor.
If the debtor is present and the debt is established, the guarantor may become liable if the guarantee was valid.
5
Contrast with a valid guarantee
If the debt had already existed at the time of guarantee, the guarantor would be liable, and evidence against him would be accepted even if the debtor is absent, because the guarantee is valid.
This contrast highlights the critical condition of debt existence in kafalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بما ذاب : أي بالذي ظهر ووجب لفلان. (عيني) لم تقبل أي من كفل عن رجل بما وجب للمكفول له على المكفول عنه أو قضي له عليه فغاب المكفول عنه، وأقام المدعي البينة على الكفيل أن لي على المكفول عنه ألف درهم، لم تقبل بينته على الكفيل حتى يحضر المكفول عنه، فيقضى عليه والمراد بـــذاب وقضي" الاستقبال، وإن كانا ماضيين، ووجه عدم القبول : أنه كفل مالاً يجب في المستقبل بالقضاء، أو بأي سبب كان، وذلك لم يوجد، فالقضاء على الغائب لا يجوز؛ إذ لم ينتصب عنه خصم، والكفيل لا يصلح خصماً هنا؛ لأنه إنما تكفل بمال يقضى به بعد الكفالة، فلم توجب هذه البينة شيئاً على الكفيل، ولو قال الطالب إني قدمت المطلوب بعد الكفالة
With 'what became apparent': i.e., what became manifest and was owed to so-and-so. (Ayni) 'It is not accepted': i.e., if someone stood surety for a man for what was owed to the creditor by the debtor, or for what was adjudged in his favor, and the debtor absented himself, and the plaintiff presents evidence against the guarantor that 'I have one thousand dirhams against the debtor', his evidence against the guarantor is not accepted until the debtor is present and judgment is passed against him. The intended meaning of 'became apparent' and 'adjudged' is future, even though they are in the past tense. The reason for non-acceptance: he guaranteed money that would become due in the future by judgment or any cause, and that [debt] has not yet occurred, so judgment against an absent person is not permissible, because no opponent has been appointed for him, and the guarantor is not a suitable opponent here; because he only guaranteed money that would be adjudged after the guarantee, so this evidence does not impose anything on the guarantor. If the claimant said, 'I brought the sought [debtor] after the guarantee'
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage explains a ruling where a person guarantees a debt that is described as 'what has become due' or 'what has been adjudged' in future tense. The key is that the guarantee was for a debt that was not yet existent at the time of guarantee. Later, the creditor attempts to prove the debt against the guarantor while the principal debtor is absent. The text explains that the evidence is not accepted because the guarantee was for a future debt that never materialized as a present obligation. The guarantor is not a suitable legal opponent for the absent debtor because the guarantee itself was conditional. The creditor must bring the debtor or wait until the debtor is present and adjudged liable.

Key Terms
بما ذاب (bimā dhāba): Literally 'with what became apparent'; used in kafalah to refer to a debt that already existed or will exist.
قضي له عليه (quḍiya lahu 'alayhi): What was adjudged in his favor against him; a debt established by court judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the creditor's evidence against the guarantor not accepted when the guarantee was for a debt to be adjudged in the future?
A) Because the guarantor is not a reliable person.
B) Because the debt had not yet come into existence at the time of guarantee.
C) Because the debtor is absent and the guarantor cannot be a proxy.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi kafalah, the guaranteed debt must be existing at the time of guarantee; otherwise, the guarantor is not liable and evidence against him is not accepted without the principal debtor.
